Heat Pump Integration and Techno-economic
Analysis for Full-scale Carbon Capture System at
Amager Resource Center
M.Sc. Thesis
Ebbe Hauge Jensen
DTU Mechanical Engineering
July 2021
==PAGE:2==
Supervisors: Professor Brian Elmegaard & Researcher Torben Schmidt Ommen
Co-supervisor: Christian Riber
DTU Mekanik
Department of Mechanical Engineering
Technical University of Denmark
Niels Koppels Allé

2800 Kongens Lyngby, Denmark
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
Anthropogenic CO2 emissions from the burning of fossil fuels have been identified primary
driver for global warming. Bioenergy Carbon Capture and Storage (BECCS) is a promising
technology to mitigate the consequences of global warming, due to its ability to offset emissions
from other hard-to-abate sectors by delivering negative CO2 emissions. Waste-to-Energy (WtE)
facilities present an interesting option for BECCS due to the large fraction of biogenic material
in Municipal Solid Waste (MSW). This report investigates the integration of an amine-based
CO2 capture system at the Amager Resource Center (ARC) WtE facility in Copenhagen,
Denmark.
Seven different CO2 capture scenarios with increasing degrees of heat recovery from the CO2
capture facility are formulated. Different strategies including external waste heat recovery to
district heating return water and internal heat recovery with mechanical vapor recompression
(MVR) systems are investigated and compared.
Detailed design work for the investigated
scenarios is conducted in Engineering Equation Solver (EES) while a Thermoflex model is used
to determine the overall operating parameters for the ARC WtE facility with integrated CO2
capture. A techno-economic analysis is applied to determine the Levelized Cost of Carbon
Capture (LCCC) to compare the different scenarios.
Results show that significant reductions in the LCCC can be achieved by implementing waste
heat recovery strategies, with scenarios with full heat recovery yielding the lowest LCCC. The
investigated MVR system results in a lower LCCC for scenarios that utilize high-quality steam,
but is not competitive against scenarios using lower quality turbine extraction steam for CO2
desorption.
The most optimal scenario yields a LCCC 63 % lower than scenarios with no
waste heat recovery from the CO2 capture facility. Overall it is found that integration of CO2
capture at ARC is capable of delivering significant emissions reductions with a large fraction of
negative emissions in all scenarios. If the maximum potential for CO2 capture and subsequent
sequestration from the ARC WtE facility is achieved, ARC can contribute to delivering 3.3
% of the CO2 emissions reduction required from 2019 to 2030 to reach the target set by the
Danish climate legislation.
==PAGE:4==
Preface
This thesis was submitted as partial fulfillment of the requirements for the M.Sc. degree in
Mechanical Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). The work conducted
through the thesis period corresponds to 30 ECTS points.
The thesis was completed in collaboration with the Section of Thermal Energy, Department of
Mechanical Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark and the Rambøll Energy-from-
Waste department. The work was carried out in a five-month period which was initiated 1st
February 2021 and concluded 22nd July, 2021. The work was conducted under the supervision
of Professor Brian Elmegaard (DTU) and co-supervision of Researcher Torben Scmidt Ommen
(DTU) and Christian Riber (Rambøll).
Kgs. Lyngby, 22nd July 2021
Ebbe Hauge Jensen - s154101

Acknowledgements
I would like to thank my supervisors from the Technical University of Denmark, Professor Brian
Elmegaard and Researcher Torben Schmidt Ommen, for their support, advice and, discussions
along the way that have helped shape the outcome of this thesis project. Additionally, I would
like to thank Business Development Manager Christian Riber from Rambøll Energy-from-Waste
for his valuable input and encouragement throughout the project period.
I would like to thank Søren Jensen from the Union Engineering branch of Pentair PLC for
supplying the chemical process models upon which my work has been conducted and for being
an excellent sparring partner on all things related to amine CO2 capture systems.
I would like to thank Jørgen Bøgild and Kirstine Hansen from I/S ARC for being very helpful
in providing information regarding the operating conditions and economic figures applicable
for the ARC WtE facility.
To my colleagues at Rambøll Energy-from-Waste, I would like to thank you for a pleasant
and inspiring working environment as I conducted the grunt of the thesis work at the Rambøll
Head Oﬀice during the COVID-19 lockdown from February to June. A special thanks to Andrea
Meroni, Claus Hindsgaul and, Lasse Sander Tobiasen for being excellent sparring partners on
all things thermodynamics.
I would like to thank my study buddies, Niels Langballe Førby, Magnus Lyck Hansen and,
Rasmus Jelsbak Knudsen for the many succesfull and interesting projects together and for
co-inventing the “Humorhjørnet” section through the last six years of studying at DTU.
Finally, I would like to thank my family and friends for supporting me throughout the project
period. Last but definitely not least, I would like to thank Sonja Becker-Hardt for her enduring
support and seemingly endless patience and understanding especially during the last sprint of
my report writing.

==PAGE:19==
Chapter 1
Introduction
This work is conducted in parallel to a project titled “Net Zero Carbon Capture at ARC: Pilot
scale development and demonstration of carbon capture from waste-to-energy plants” which is
funded by EUDP (Energy Technology Development and Demonstration). While the EUDP
project considers a pilot scale carbon capture system, the investigations carried out in this work
focuses solely on technologies relevant for a full-scale carbon capture system at the Amager
Resource Center (ARC) WtE facility in Copenhagen, Denmark. The motivation for studying a
full-scale carbon capture system is to determine the key energy recovery technologies that are
relevant for demonstration in the pilot-scale carbon capture system. The EUDP project aims
to demonstrate technologies that allow for the integration of a full-scale carbon capture system
with a net-zero energy consumption. The expectation of the EUDP project is that reducing
the energy consumption will in turn reduce the cost of carbon capture. A full-scale carbon
capture system at the Amager Bakke waste-to-energy facility is planned for operation in 2025
and the energy integration technologies demonstrated in the pilot-scale technology will be key
in informing what technologies will need to be deployed in the full-scale facility.
Hypothesis
Based on the target of implementing a full-scale CO2 capture system at the ARC WtE facility,
this project aims to evaluate different waste heat recovery strategies and to demonstrate that
operating the CO2 capture facility at a net-zero energy consumption does indeed translate to
a lower cost of carbon capture. To this end an overall hypothesis was formulated:
Integration of a high degree of heat recovery technologies, eg. heat pumps, to recover
waste heat from CO2 capture systems will decrease the overall economic costs of
operating a CO2 capture facility and ultimately yield a lower cost of CO2 capture
compared to a facility with only limited or no heat recovery.
It is the overall goal of this project to either prove or disprove the formulated hypothesis. The
techno-economic investigation conducted in this project is not the center of the study but is
applied principally to quantify the relative differences between the investigated scenarios with
regards to the costs of CO2 capture.
==PAGE:20==
1.1 Structure of the Study
2
1.1
Structure of the Study
The motivation for studying the implementation of a CO2 capture system at the ARC WtE
facility as well as the state of the art for heat recovery technologies for CO2 capture systems
may be found in chapter 2. A detailed desrciption of the applied methodologies in subsystems
design and hollistic modelling of the complete ARC WtE facility with and without CO2 capture
are presented in chapter 3. Additionally, the applied methodologies for the techno-economic
evaluations are also shown in chapter 3. The results for the the subsystems investigations and
the CO2 capture integrations are detailed in chapter 4 where also the results of the techno-
economic investigations are presented. A discussion of the modelling results may be found
in chapter 5 where also recommendations for future works related to the project are listed.
Finally, a summary and conclusion is detailed in chapter 6.
While ARC is technically the owner and operator of the Amager Bakke WtE plant in Copen-
hagen, the names ARC and Amager Bakke will be used interchangeably through this report
when referencing the WtE facility. Additionally, the terms stripper, desorber and regenerator
are used interchangeably through this report but all refer to the the part of the CO2 capture
process where CO2 is removed from the solvent.
==PAGE:21==
Chapter 2
Background and Motivation
This section details section details the motivation for studying CO2 capture technologies and
provides background information related to the impetus behind integrating CO2 capture sys-
tems in Waste-to-Energy (WtE) facilities in Denmark. Additionally, a state of the art section
presents an overview of the available technologies and details the work conducted on heat
recovery technologies in amine CO2 capture systems.
2.1
Introduction to Carbon Capture Utilization and Storage
The current global warming phenomenon that scientists have observed since the 1950s is largely
attributed to anthropogenic emissions of a number of gases that contribute to an amplification
of the Greenhouse Effect [1]. The Greenhouse Effect is a term commonly used to describe the
warming that results from the Earth’s atmosphere trapping heat radiation from its path from
Earth towards space [2]. The list of gasses that prevent heat radiation from escaping are known
as Greenhouse Gases (GHGs) and include water vapor (H2O), nitrous oxide (N2O), methane
(CH4), fluorocarbons (HFCs, PFCs etc.) and carbon dioxide (CO2) [2]. Of the total GHG
emissions worldwide, CO2 is by far the largest contributor, accounting for 76 % of global GHG
emissions (measured in CO2-equivalents), while CH4, N2O, and fluorocarbons account for 16,
6, and 2 %, respectively [3]. Since CO2 is the predominant emission from point sources such as
power plants and industrial facilities, and are thus the easiest to mitigate with carbon capture,
all data and figures in this section account for pure CO2 emissions only.
Since the industrial revolution, humanity’s reliance on the combustion of fossil fuels such as
coal, oil and gas has increased the concentration of CO2 in the atmosphere by nearly 47 %
from 280 parts per million (ppm) before the industrial age to nearly 412 ppm by 2020 [4].
The developments in worldwide CO2 emissions along with individual contributions from the
six countries or regions with the largest emissions may be seen plotted in fig. 2.1. It should
be noted that the emissions shown in fig. 2.1 account only for CO2, and thus do not include
CO2-equivalent emissions from e.g. CH4 and N2O. The emissions share from the European
Union (EU) is shown for the EU-28 countries, which includes the 27 current EU countries
and the United Kingdom. As it may be seen in fig. 2.1, worldwide CO2 emissions have been
increasing steadily since year 1900, and have accelerated particularly rapidly after year 2000
with the emissions contribution from countries such as India and China increasing significantly.
While the exact consequences of climactic changes from the Greenhouse effect are diﬀicult to
predict, a number of likely effects that could adversely impact the overall livability of the planet
==PAGE:22==

Figure 2.1: Worldwide annual CO2 emissions from year 1900 to 2019 measured in billions of
tons. Individual shares from the 5 largest single emitters along with the EU-28
countries are shown. Adapted with data from [5].
have been identified. These will be briefly described in the following.
Based on a number of CO2 emissions scenarios, scientists have predicted that the average
surface temperature will increase by 2–6 °C by year 2100 [6]. Since plants, animals and the
agricultural crops we rely on for sustenance need suitable conditions to grow, rising tempera-
tures can potentially represent an existential threat to these species [6]. The global increase
in temperature is projected to increase the intensity and the spread of extreme weather events
such as droughts, hurricanes, heat waves and floods [7]. This increase in intensity and spread is
projected to increase the number of human casualties and material losses from extreme weather
events [8]. Increasing temperatures are also likely to increase the melting of the World’s glaciers
and snow-covered surfaces. This will cause surfaces that at present reflect large amounts of
radiation energy away from Earth to disappear and instead become surfaces that absorb radi-
ation energy — trapping further heat energy in Earth’s atmosphere [6]. Much of the emitted
CO2 as well as the heat energy trapped by the Greenhouse Effect is absorbed by the Worlds
oceans. The absorption of CO2 has caused an acidification of the surface water that can be
detrimental to the life cycle of many marine species. Finally, the aforementioned melting of
the Worlds ice- and snow-covered surfaces is projected to increase the sea level by 0.8 to 2.0
meters by year 2100, which places coastal cities and low-lying islands at greatly increased risk
of flooding and storm surges [8].
The described projected climate change related impacts paint a stark picture and should serve
as the motivator for climate action and efforts to mitigate and prevent the consequences of
global warming.
The Paris Agreement is a legally binding international treaty on climate change that was almost
universally adopted by the worlds countries, being signed by 196 parties on December 12th, 2015
and entering into effect on November 4th, 2016. The agreement targets to keep the increase
in the global average temperature to well below 2 °C above pre-industrial levels and to pursue
==PAGE:23==
2.1 Introduction to Carbon Capture Utilization and Storage
5
efforts to limit the temperature increase to 1.5 °C above pre-industrial levels. The motivation
for maintaining the temperature change below this level is that this will significantly reduce
the risks and impacts of climate change [9].
The Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC) published a report in 2018 on the
impacts of global warming scenarios with Global Mean Surface Temperatures (GMST) of 1.5 °C
versus 2 °C above pre-industrial levels and detailed the specific pathways and mitigation strate-
gies through which, the GMST may be kept below 1.5 °C [10]. The report details the conse-
quences of keeping the increase in the GMST below 1.5 °C, which is summarized in the following.
• Human activities are estimated to have caused a 1.0 °C increase in the GMST above
pre-industrial levels. Global warming is likely to reach 1.5 °C between 2030 and 2052 if
the increase continues at the present rate. [10]
• Climate-related risks for both natural and human systems are higher for global warming
of 1.5 °C than present risks, but lower than the risks imposed by warming of 2 °C [10].
• Climate models project that global warming between 1.5 °C to 2 °C will cause changing
weather patterns with hot extremes in most inhabited regions, increasing the intensity or
frequency of droughts in some regions as well as heavy precipitation and increased flood
hazards in other regions. These consequences are projected to be less severe for global
warming of 1.5 °C than for 2 °C. [10]
• Global mean sea level rise is projected to be around 0.1 meter lower for global warming
of 1.5 °C than for 2 °C, corresponding to 10 million fewer people being affected by sea
level rise. [10]
Thus the IPCC makes a very clear case for continuing and expanding efforts to limit global
warming to below 1.5 °C. The report additionally finds that all modeled pathways that limit
global warming to 1.5 °C, the net global anthropogenic CO2 emissions decline by 45 % from
2010 levels by 2030, ultimately reaching net zero around 2050. For scenarios that limit global
warming to 2 °C, global CO2 emissions decline by 25 % from 2010 levels by 2030, ultimately
reaching net zero around 2070 [10]. Worldwide CO2 emissions from year 1900 to year 2019 as
well as the CO2 emission reduction that, according to the pathways modeled in the IPCC report
[10], is required to limit global warming to 1.5 °C and 2 °C may be seen in fig. 2.2. It should
be noted, that fig. 2.2 accounts only for emissions of CO2 and not other GHGs. Thus, actually
realizing net zero GHG emissions by 2050 or 2070 will require similarly steep reductions from
the other GHGs such as CH4 and N2O.
The IPCC report details a large number of different model pathways that all limit global
warming to 1.5 °C with no or limited overshoot. All modeled pathways actively utilize Carbon
Dioxide Removal (CDR), but the exact extent and method of implementation varies across
pathways. Some pathways utilize afforestation and reforestation only to facilitate active CDR,
while others rely on a combination of Bioenergy Carbon Capture and Storage (BECCS) and
afforestation and reforestation to offset unavoidable emissions. Interestingly, most of the model
pathways utilize BECCS and/or Direct Air Capture (DAC) with only scenarios with a very
low energy demand not utilizing these technologies. [10]
A special report by the International Energy Agency (IEA) [11] published in 2020 reached
similar conclusions. The report describes that CCUS, a broader term describing both CO2
==PAGE:24==

Figure 2.2: Worldwide annual CO2 emissions from year 1900 to 2019 measured in billions of
tons. The CO2 emission reduction that, according to the pathways modeled in the
IPCC report [10], is required to limit global warming to 1.5 °C and 2 °C, may be
seen plotted on the right. Adapted with data from [5].
storage and utilization schemes, “will need to form a key pillar of efforts to put the world on
path to net-zero emissions” and envisions a net-zero energy system that utilizes a wide portfolio
of technologies, where CCUS alongside electrification, hydrogen and sustainable bioenergy will
play a major role. Electrification, hydrogen technology and sustainable bioenergy all contribute
to reducing emissions in key sectors, while CCUS may be deployed to remove CO2 to balance
emissions that cannot be avoided. The report additionally states, that reaching net zero will
be virtually impossible without the deployment of CCUS. [11]
The IEA describes the contribution from CCUS in the clean energy transition in the following
ways: CCUS may be retrofitted to existing energy infrastructure to tackle emissions. Addi-
tionally, CCUS may be integrated in heavy industry, such as cement production, iron and steel
and chemicals manufacturing, that according to the report account for 20 % of global CO2
emissions today. Further, the report details how CCUS can be applied for a rapid expansion
of low-carbon hydrogen production, eg. by integrating downstream CCUS in steam reforming
of natural gas. Finally, CCUS applied to bioenergy facilities may be used to realize negative
emissions that may be used to offset emissions from other sectors. [11]
The IEA report [11] details a sustainable development scenario in which global CO2 emissions
decrease to net-zero in 2070 - similarly to the 2 °C pathway detailed in the IPCC report [10].
In the IEA scenario the initial focus for CCUS deployment, which accounts for 15 % of the
cumulative emissions reduction, is on retrofitting existing fossil-fuel based power and industrial
plants as well as deploying CCUS for low-carbon hydrogen production. If the target for net-
zero emissions is moved forward to year 2050, the deployment of CCUS will need to be 50 %
larger. The report however also finds that a major deployment of CCUS hinges on getting the
infrastructure for transportation, utilization and sequestration right. The development of CO2
hubs or clusters, in which industrial players and power plants in close geographical proximity,
use shared transport and sequestration infrastructure could be a major accelerator of CCUS
==PAGE:25==
2.1 Introduction to Carbon Capture Utilization and Storage
7
deployment by reducing costs. Twelve such hubs are under development globally in Australia,
Europe and the United States. [11]
2.1.1
Carbon Capture and Storage Developments in Denmark
In 2020 the Danish Parliament passed new climate legislation, the purpose of which is to reduce
GHG emissions in the year 2030 by 70% compared with emission levels in 1990. Additionally,
the new climate legislation targets for Denmark to become climate neutral and thus reach net
zero emissions by the year 2050. By net zero emissions, the climate legislation specifies that
Denmark shall not emit more GHGs than it absorbs [12]. The annual Danish CO2 emissions in
millions of tons from year 1900 to year 2019 may be seen in fig. 2.3. Additionally, the required
trend line corresponding to the fulfillment of the 70% reduction goal by 2030 and net-zero
CO2 emission target defined in the climate legislation may be seen highlighted in yellow at
the right of the graph. While it may be seen that the emissions in 2019 have already been
significantly decreased from the highest levels before the turn of the millennium, fulfilling the
target emissions reduction in 2030 and net-zero in 2050 will require a steep emissions decrease
in the coming decades. It should be noted, that the numbers shown in both fig. 2.3 and table 2.1
account only for fossil CO2 emissions and do not include CO2-equivalent emissions from e.g.
CH4 and N2O. Thus, actually realizing net zero GHG emissions by 2050 will require similarly
steep reductions from eg. CH4 and N2O.

initiatives of the Danish government to determine whether the initiatives currently implemented

are suﬀicient to reach the required emissions reduction by year 2030. The report finds that
the current climate efforts of the Danish government are insuﬀicient to reach the 2030 target,
and further states that there is a need to deploy new technologies that can provide negative
emissions to counterweight CO2 emissions that may still exist in year 2030 and even by year
2050. [13]
In an earlier report from March 2020, the DCCC describes the efforts required to achieve
the 70 % reduction goal by 2030.
As part of a larger catalog of technologies, the report
projects an annual emissions reduction through the implementation of CCS in Denmark of
approximately 4.5 MtCO2/yr from a number of sectors. The projected CCS emissions reduction
include 1.2 MtCO2/yr from biogas production, 1.1 MtCO2/yr from Waste-to-Energy facilities,
1.2 MtCO2/yr from biomass power plants and finally, 1.0 MtCO2/yr from industrial facilities.
Aditionally, the report estimates the cost of achieving a 1 t CO2 emission reduction through
CCS to be 1 000 DKK. The report however, also emphasizes that the deployment of CCS
should not become a reason to halt developments of technologies that reduce the reliance on
fossil fuels. [14]
In a report by the Danish Economic Councils from March 2021, the Danish climate policies
going forward to year 2030 are addressed.
The report finds that the implementation of a
uniform carbon taxation is important to reach the 2030 emissions reduction and that the
continuation of such a tax is vital to achieving climate neutrality by 2050. Modelling results
that include deployment and subsidies for CCS find that a uniform GHG-taxation covering all
net-emissions in Denmark must increase to approximately 1 200 DKK/tCO2 to ensure the 2030
emissions reduction target. For this taxation level, a large-scale deployment of BECCS that
through negative emissions achieve a 5 MtCO2/yr emissions reduction towards 2030 is required.
The degree to which the deployment of BECCS is implemented impacts greatly the required
CO2 tax, with the report stating that if only half of the BECCS potential is utilized, then
the resultant CO2 tax must be on the order of 2 000 DKK/tCO2 to reach the 2030 emissions
reduction target. [15]
2.1.2
Worldwide CO2 storage potential
In [16], a consistent database for worldwide geological CO2 storage capacity with specific capac-
ities for each region was developed. The paper distinguishes between onshore and offshore CO2
storage capacity with offshore storage being further divided into technical storage potential and
practical storage potential, with practical offshore CO2 storage capacity being limited to water
depths of less than 300 m and locations within 320 km of a major landmass. Additionally,
both a lower and an upper estimate with storage potentials of 0.0037 GtCO2 per 1 000 km3
and 0.26 GtCO2 per 1 000 km3 of sedimentary basin, respectively are applied. [16] Results for
global estimates of the geological CO2 storage potentials are summarized in table 2.2.

With a simple summation of the data for which the period between year 1900 and 2019 is
plotted in fig. 2.1, the global CO2 emissions from year 1750 to 2019 may be calculated. The
data is sourced from [5]. The result of this summation reveals that from 1750 to 2019 global
cumulative CO2 emissions amount to approximately 1 653 GtCO2. This means that even with
the lower estimate of global CO2 storage potentials, all of the worlds historical CO2 emissions
may be sequestered in geological formations many times over, and it may thus be possible to
in effect turn back the clock on anthropogenic CO2 emissions to pre-industrial levels.
In a report from 2016 by Geological Survey of Denmark and Greenland (GEUS) the CO2
storage potential in saline aquifers and hydrocarbon fields in the Nordic region was mapped
with a simple methodology based on the volumetric calculations of the available pore space
and the associated estimated storage eﬀiciency factor. The results are summarized in table 2.3.
[17]

In addition to the storage potential in Denmark, Norway and Sweden, the report also details
the storage potential in porous basalts in Iceland, where capacity estimates range from 21 000
MtCO2 to 400 000 MtCO2 [17].
The 2016 report also specifies the CO2 emissions from all large (>70 ktCO2/yr) stationary
point sources in Denmark, Norway and Sweden as 25.8 MtCO2/yr, 23.1 MtCO2/yr and 46.6
MtCO2/yr, respectively.
On a European scale, the report details the total emissions from
stationary point sources as 2 000 MtCO2/yr, which would imply that approximately 67 annual
emission equivalents from these sources may be stored in geological formations in the Nordic
region. [17]
By applying the same methodology as with the calculation of the global cumulative CO2 emis-
sions, the cumulative Danish CO2 emissions from all sources may be calculated with data from
[5]. The cumulative Danish CO2 emissions from year 1853 to 2019 amount to approximately
4 057 MtCO2.
This means that in Danish territories alone, it is possible to sequester the
cumulative Danish CO2 emissions six times over. More likely though, with the proper infras-
tructure established, the Nordic region may potentially, in a future scenario with widespread
deployment of CCS in Europe, be an endpoint destination for sequestration of captured CO2
from countries with more limited storage resources.
While this project focuses exclusively on CCS technology, it is important to acknowledge that
==PAGE:28==
2.1 Introduction to Carbon Capture Utilization and Storage
10
CCUS technology is also a valid option being investigated to convert the captured CO2 into
useful products such as green fuels. CCUS will not be described in depth in this section, but
a description of some of the potential utilization pathways for captured CO2 is included in
appendix B.
2.1.3
Carbon Capture from Waste-to-Energy Facilities
A special report by IEA Bioenergy from 2003 [18] assesses the role and sustainability of Waste-
to-Energy (WtE) facilities fueled with Municipal Solid Waste (MSW). The MSW typically
treated by WtE facilities typically consists primarily of waste products generated by households
but can also include commercial and industrial waste products. The carbon content in MSW
is classically divided into fossil carbon and biogenic carbon, where fossil carbon is derived
from e.g. plastics or other oil-derived products and biogenic carbon is derived from biomass,
e.g. paper and organic waste. Assessments have shown that 20-40 % of the carbon in MSW
is fossil carbon, with the remaining fraction being renewable biomass.
The lower calorific
value is typically in the range between 8-12 MJ/kg for MSW, heavily dependent upon the
individual fractions of fossil and biogenic wastes. When the energy in the MSW is recovered
with conventional WtE-systems, the report details the total CO2 emission per ton of MSW as
1 100 kg. [18]
As long as consumption patterns that generate non-recyclable wastes exists, there will be a
need for waste disposal.
The fact that the utility of WtE plants is twofold; disposing of
waste products and recovering the energy as power and/or heating, means that WtE facilities
generally operate as base-load facilities. The combination of being base-load plants and having
a relatively high biogenic waste fraction makes WtE plants uniquely suited for BECCS, and
can thus meaningfully be applied to achieve negative CO2 emissions. In comparison with other
power generation facilities that fluctuate heavily in both energy in- and output to meet the
varying consumption of the energy grid, the base-load operation of the WtE plants mean that
as they are typically operating at or near the design load of the facility. Because of this they
can make better use of the design capacity of the facility, and WtE plants are thus uniquely
suited to realize negative emissions with a relatively low cost of carbon capture.
Globally around 2 500 Waste-to-Energy (WtE) facilites are in operation, with a combined
annual waste burning capacity of approximately 400 MtMSW [19]. Additionally, the current
prognosis is that with ongoing market expansion for WtE facilities the overall incineration
capacity will be 550 MtMSW/yr by 2029 [19]. Thus adopting the emission factor detailed in
[18] and assuming they operate at design capacity, this equates to a present day global CO2
emission of 440 MtCO2 from WtE facilites worldwide equal to approximately 1.21 % of global
total CO2 emissions from all sectors in 2019 of 36.41 GtCO2 [5].
In a 2020 report for the Danish Waste Association, Rambøll mapped the Danish total WtE ca-
pacity to 4.0 MtMSW. However, conversely to the worldwide projections for WtE, the Danish
combined capacity is projected to decrease to 3.3 MtMSW with the advent of new large-scale
recycling schemes and waste-sorting infrastructure [20]. Regardless of future projections, by
applying the emission factor detailed in [18], the current annual CO2 emission from the com-
bined Danish WtE capacity operating at rated capacity is 4.4 MtCO2/yr. Assuming a fossil
emissions percentage of 40 % this is equal to approximately 11 % of the fossil CO2 emissions
reduction necessary to reach the 2030 target described in the Danish climate legislation. As-
suming that the Danish WtE facilities are retrofitted with CCS modules with a 90 % CO2
==PAGE:29==
2.2 The Carbon Capture Project at Amager Resource Center
11
removal rate, which means that they will capture both fossil and biogenic CO2, the current
Danish WtE facilities are able to deliver 24.7 % of the CO2 emissions reduction necessary to
reach the 2030 target described in the Danish climate legislation.
Decarbonizing the WtE
plants in Denmark with CCS would thus go a long way to realize the climate goals set by
the Danish government, and would ultimately present a very promising solution for realizing
long-term negative emissions that can offset emissions stemming from eg. transportation and
aviation.
2.2
The Carbon Capture Project at Amager Resource Center
Motivated by the ambition of the Municipality of Copenhagen to reach net-zero CO2 emissions
by 2025, Amager Resource Center (ARC) who operates and maintains the Amager Bakke WtE
facility in Copenhagen, initiated in early 2019 an investigation of integrating a carbon capture
system at ARC. The Amager Bakke facility emits approximately 480 000 tons of CO2 annually,
and the implementation of carbon capture would be able to deliver a significant CO2 emissions
reduction in the path to realize the ambitions of the Municipality. A study conducted by ARC
in February 2020 found that coupling the WtE plant with carbon capture is both technically
and economically feasible [21].
As a stepping stone to full-scale carbon capture at ARC, a consortium consisting of ARC, the
Technical University of Denmark (DTU), Pentair PLC and, Rambøll submitted an application
for funding from the Danish Energy Technology Demonstration Program (EUDP) for a research
project demonstrating that a full-scale carbon capture system can be integrated at ARC with
a net-zero energy consumption. The expectation is that reducing the energy consumption will
in turn reduce the cost of carbon capture. Funding for the EUDP project was granted in May
2020 and development works began in November 2020. ARC has estimated that the investment
costs required for a full-scale carbon capture facility at ARC to be between 450 to 900 million
DKK with annual operating costs of 85 million DKK. [21]
The principal purpose of the EUDP project, is to construct, commission and operate a cost-
effective pilot scale carbon capture plant at ARC and to demonstrate operation of the pilot
plant at net-zero energy consumption. Additionally, the collection of data and learnings from
operation of the pilot scale facility will be key to determine how to optimize the layout, config-
uration and overall operation of carbon capture systems. The final outcome of the project will
be to inform about the technologies that may be relevant in a full-scale carbon capture facility
at ARC. [21]
The overall carbon capture project at ARC includes four phases. Phase one is a pre-study
and initial preparations. Phase two is the Front End Engineering Design (FEED) study to
clarify what technologies must be demonstrated in the pilot plant. Phase three includes the
construction and commissioning of a 12 tons CO2 per day pilot scale carbon capture facility.
Phase four includes the actual demonstration of the pilot scale and the gaining of first-hand
experience with carbon capture systems in a WtE environment.
Phase five is a full-scale
deployment of carbon capture at ARC which is currently planned for 2025. [21]
A detailed description of the current ARC WtE facility may be found in the following section,
section 3.1.
==PAGE:30==
2.3 State of The Art
12
2.3
State of The Art
This section will provide an overview of the available CO2 capture technologies and describes
the motivation for investigating an amine-based CO2 capture technology for this report. Addi-
tionally, an overview of the strategies investigated in scientific literature for waste heat recovery
from amine-based CO2 capture systems are presented.
2.3.1
Available Carbon Capture Technologies
Carbon Capture technologies relevant for power generation facilities are classically divided into
three separate sub-categories that include pre-combustion capture, oxy-fuel combustion and
post-combustion capture. A summary table listing the advantages and disadvantages of each
carbon capture technology may be seen in table 2.4.

This project focuses solely on a post-combustion CO2 capture integration, due to the tech-
nology being more applicable for power plant retrofits, as shown in table 2.4. As a result,
pre-combustion capture and oxy-fuel combustion will not be further detailed in this section.
Instead, a brief description of each of these processes may be found in appendix C.
2.3.1.1
Post-Combustion Capture
As the name suggests, post combustion capture removes the CO2 from the flue gases after
combustion has taken place. It is the preferred option for retrofitting existing power plants
with carbon capture, since it may with little modification to the existing plant be fitted to
the tail-end of a power and/or heat generation facilities.
Post-combustion technology has
==PAGE:31==
2.3 State of The Art
13
been proven for relatively small-scale facilities with CO2 recovery rates upwards of 800 tons
per day [22]. The major drawback of post-combustion CO2 capture is a large parasitic load
when integrated in power generation facilities. Due to the relatively low concentration of CO2
in conventional combustion flue gases, the energy penalty is larger for post-combustion CO2
capture than for pre-combustion capture. [22]
2.3.1.2
CO2 Separation Technologies
This section will briefly detail the two main CO2 separation technologies that may be appropri-
ately utilized in post-combustion carbon capture to remove CO2 from flue gas flows; absorption
and adsorption. Other advanced technologies for CO2 removal also exist, such as membranes,
hydrates and cryogenics. These will however not be discussed in this paper.
In absorption-based CO2 separation technologies, a liquid sorbent is used to separate the CO2
from the flue gas. The sorbent may then be regenerated through a stripping or desorption
process by heat addition and or depressurization. Absorption-based CO2 separation is a mature
technology [22] that has been used for decades in the oil and gas industries [23]. Common
sorbents include monoethanolamine (MEA), diethanolamine (DEA) and potassium carbonate
[22].
Adsorption-based CO2 separation uses a solid sorbent to bind the CO2 in the flue gas on the
surfaces of the sorbent. Thus large specific surface area, high selectivity and high regeneration
ability are the main criteria for sorbent selection. Typical adsorbents include molecular sieves,
activated carbon, zeolites, calcium oxides, hydro-talcites and lithium zirconate. The CO2 ad-
sorbed on the surface of the sorbent may be recovered by swinging the pressure or temperature
of the system containing the CO2 saturated sorbent. CO2 is preferentially absorbed on the
surface of the solid sorbent at high pressure or low temperature and desorbed at low pressure
or high temperature. [22]
A summary table detailing the advantages and disadvantages of absorption- and adsorption-
based CO2 separation may be found in table 2.5.
Table 2.5: Advantages and disadvantages of the different CO2 separation technologies.
Adapted from [22].
Separation process
Advantage
Disadvantages
Absorption
High absorption eﬀiciency (>90 %).
Sorbents can be regenerated with heat-
ing and/or depressurization. Most ma-

gies.

2.3.2
Amine Scrubbing Technology for Carbon Capture
Of the CO2 separation technologies described above, absorption-type CO2 capture with amine-
based solvents is currently the most technologically mature and cost-effective solution. Amine
CO2 capture technologies have been used for more than 60 years to remove CO2 from natural
==PAGE:32==
2.3 State of The Art
14
gas in a process known as natural gas sweetening. However, integration of amine-based CO2
capture in the flue gas streams from power generation facilities is substantially different than
CO2 capture from natural gas. This is due primarily to the much lower partial pressure of CO2
and relatively high temperature of the flue gas. [23]
Amine scrubbing technologies capture CO2 with an aqueous amine solution, that is typically
denoted as the solvent [23].
A process flow diagram for a typical amine scrubbing system
integrated in a power plant may be seen in fig. 2.4. As is shown in the diagram, flue gas is
passed through a scrubber that contains the aqueous amine solvent, which absorbs the CO2
contained in the flue gas. The CO2 rich amine is then sent to a stripper column where it
is heated and the absorbed CO2 is released or desorbed from the solvent. After the CO2 is
released it is typically compressed and/or liquefied for transportation. The biggest disadvantage
of amine-based CO2 capture is the high amount energy required for the solvent regeneration
or CO2 desorption. Of the amines applied for CO2 capture, monoethanolamine (MEA) is by
far the most well studied and is often considered as the bench-mark for amine scrubbing. [23]
For this reason, a MEA-based CO2 capture system is investigated in this thesis.
Figure 2.4: Process flow diagram for a typical amine scrubbing unit. From [24].
A traditional amine-based CO2 capture system has specific reboiler duty around 4.0 MJ/kgCO2
[24]. This is thus the overall external energy input that must be delivered to the reboiler, shown
in fig. 2.4. For amine CO2 capture, the solvent regeneration energy is typically sourced from the
power plants’ steam cycle [23], which has a significant penalty on the overall energy production
and eﬀiciency of the facility. For MEA-based solvents this penalty can incur an upwards of
30 % eﬀiciency drop [25, 26]. The high required energy for solvent regeneration remains the
biggest technical challenge with MEA-based CO2 capture.
2.3.3
Heat Pump Integration in Amine Carbon Capture Systems
Due to the high energy requirement for solvent regeneration in MEA CO2 capture systems,
numerous research projects have studied ways to recover and reuse the waste heat from the
CO2 capture system. Since MEA-based CO2 capture systems generally require both process
heating and cooling, several projects have been dedicated to investigating recovery of waste
heat to deliver it as useful heat in the CO2 desorption process through the utilization of heat
pump systems.
In Abdulkareem et al.
[27], seven configurations of heat pumps were integrated with the
CO2 capture plant of a Natural Gas Combined Cycle (NGCC) power plant. The heat pumps
==PAGE:33==
2.3 State of The Art
15
provide the required heat input for solvent regeneration by recovering the waste heat from
the CO2 capture plant.
A large number of refrigerants were investigated for the different
heat pump configurations with the best investigated combination yielding a reduction of the
energy input required for solvent regeneration and CO2 compression of 16 % compared to a
base case using steam extraction for solvent regeneration. Additionally the overall power plant
eﬀiciency increased 13.5 % with the best heat pump and refrigerant combination compared to
the predescribed base case.
Jeong et al. [28] investigated a Mechanical Vapor Recompression (MVR) system that reduces
the thermal energy required for solvent regeneration by recovering heat from the compression
process required for CO2 transportation.
Specifically, they investigated a two-stage MVR
system that aside from reducing the amount of steam extracted from the power plant also
serves as the interface between CO2 capture and compression. A process flow diagram of this
MVR system may be seen in fig. 2.5. From simulation results, an overall energy saving of 8.4
% was obtained in comparison with a base case that comprises a conventional CO2 capture
process and a two-stage CO2 compression. [28]
Figure 2.5: Process flow diagram for two-stage MVR process (Absorber column is not shown).
From [28].
In Reddick et al. [29] waste heat upgrading using steam ejectors in a CO2 capture system
using a 20 wt.% MEA solvent was investigated. In total three separate waste heat recovery
strategies were investigated to produce the ejector secondary steam; (1) using the stripper
overhead condensate, (2) using the lean solvent and, (3) using the rich solvent flow.
The
results show that steam ejector waste heat recovery utilizing the stripper overhead condensate
and the lean solvent to produce the ejector secondary steam are viable options with total energy
savings of 10 % and 14 %, respectively. [29]
In Muhammad et al. [30] a Lean Vapor Compression (LVC) system was investigated for a MEA-
based CO2 capture facility. A process flow diagram for a generic LVC system may be seen in
fig. 2.6. LVC systems recover heat from the hot lean amine stream from the desorber to generate
steam for the desorption process, thus saving external energy input from the reboiler. The lean
==PAGE:34==
2.3 State of The Art
16
amine stream stemming from the desorber is throttled to a 2-phase mixture and directed to a
flash drum where the vapor phase flashes off and is compressed to the desorber pressure and
returned to the desorber. While the results show that the reboiler steam requirement may
be decreased by upwards of 18%, factoring in the required energy input for the lean vapor
compressor and additional pump work, a net energy decrease of 7.3% may be realized with
LVC. [30]
Figure 2.6: Process flow diagram for Lean Vapor Compression system. From [24].
Andersson et al. [26] studies a MEA-based CO2 capture system for the Sävenas WtE facility
in Gothenburg, Sweden. For the integration, a heat pump is integrated in the CO2 capture
system to recover waste heat streams and to a lesser degree, waste heat is recovered with
district heating return water. The integration of a base-case CO2 capture system incurs an
eﬀiciency penalty relative to the base plant without CO2 capture on a yearly basis of 30 %.
By implementing these heat recovery technologies, the eﬀiciency loss is reduced to only 12 %
lower than the base plant without CO2 capture. [26]
Thus while internal optimizations and heat recovery technologies that minimize the overall
reboiler duty have been extensively studied, heat recovery from CO2 capture systems to district
heating has not previously been the main focus of scientific literature to reduce the costs of
carbon capture. Due to the unique opportunity for the ARC WtE facility to recover waste
heat to the district heating networks connected to the plant, this will be the main focus for
investigation in this report.
==PAGE:35==
Chapter 3
Methods
This section details the methodologies applied throughout this project. Holistic modelling of the
entire Amager Bakke WtE plant is conducted in Thermoflex®1 (TFX) in a complete Ramboll-
developed model of the water-steam cycle and flue gas path. TFX is useful for modelling of
large-scale thermal systems, but it is not suitable for detailed design work of subsystems due
to limited flexibility and high computational power required to run parametric studies for the
thermodynamic model of the ARC WtE facility. For this reason, detailed design work, inves-
tigation of subsystems and optimization efforts have been performed in Engineering Equation
Solver (EES).
The structure of the methods section will be addressed in the following. Initially, a detailed
description of the process at the ARC WtE facility is presented to provide the reader with
an overview of the facility.
After providing the overview of ARC, a definition of the nine
investigated scenarios through this report is presented.
Then the modelling methodologies
applied to model the existing WtE facility without CO2 capture integration is detailed to
provide the reader with an overview of how the different systems are modelled. After this, the
results of the chemical process modelling are shown. Based on the chemical process modelling
results, a reference CO2 capture system is defined. This reference CO2 capture system will be
applied more or less unchanged throughout the investigated scenarios that include integration
of CO2 capture. With the reference CO2 capture system defined, a detailed description of the
applied modelling methodologies in the scenarios are presented. Finally, the applied methods
for the techno-economic evaluations are detailed.
3.1
Description of The ARC Waste-to-Energy Plant
The ARC WtE faility consists of two identical waste incineration boiler lines, each with a
design capacity of 35 tons of MSW per hour [21]. In operation at the nominal point, each
boiler raises 38.8 kg/s of high-pressure steam at 440 °C and 70 bar. The facility was originally
designed for MSW with a Lower Heating Value (LHV) of 11.5 MJ/kg. The value of LHV
and the design waste capacity correspond to a total energy input for both boiler lines of 223.6
MJ/s. It should be noted however, that the actual LHV of the MSW incinerated at the facility
today is typically between 9.3-9.4 MJ/kg, which means that the actual waste throughput in
each boiler line is closer to 43 tons/hr when operating at nominal steam generation. The ARC
WtE facility has a nameplate fuel utilization of 108 % measured on the Lower Heating Value,
1Thermoflex is a registered trademark of Thermoflow Inc.
==PAGE:36==
3.1 Description of The ARC Waste-to-Energy Plant
18
when operating at nominal conditions.2
A schematic overview of the process equipment at ARC may be seen in fig. 3.1. As is shown,
the flue gas path from the boiler outlet travels through an Electrostatic Precipitator (ESP) to
remove particulate matter. After the ESP, NOx is removed by reduction with ammonia-water
in a Selective Catalytic Reduction (SCR) system. After the SCR, flue gases are cooled in a
low-temperature Economizer (ECO) before being led through the first acidic scrubber that
removes Hydrochloric acid (HCl) and heavy metals present in the flue gases. A second caustic
scrubber removes SOx by scrubbing the flue gases with limestone. Finally, a third scrubber
or Flue Gas Condensation (FGC) unit extracts the condensation heat from the flue gas to
recover it as district heating. The scrubbing in the third scrubber occurs with the presence of
activated charcoal to reduce emissions of dioxin, furans and mercury. The flue gas condensation
is performed in two stages, denoted in this paper as FGC1 and FGC2, respectively. Of these
two, FGC1 may be said to be a direct FGC unit, cooled by District Heating (DH) return water
from the HOFOR DH network, while the second, FGC2, is indirectly cooled by a chilled water
circuit with Absorption Heat Pumps. While the temperature levels at the inlet and outlet of
FGC1 and FGC2 fluctuate throughout the year, depending on DH requirements, the average
temperature levels in nominal operation is approximately 65 °C at the inlet of FGC1, 55 °C at
the outlet of FGC1 and inlet of FGC2 and 37 °C at the outlet of FGC2 3.
Figure 3.1: Schematic overview of the process equipment at ARC. Adapted from [21].
Flue gas condensation units are basically gas-to-water heat exchangers that are characterized
by a very large direct contact area between the cooling media and the gas. In typical operation
water droplets are sprayed in a counter-current direct contact heat exchange column, where
a packing material allows for a very large overall heat transfer coeﬀicient (UA-value), which
enables very low pinch temperatures.
On the first stage, FGC1, there is a manufacturer-
2Information supplied by ARC
3Information from average values from ARCs CMS system
==PAGE:37==
3.1 Description of The ARC Waste-to-Energy Plant
19
supplied guaranteed maximum pinch temperature in nominal operation between the district
heating return water in the HOFOR district heating network and the cooled outlet flue gas of
1.8 °C 4. Similarly, for the FGC2 unit, there is a manufacturer-supplied guaranteed maximum
pinch temperature in nominal operation between the coolant circuit from the heat pumps and
the cooled outlet flue gas of 2.5 °C3. These values are of course liable to change in part load
operation of the plant, but as this project is centered strictly on the integration of a CC facility
operating at the nominal point, relatively low pinch temperatures can be assumed for the flue
gas condensation units in the integrations.
After FGC1 and FGC2, the flue gas is reheated and forced through an Induced Draft (ID)
fan to the ambient. The principal purpose of the flue gas reheating is to prevent water vapor
condensing on the inside of the stack, while the ID-fan serves to maintain a slight vacuum
throughout the flue gas path from the boiler to the stack. Maintaining a slight vacuum pre-
vents the egress of toxic fumes and particles from the boiler. Additionally, ARC has a relatively
advanced multi-stage condensate treatment system that is primarily used to treat the conden-
sate coming from the condensing water vapor in FGC1 and FGC2. The condensate is treated
and cleaned with the end-product being water suitable for use as make-up water for the boiler
and steam systems. This condensate treatment system will not be included in the modelling
of ARC.
The high-pressure, high-temperature steam raised by the boiler is expanded through a back-
pressure turbine in six separate turbine stages with different turbine bleeds and extraction
points as shown in fig. 3.2. A distinction is made that turbine bleeds are governed by the heat
requirements of the bleed endpoint, while extraction points may be controlled by means of
valves in the turbine. Before live steam enters the turbine on the left side of the schematic, a
turbine bypass allows for extractions of live steam for various applications. The turbine bypass
is classically used during start-ups of the plant as well as in periods with high heat demand,
where live steam may be bypassed directly to the third turbine condenser to maximize district
heating production.
As suggested in fig. 3.2, the turbine bypass will also, in a number of
scenarios with integrated carbon capture systems, be utilized as the energy input required for
the CO2 desorption process. After the turbine bypass, steam expands through the first turbine
stage, denoted as T1. T1 is equipped with a Turbine Control Valve (TCV) that allows for
partial admission of the first impulse stage of the turbine and regulates the pressure in the
boiler. In operation, due to the inhomogenous nature of MSW, the thermal input to the boiler
can vary significantly. As a consequence of Stodolas Ellipse Law, this can cause the pressure in
the boiler to fluctuate. From a materials engineering perspective this is undesirable, since this
temperature fluctuation induces thermal stresses in the boiler, which can potentially decrease
the lifetime of the boiler plant. After T1, a steam turbine bleed directs a small percentage of
the steam flow to the high temperature air preheater (HT LUFO). Steam then expands through
the second turbine stage, T2, followed by a large extraction point that directs steam to the
AHPs and the low temperature air preheater (LT LUFO). The extraction flow is controlled by
a grid valve installed at the inlet of the third turbine stage, T3. After T3, a small percentage
of the steam flow is bled, split into two separate streams and directed to the SCR and the
dearator. Between the fourth and fifth turbine stages, denoted as T4 and T5 respectively, is
a turbine bleed directing steam to the third turbine condenser stage, DH3. with the second
turbine condenser, DH2, being fed by a bleed between the fifth and sixth turbine stages, T5
and T6, while the turbine exhaust steam is fed to the first turbine condenser stage. It should
be noted that for modelling purposes, the bleeds for the turbine condensers are split in two
4From ARC supplier-guarantee documentation from LAB S.A.
==PAGE:38==
3.1 Description of The ARC Waste-to-Energy Plant
20
different flows directed to the two different district heating networks, as shown in fig. 3.2. In
reality though, the bleeds are not split but directly directed to each of the three separate turbine
condensers. The specifics of the turbine condenser arrangement will be discussed below.
Figure 3.2: Schematic overview of the turbine installation at ARC.
The three separate connected turbine condensers consist, internally, of two separated tube
bundles containing the two district heating flows. These two tube bundles are separated by a
wall that allows for flooding of the individual tube bundle sections to control the distribution
of condensation energy between the two district heating networks. For explanatory purposes,
a schematic of the turbine condenser arrangement at ARC with each turbine condenser split
to separate the two tube bundles may be seen in fig. 3.3. For modelling purposes in the later
sections of this paper, the turbine condensers will be modelled as separate units for the different
district heating networks. As it may be seen from fig. 3.3, turbine bleed and exhaust steam,
shown in red, enters each of the different turbine condensers from the points shown in fig. 3.2
and condenses on the tube bundles containing the respective district heating flows. Starting
with the top turbine condenser group shown in fig. 3.3 containing the HOFOR district heating
flow, district heating return water is preheated in the first, direct flue gas condensation unit,
FGC1, before passing through the three turbine condensation stages where it is heated to the
required forward temperature. Steam condensate, shown in purple, is drained from the bottom
of DH1 and pumped to DH2 where it is sprayed into the condenser. A similar setup exists
for DH2, where condensate is injected in DH3. This provides a successive preheating of the
condensate before being finally drained at the bottom of DH3 and pumped to the dearator,
where a further preheating step is achieved with turbine bleed steam as shown in fig. 3.2.
On the lower turbine condenser group shown in fig. 3.3 containing the CTR district heating
flow, district heating return water is split with the majority of the flow bypassing the first two
turbine condensation stages and only a small flow being directed through DH1 and DH2. The
CTR bypass flow is then heated to 85 °C before being remixed with the rest of the flow and
heated in DH3 to the forward temperature. According to ARC, the nominal DH production
for the HOFOR DH network is 81.4 MJ/s, while the CTR network being significantly larger is
capable of receiving significantly more heat energy.
The absorption heat pumps (AHPs) at ARC were supplied by Thermax and are rated at
82 MJ/s of heating with a nominal steam consumption of 50 MJ/s, which corresponds to a
Coeﬀicient of Performance (COP) of 1.64. The AHPs are driven by extraction steam taken at
the extraction point, E1, between T2 and T3, as shown in fig. 3.2. In typical operation however,
the AHPs deliver significantly less heating than the rated heat effect5. A schematic overview
5Information supplied by Jørgen Bøgild from ARC
==PAGE:39==
3.1 Description of The ARC Waste-to-Energy Plant
21
Figure 3.3: Schematic overview of the turbine condenser arrangement at ARC. While the
DH1, DH2 and DH3 actually include both the CTR and HOFOR streams in
each of the three condensers, these are shown as separate units for explanatory
purposes.
illustrating the couplings in the district heating connections at ARC may be seen in fig. 3.4.
For the HOFOR DH stream, the DH water flow goes through FGC1, and then goes through
the three turbine condensers DH1 to DH3 where it is heated to the forward temperature.
As previously mentioned, for the CTR DH arrangement, the flow is split with one primary
stream going directly through the three turbine condensers and the other flow bypassing the
first two turbine stages, DH1 and DH2, before being readmitted just before DH3. The DH
bypass flow is initially heated in the absorber section of the AHPs, before being directed to the
condenser section of the AHPs where additional heat energy is imparted to the bypass flow.
The evaporator section of the AHPs is used for cooling a chilled water circuit that in turns
cools the indirect flue gas condensation unit, FGC2. The drive steam leaves the AHP desorber
section as saturated liquid, after which the condensate is subcooled against the DH bypass
flow, before the now subcooled condensate is led to the dearator.
A schematic showing the dearator at ARC with incoming and outgoing streams is shown in
fig. 3.5. As it may be seen, the subcooled AHP condensate along with condensate from the
HT and LT LUFOs enter the dearator at the top right, while turbine condensate from DH3
enters the dearator on the left. Bleed steam from turbine bleed B2 enters the dearator on the
top left side to preheat the different condensate streams and thus increase the overall eﬀiciency
of the plant. Preheated condensate leaves the dearator through a drain at the bottom from
where it travels to the main feedwater pumps and is pumped into the boiler. For scenarios
with carbon capture, the condensate from the reboiler will be returned to the dearator in the
turbine condensate line as shown in fig. 3.5.
==PAGE:40==
3.1 Description of The ARC Waste-to-Energy Plant
22
Figure 3.4: Schematic overview of the CTR bypass to the absorption heat pumps with AHP
condensate subcooling.
Figure 3.5: Schematic overview of the dearator with incoming and outgoing streams.
Since all investigated scenarios, detailed in section 3.2, will be modelled with the same steam
generation layout and nearly identical operating conditions, an in-depth description of the
steam generating equipment, namely the economizer, boiler and superheating arrangement
will not be included in this study. Similarly other equipment not specifically relevant to the
energy production capacity of ARC, such as SCR and the caustic and acidic scrubbers will not
be further detailed in this study.

3.2 Definition of Investigated Scenarios
23
3.2
Definition of Investigated Scenarios
To properly validate the hypothesis, nine different scenarios are defined, two without CO2
capture and seven with integrated CO2 capture.
Based on the designed reference carbon
capture scenario that will be detailed in section 3.5, the seven different carbon capture waste
heat recovery scenarios are defined with varying degrees and methods of energy recovery. These
waste heat recovery scenarios with carbon capture (CC) utilize the future DH temperatures as
detailed in table 3.1.
The Metropolitan Copenhagen Heating Transmission company (CTR) and Greater Copen-
hagen Utility (HOFOR) have provided projections for the district heating temperatures for
their respective networks for the year 2030. Current and projected district heating temper-
atures for the two district heating networks for which ARC delivers heating may be seen in
table 3.1. All thermodynamic modelling is conducted with the winter temperature levels for
both the CTR and HOFOR networks as shown in table 3.1.
Table 3.1: Current and projected district heating temperatures at ARC.

The principal motivation for designing multiple scenarios with varying degrees of waste heat
recovery from the carbon capture facility is to evaluate whether the hypothesis stated in chap-
ter 1 is true. In total, nine separate scenarios including the reference scenario and a scenario
detailing the current WtE plant without carbon capture integration are investigated. A brief
description of the nine investigated scenarios will be included in the following.
Definition of Scenario 0
As will be detailed in section 3.3.1, Scenario 0 investigates ARC as-built without the integration
of carbon capture for the current DH temperature levels detailed in table 3.1. The purpose of
Scenario 0 is to verify the precision of the developed TFX model against real overall performance
data.
Definition of Scenario 1
The reference scenario, denoted as Scenario 1 investigates ARC as-built without the integration
of carbon capture for the future DH temperature levels detailed in table 3.1. The purpose for
this is two-fold: The reference TFX model of ARC serves to establish a reference point to
quantify the penalty of carbon capture implementation, and to verify the precision of the
thermodynamic model. A detailed description of scenario 1 may be found in section 3.3.2.
==PAGE:42==
3.2 Definition of Investigated Scenarios
24
Definition of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 2
Scenario 2 investigates implementation of CO2 capture at ARC without any heat recovery.
Thus nearly all waste heat stemming from absorber, stripper and compression train will be
cooled away to the environment using air coolers. The purpose of Scenario 2 is to establish
a benchmark against which waste heat recovery schemes may be evaluated.
It should be
noted that, in Scenario 2, the AHPs already present at ARC will be moved from their present
location at FGC2 to instead utilizing waste heat from elsewhere in the designed CO2 capture
system. Steam for the desorption process is provided with a mix of turbine bypass live steam
and extraction steam from E1 as shown in fig. 3.2. The combination of the relatively large
steam extractions for the desorption process and no heat recovery from the CC facility means
that the total DH production will decrease significantly. The compressor intercoolers and the
liquefaction unit condenser are cooled to the ambient with air coolers.
Definition of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 3
Scenario 3 utilizes the same exact configuration of the ARC CC-integration as Scenario 2.
The only difference is that the penalty relative to Scenario 1 (reference scenario) in the total
DH production incurred by the integration of CC without heat recovery is made up with
external air-source heat pumps producing district heating for the CTR network. The principal
motivation for implementing Scenario 3 is to verify that the way in which heat pump systems
are integrated in the CC facility in later scenarios yields a better solution than external heat
recovery. The compressor intercoolers and the liquefaction unit condenser are cooled to the
ambient with air coolers.
Definition of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 4
Scenario 4 utilizes the same basic configuration of the AHPs as Scenario 2 and 3. The waste
heat from the CC facility however is utilized in the CTR bypass flow with the heat exchangers
coupled to maximize the temperature where the bypass returns before DH3. Scenario 4 serves
as an evaluation of whether the AHPs at ARC present an attractive solution to realize the
targets of operating the CC facility at net zero energy consumption as detailed in section 2.2.
The compressor intercoolers and the liquefaction unit condenser are cooled to the ambient with
air coolers.
Definition of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 5
Scenario 5 utilizes the same coupling of the heat exchangers for heat recovery from the CC
facility as Scenario 4.
However, rather than using AHPs to recover low-temperature heat
energy, Scenario 5 uses vapor compression heat pumps (VCHPs) that are implemented such
that the heat rejection from the VCHPs is used to boost the temperature of the bypass flow
before final heat upgrading in DH3. An additional perceived benefit of using VCHPs is that
the elimination of extraction drive steam for the AHPs increases the steam flow through the
turbine, which increases the gross turbine power and the heat upgrading potential in DH1,
DH2, and, DH3. The compressor intercooler and the liquefaction unit condenser heat effects
are cooled to the ambient with air coolers, and Scenario 5 may thus be described as a limited
heat recovery scenario.
==PAGE:43==
3.2 Definition of Investigated Scenarios
25
Definition of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 6
Scenario 6 uses generally, the same heat exchanger and VCHP coupling as Scenario 5. The
major difference is that rather than using the high-temperature energy from the stripper outlet
for district heating, a Mechanical Vapor Recompression (MVR) system derived from Jeong
et. al. [28] is implemented to recover this high-quality heat energy as reboiler steam and thus
decrease the overall consumption of bypass and extraction steam for the desoption process. The
liquefaction unit condenser heat effect is cooled to the ambient with air coolers, and Scenario
6 may thus be described as a limited heat recovery scenario. This scenario additionally serves
to form a comparison between internal heat recovery in the CO2 capture system and external
heat recovery to DH investigated in eg. Scenario 5.
Definition of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 7
Scenario 7 is very similar to the overall configuration in Scenario 5 with the integration of
VCHPs. However contrary to Scenario 5, where the compression intercooling and the liquefac-
tion unit condenser heat effect is cooled away to the ambient, these heat sources are utilized
for district heating production. Scenario 7 may thus be described as a scenario with full heat
recovery.
Definition of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 8
Scenario 8 is very similar to the overall configuration in Scenario 6 with the integration of
VCHPs and MVR. Contrary to Scenario 6, where the liquefaction unit condenser heat effect
is cooled away to the ambient, Scenario 8 utilizes this heat energy with the same general
configuration as for Scenario 7. Scenario 8 may thus be described as a scenario with full heat
recovery.

3.3 Thermoflex Modelling of The ARC WtE Facility without Carbon Capture
26
3.3
Thermoflex Modelling of The ARC WtE Facility without Carbon
Capture
Before any investigations or calculations can be performed with regards to carbon capture
integration at ARC, two separate but nearly identical modelling-wise simulations of the ARC
WtE facility were conducted in TFX. The first model, Scenario 0, simulates the current WtE
facility with the current DH temperature levels, to demonstrate the overall accuracy of the TFX
model and validate it against plant performance data provided by ARC. The second model,
Scenario 1, also simulates the ARC WtE facility as-built but utilizes future DH temperature
levels, to establish a reference point against which to compare the respective scenarios for the
integration of CO2 capture at ARC (Scenario 2 to 8).
3.3.1
Thermoflex Modelling of Scenario 0
Thermoflex (TFX) is a component-based thermodynamic modelling software with a graphic
user interface, that allows the creation of thermal energy systems by selecting different built-in
components and connecting them to properly model the desired system.
The Rambøll Energy-from-Waste department was heavily involved in the design and construc-
tion of the ARC WtE facility. As such, a thermodynamic model of the plant was developed in
TFX during the design phase of the project. During the tendering and construction processes,
the TFX model was continually updated according to developments in the overall design of
the facility to be able to evaluate the overall performance of the plant. Thus the present ther-
modynamic model models the as-built WtE facility. An overview of the TFX model may be
seen in fig. 3.6. A full overview of the TFX model for the ARC WtE facility may be found in
appendix A.2.
An in-depth description of the furnace and boiler arrangement in the TFX model, shown on the
left of fig. 3.6, will not be provided in this thesis. The reasoning for this is that the modeled
boiler arrangement represents an inherited complexity that allows for accurate modelling of
the ARC facility in a variety of different operating points. Since this project considers only
nominal operation of the ARC facility, the advanced boiler modelling does not significantly
influence the results.
As it may be seen from fig. 3.6, the boiler arrangement has four couplings to water/steam
loop and flue gas scrubbing and condensation parts. The flue gas coming from the medium
temperature economizer, shown as a red vertical line in in the middle of the figure, is connected
to a water scrubber unit that simulates the acidic and caustic scrubbers, after which the flue
gas goes through the two flue gas condensation units and then to the stack. In the top middle
of fig. 3.6, the high-pressure steam from the boiler enters the turbine arrangement. Before the
first turbine stage, two flow splitters are implemented to simulate the turbine bypass to either
the CC-system or DH3. The final two couplings are bleed steam going to the LUFOs, shown on
the bottom left of fig. 3.6. It should be noted, that since ARC consists of two identical boiler
lines with a common turbine and turbine condenser arrangement, only one boiler line as well
as one flue gas path and one turbine are shown in fig. 3.6. Two boiler lines may still accurately
be modelled with this arrangement, since the TFX model allows for the implementation of flow
multipliers and flow dividers. Thus, where relevant, streams are doubled or divided by two to
properly simulate the actual facility at ARC.
==PAGE:45==
3.3 Thermoflex Modelling of The ARC WtE Facility without Carbon Capture
27
Figure 3.6: Overview of the Thermoflex model for the ARC WtE facility without carbon capture.
==PAGE:46==
3.3 Thermoflex Modelling of The ARC WtE Facility without Carbon Capture
28
As previously stated, the ARC boiler plant was designed for a MSW LHV of 11.5 MJ/kg,
which is also the input calorific value in the inherited TFX model. To properly model the
actual operating conditions of the plant, the input LHV is instead defined as 9.35 MJ/kg 6 and
a control loop is implemented in the TFX model where the mass flow rate of the MSW fuel is
varied until the nominal steam production of 38.8 kg/s per boiler line is reached. The result
of the control loop is that a mass flow rate of 12.05 kg/s MSW corresponding to 43.38 tons/hr
for each boiler line is required to raise the nominal steam flow. The MSW tonnage per line is
thus very close to the value provided by ARC as detailed previously.
The current ARC WtE facility is modelled with a DH heat delivery to HOFOR of 81.4 MJ/s,
while the CTR network will receive the remaining energy from the turbine condensers and
the bypass flow.
The HOFOR delivery of 81.4 MJ/s is a design requirement specified by
ARC and represents the nominal heat production that ARC supplies to the HOFOR DH.
The principal motivation for defining the HOFOR delivery to the maximum possible value is
that the implementation of carbon capture with waste heat being recovered as heat energy is
expected to increase the overall district heating production significantly. The heat from the
carbon capture unit shall be integrated with the CTR bypass flow, since the capacity of the
CTR network is significantly higher than the HOFOR network7.
A detailed view of the turbine condenser section of the TFX mode may be seen in fig. 3.7. The
condenser arrangement is modelled with the same overall configuration as detailed in fig. 3.3,
with the two district heating networks modelled as two separate turbine condensers. The three
turbine condensers shown on the top of fig. 3.7 contain the HOFOR DH flow, where the DH
flow at the inlet of DH1 has been previously heated in the direct flue gas condensation stage,
FGC1. Through the three turbine condensers, the HOFOR DH flow is gradually heated to
the forward temperature of 85 °C at the outlet of DH3. The set point temperatures for the
outlet flows from each turbine condenser for the HOFOR condenser series is defined as 75 °C,
85 °C and 85 °C for DH1, DH2 and DH3 respectively. The heat delivery to both the HOFOR
and CTR DH networks are modelled with the built-in TFX heat remover components, shown
as yellow boxes in the lower left corner. These component allows for specification of both the
outlet temperature and the removed or added heat. For the case of the HOFOR network heat
remover, both the outlet temperature of 50 °C and the specified 81.4 MJ/s of heat removal
are defined as input. Thus with both the heat removal and forward and return temperature
specified for the HOFOR network, the mass flow rate of the HOFOR DH production is governed
by the overall heat removal in the heat remover component.
The bottom three series of turbine condensers contain the CTR DH flow, with the CTR bypass
flow being visible as a split in the DH return water flow in the bottom left of fig. 3.7. The
bypass flow is directed to the AHPs where it is heated to 85 °C before being returned between
DH2 and DH3 in node 256. Thus the mass flow rate for the CTR bypass stream is governed by
the heat effect delivered from the AHPs. The set point temperatures for the outlet flows from
each turbine condenser for the CTR condenser series is defined as 75 °C, 85 °C and 107.5 °C for
DH1, DH2 and DH3 respectively. The heat remover for the CTR network cools the CTR return
to the specified return temperature of 50 °C while the overall heat removal is governed by the
heat effect delivered from the AHPs and the three turbine condensers on the CTR stream.
The TFX model automatically distributes the heat energy between the six turbine condensers
on the two different DH networks and governs the mass flow rate of the CTR DH flow going

A detailed view of the scrubber and flue gas condensation modelling may be seen in fig. 3.8.
Flue gases from the boiler enter at the upper left corner at a temperature of 156 °C and are

After the scrubber, the flue

internal water coolant loop cools the flue gases and condenses out water vapor and transfers
the absorbed heat energy through a heat exchanger to the HOFOR DH flow.
The excess

scrubber. To control the heat removal in FGC1 and thus the outlet temperature from FGC1,
a control loop in implemented where the mass flow rate of cooling water in the internal coolant
loop is varied until the desired exit flue gas temperature of 55 °C is achieved. For FGC2, which
is cooled with the AHPs, an outlet temperature of 37 °C is specified. The outlet temperature
