3.3.2
Thermoflex Modelling Scenario 1 (Reference Scenario)
To establish a reference scenario against which scenarios equipped with CC systems may be
compared, an additional simulation of the ARC WtE facility was conducted. For the reference
scenario, district heating for the two DH networks is delivered and returned at the 2030 tem-
perature levels, as shown in table 3.1. Modelling with the future temperature levels constitutes
the principal difference from the TFX modelling of Scenario 0. To ensure consistency between
scenarios, the reference scenario is modelled with the same MSW flow as for the current plant
with a mass flow rate of MSW fuel of 12.05 kg/s per boiler line. The reference scenario will be
denoted as Scenario 1 through the remainder of this paper.
To facilitate the modelling of the future DH temperatures, the outlet temperature level from the
two heat removers shown in fig. 3.7 are specified to 45 °C, while the absolute heat rejection of
81.4 MJ/s from the HOFOR heat remover is maintained. With the heat removal is maintained,
since the temperature change from the return temperature to the forward temperature is some
5 °C higher than the current temperature levels (85-45 °C vs. 85-50 °C), the mass flow rate of
the HOFOR DH flow decreases slightly in the reference scenario. The lower return temperature
==PAGE:49==
3.4 Chemical Modelling of Amine Carbon Capture with Aspen Plus
31
and DH mass flow rate requires a reiteration of the control loop governing the mass flow rate
of the internal water coolant loop in FGC1 to ensure that the flue gas outlet temperature level
from FGC1 is maintained at 55 °C.
The mass flow rate of the CTR bypass flow going through the AHPs is kept equal to the flow
rate in Scenario 0. Since the return temperature for the CTR network in the reference scenario
is approximately 5 K lower than in the current plant (Scenario 0), maintaining the flow rate
through the AHPs implies that the outlet temperature from the AHPs of will correspondingly
also be approximately 5 K lower than for the current temperature levels. To ensure consistency,
all modeled scenarios with carbon capture integration will use the same set of return and
forward temperatures for the individual district heating networks that are used in the reference
scenario.
For all modelled scenarios, a number of definitions are made with regards to the eﬀiciencies of
the ARC WtE facility. First of, a net electrical eﬀiciency, ηel, is defined as the fraction between
the net power production and the MSW fuel input. Secondly, a separate eﬀiciency known as the
fuel utilization or the total energy eﬀiciency, ε, is defined as the fraction between the combined
useful energy production, the sum of net power production and total DH production, and the
fuel input. Here, the total heat production is the combined DH production for the CTR and
HOFOR networks.

The remainder of the methods section beyond this point will investigate the specifics of CO2
capture integration at the ARC WtE facility.
This will be done by initially providing an
overview of the supplied chemical process modelling results and then detailing the design of
a reference CO2 capture system.

methodologies for each investigated scenario will follow.
3.4
Chemical Modelling of Amine Carbon Capture with Aspen
Plus
Integrating carbon capture in the ARC WtE facility requires an evaluation of the chemical
process taking place in the absorber and stripper sections of the carbon capture system. The
chemical process modelling for the MEA carbon capture unit was performed by the Union
Engineering branch of Pentair PLC, one of the key partners in the EUDP project described in
section 2.2. The process modelling was conducted in Aspen Plus, with a modified version of an
Aspentech-developed rate-based simulation model of the CO2 capture process using aqueous
30 % Monoethanolamine (MEA) as solvent. 8
Contrary to the lower absorber inlet temperatures investigated in previous projects [31, 30],
this thesis investigates the integration of a CC-facility with an absorber inlet temperature of
55 °C.
As was described in section 3.1, the temperature at the outlet of FGC1 is exactly
55 °C, which means that the proper flue gas temperature to the inlet of the absorber may
8Information supplied by Søren Jensen from Union Engineering
==PAGE:50==
3.4 Chemical Modelling of Amine Carbon Capture with Aspen Plus
32
be achieved simply by not employing the indirect flue gas condensation stage, FGC2. The
implication of turning off FGC2 is, that the AHPs will have to derive their heat input from a
different source. It may additionally be noted, that while FGC2 may in principal be turned
off completely such that no heat energy is extracted from the flue gas flow in this stage, the
component and its subsystems such as packing material will still incur a slight pressure loss
for the flue gases when passing through FGC2. Higher absorber inlet temperatures, generally
means that the total specific heat input for the CO2 desorption process also increases [31].
However, higher absorber temperatures also means that waste heat from the absorber may be
more easily recovered as useful heat energy. The process model also differs from previous works
[31, 30] by utilizing two-stage absorber intercooling to extract the maximum amount of energy
from the exothermal absorption process at the highest possible temperature-level.
The results shown in table 3.3 to table 3.5 are the result obtained from the Aspen Plus model.
As it may be seen, table 3.3 contain results for the flow at the inlet and outlet of the absorber
as well as from the outlet of the stripper. It should be noted that the flow conditions such as
mass flow rate and molar fractions of flue gas constituents for the absorber inlet, were supplied
by ARC to Union Engineering based on actual operating data from the WtE facility. The
flue gas constituents correspond to a flue gas with a dewpoint of 55 °C at 1.01325 bar, that is
superheated by 5 K above the dew point (60 °C) through an ID fan placed before the absorber.
As it may be seen from the values determined with TFX, see fig. 3.8 and appendix A.2, the
pressure at the outlet of FGC2 before the absorber inlet is actually in a slight vacuum at 0.9667
bar. While the TFX model might be slightly off from the actual value, the pressure level at the
outlet of FGC2 is subatmospheric, and should optimally have been modelled as such. With a
flue gas of a given molar composition at high pressure, the water vapor will start to condense
out of the gas mixture at a higher temperature level than the same flue gas at lower pressure.
The high pressure gas may thus be said to have a higher dewpoint temperature even though
the overall molar composition is the same. This relation means that a high pressure gas at
1.01325 bar when cooled to 55 °C will have a considerably lower molar fraction of water than
a flue gas with equal composition cooled to 55 °C at a lower pressure level.
Since the results of the Aspen Plus simulations form the basis for all modeled scenarios with
carbon capture and were not conducted by the author, the overall results from the chemical
process modelling are detailed below. A screen dump from the Aspen Plus model may be seen
in fig. 3.9, however it might be helpful for the reader to compare the screen dump with the
stylized schematic shown in fig. 3.10. The absorber as well as the two stages of intercooling are
shown on the middle left of fig. 3.9, while the stripper is shown on the middle right. Flue gas
may be seen entering the absorber coming from the extreme left of fig. 3.9 where also the cool
lean amine enters the absorber, denoted in the figure as “LEANIN”. MEA solvent with a low
CO2 loading is generally referred to as “lean amine”, while solvent with a high CO2 loading is
referred to as “rich amine”. The two absorber intercooler stages, AIC1 and AIC2, may be seen
on the right side of the absorber module. It should be noted that the exact positioning of the
incoming stream entering the absorber as shown in fig. 3.9 is not necessarily indicative of the
actual physical placement of the stream in the absorber. The lean amine and flue gas enters
the absorber section in a counter current where the solvent begins to absorb the CO2 present
in the flue gas. The absorption process is exothermal and releases heat energy to the flue gas
and the aqueous amine solution in three ways, which are described in the following:
1. The temperature of the flue gas increases through the absorber as may be seen in table 3.3
as the difference between inlet and outlet temperatures in the absorber.
==PAGE:51==
3.4 Chemical Modelling of Amine Carbon Capture with Aspen Plus
33
2. The temperature of the aqueous amine solvent increases as may be seen in table 3.4 as
the temperature change from the outlet of APC to the inlet of AIC2, the outlet of AIC2
to the inlet of AIC1, and once again from the outlet of AIC1 to the inlet of IHEX.
3. The overall molar fraction of water, H2O increases through the absorber and a small
amount of MEA is carried to the outlet of the absorber as may be seen in table 3.3. This
occurs due to evaporation of the aqueous solvent in the absorber section.
The heat energy resulting in the temperature increase of the solvent is recovered in two stages,
denoted as AIC1 and AIC2, such that amine will be twice sprayed over packing material,
recovered in trays below the packing material and cooled in the respective absorber intercooler
before being sprayed over packing material once again. After the second absorber intercooler,
the solvent is sprayed over a final section of packing material before being withdrawn from
the bottom of the absorber. It should be noted that AIC1 refers to the absorber intercooling
stage nearest the flue gas inlet at the bottom of the absorber, while AIC2 refers to the stage
nearest the outlet of the absorber. To prevent the loss of amine to the environment as well as
to maintain the water balance of the capture plant by condensing out the evaporated solvent,
a water wash section is generally implemented at the outlet of the absorber module.
The
water wash module may be seen as a barrel shaped module above the absorber in fig. 3.9.
The reclaimed water and MEA solvent as well as the coolant water exits the water wash
at the bottom of the water wash module after which it goes through a splitter that directs
the reclaimed solvent back to the absorber while the coolant water is cooled through a heat
exchanger before being readmitted to the water wash section. The clean flue gases then exit
the carbon capture system at the top of the water wash module in fig. 3.9. The rich amine
withdrawn from the bottom of the absorber is pumped through an Internal Heat Exchanger
(IHEX) where it is preheated before being admitted to the stripper. In the stripper, heat
energy imparted to the reboiler from an external steam flow causes a desorbtion or stripping
of the absorbed CO2 from the MEA solvent. The result is a hot regenerated or lean MEA
solvent that is then cooled against the incoming rich solvent to the stripper in the IHEX.
After the IHEX the still relatively hot lean amine is cooled in a solvent precooler, denoted as
APC and shown in the bottom left of fig. 3.9, before being readmitted to the absorber. Aside
from stripping the CO2 from the solvent, the heat energy imparted in the reboiler causes the
evaporation of a significant amount of water in the stripper. As a result, the flow leaving the
stripper is a gaseous mixture of CO2 and steam as may be seen in table 3.3.
Of the heat exchangers detailed in table 3.4, the lean/rich amine heat exchanger will not be
investigated in depth in this project, since this heat exchanger is internal in the process. Thus
utilizing for example the energy in the hot lean amine coming from the stripper for different
purposes will result in a cooler flow of rich amine going in to the stripper, which inevitably
must be made up with additional energy input in the reboiler. Concepts that utilize the heat
energy available in the hot lean amine stream do exist, such as Lean Vapor Compression (LVC)
or steam ejectors as described in section 2.3.3. Since an investigation of LVC and steam ejector
performance would require a wholly separate Aspen Plus simulation model, for which resources
were not allocated in this project, these systems will not be investigated through this thesis.
To quantify the overall CO2 removal eﬀiciency of the capture unit, a relatively simple calculation
was performed based on the molar fractions and flow rates at the absorber inlet and outlet as
detailed in table 3.3. At the absorber inlet and outlet, the molar mass of the gas mixture may

3.5
Definition of Reference Carbon Capture System
To ensure consistency between the modelled scenarios, a reference carbon capture system is
defined based on the modelled CO2 capture system described in section 3.4. This reference
CO2 capture system shall be used with only minor modifications for all modelled heat recovery
scenarios, that are described in section 3.2. A stylized schematic of the defined carbon capture
system may be seen in fig. 3.10.
The existing two-stage flue gas condensation system with FGC1 and FGC2 is shown in the
bottom left corner of fig. 3.10. As previously mentioned in section 3.4, FGC1 cools the flue
gases to 55 °C and FGC2 is taken out of operation to facilitate the relatively high-temperature
flue gas flow to the absorber. An ID fan is placed between FGC2 and the absorber inlet that
provides a slight pressure lift as well as delivering 5 K superheating to the flue gas flow. The
reference carbon capture system utilizes the exact same configuration of the absorber, stripper,
and process specific heat exchangers as detailed in section 3.4.
At the outlet of the absorber, a two-stage flue gas condensation unit, denoted as FGC3 and
FGC4, is implemented to recover the relatively high-temperature flow at the outlet of the
absorber that results from the exothermal absorption process. The post-capture flue gas con-
densation is highlighted by the dashed line in fig. 3.10. The main impetus for implementing
two separate flue gas condensation units is that the outlet flow conditions from the absorber
section, as may be seen in table 3.3, are relatively close to the flue gas conditions at the inlet
of FGC1 as detailed in section 3.1. With flue gas condensation units, dependent upon how
deep the flue gas is cooled, there can be a significant benefit from having more than one con-
densation stage due to the overall shape of the condensation curve, as may be seen in fig. 4.1.
Due to pinch point temperature considerations, the design temperature between FGC3 and
FGC4 is defined as 56 °C. The outlet temperature from FGC4 is defined as 37 °C, which is
==PAGE:56==
3.5 Definition of Reference Carbon Capture System
38
the same temperature as the outlet temperature from FGC2 in the reference scenario without
carbon capture. Since additionally, the 56 °C outlet temperature from FGC3 is very close to
the temperature between FGC1 and FGC2 in the reference scenario, a similar cooling layout to
the reference scenario (Scenario 1) is implemented. Thus FGC3 will be a directly cooled with
either district heating return or an air cooler water coolant loop, while FGC4 will be indirectly
cooled through a chilled circuit cooled with heat pumps.
Figure 3.10: Stylized schematic of the designed overall CO2 capture system.
At the outlet of the stripper on the right of fig. 3.10, an additional flue gas condensation unit
is shown atop the stripper. The condensation unit is called the Stripper Overhead Condenser
(SOC), and the principal purpose of this component is to condense out the majority of the
water vapor in the hot gaseous CO2 and steam mixture leaving the stripper ahead of the CO2
compression train. Between the SOC and the compression train, a Carbon Dioxide Precooler
condenses out additional water vapor and cools the gas mixture before the compression train
to reduce the compression work. The design outlet temperature from the SOC is be defined as
69 °C, governed by pinch point considerations detailed in section 4.3.1. The outlet temperature
of CPC, which for all modelled scenarios will be cooled with a heat pump cooled chilled circuit,
is defined as 37 °C. This ensures that CPC may be cooled with the same chilled circuit also
cooling FGC4.
While a compression train with three compression and intercooling stages are shown in fig. 3.10,
an investigation into the most suited compression layout will be conducted in section 3.5.2.
Through the compression train the CO2 rich gas mixture is compressed to 15 bar after which,
a liquefaction or refrigeration unit, shown in the top right of fig. 3.10, cools the CO2 to -30
°C which causes the CO2 to condense and liquefy. Ahead of the liquefaction unit, a desiccant-
==PAGE:57==
3.5 Definition of Reference Carbon Capture System
39
based H2O absorption system is implemented to prevent water vapor from freezing on the
inside of the liquefaction unit evaporator. Because of this, for all modelling purposes, the gas
flow entering the liquefaction unit evaporator will be considered as pure CO2. Additionally, it
should be noted that while a relatively basic single stage liquefaction unit is shown in fig. 3.10,
the actual configuration of the liquefaction unit will be investigated in depth in section 3.5.3.
A summary table of the design conditions for the reference carbon capture scenario may be
seen in table 3.8. In the next section a simple methodology for determining the heat transfer
from gas condensation units is presented. The reasoning for determining the heat transfer is
that this will be key in designing an optimal system for utilizing the waste heat from the CO2
capture system to the district heating networks at ARC.
Table 3.8: Summary table of the design conditions for the reference carbon capture scenario.
Parameter
Symbol
Value
Unit

3.5.1
Numerical Modelling of Gas Condensation Units in EES
With the defined design temperatures for the implemented gas condensation units in the CO2
capture system shown in table 3.8, the first step is to clarify the magnitudes of the energy
recovery that is possible for each given gas condensation stage. This work is conducted using
Engineering Equation Solver (EES) [32].
3.5.1.1
Modelling of Post-absorber Flue Gas Condensation
The thermodynamic state variables and molar fractions of the outlet flow from the absorber
are listed in table 3.3. It may be seen, that the flue gas as calculated with Aspen Plus contains
trace amounts of MEA. To simplify the calculation, this trace content of MEA in the gas is
neglected, and offset by slightly increasing the concentration of nitrogen, N2.
The modelling procedure described in the following will utilize the state point designations
shown in fig. 3.11, with state point 1 describing the outlet of the absorber before FGC3, state
point 2 is located between the FGC3 and FGC4, and state point 3 is located at the outlet
of FGC4. It should be noted that these calculations investigate only the heat transfer from
the flue gases, and thus considerations related to the internal water coolant loop in FGC3 and
FGC4 are not accounted for.
In state point 1 at the outlet of the absorber before FGC3, the molar mass of the gas mixture
may be calculated from the molar fractions detailed in table 3.3 and the molar weights of the
==PAGE:58==
3.5 Definition of Reference Carbon Capture System
40
Figure 3.11: Stylized schematic of the two post-capture flue gas condensation units, FGC3
and FGC4.
individual flue gas components detailed in table 3.6.
MWmix,1 = xH2O,1 · MWH2O + xCO2,1 · MWCO2 + xO2,1 · MWO2 + xN2,1 · MWN2
(3.8)
With the molar weight of the gas mixture in state point 1 established, the mass fractions of
the individual flue gas constituents may be calculated.
yCO2,1 = xO2,1 · MWCO2
MWmix,1
yH2O,1 = xH2O,1 · MWH2O
MWmix,1
(3.9)
yN2,1 = xN2,1 · MWN2
MWmix,1
yO2,1 = xO2,1 · MWO2
MWmix,1
(3.10)
Based on the mass fractions, the mass flow rate of the individual flue gas constituents may be
calculated. It should be noted that the total mass flow rate in state point 1 is equal to the
mass flow rate from the absorber as described in table 3.3, such that ˙m1 = ˙mABS,o.
˙mCO2,1 = yO2,1 · ˙m1
˙mH2O,1 = yH2O,1 · ˙m1
(3.11)
˙mN2,1 = yN2,1 · ˙m1
˙mO2,1 = yO2,1 · ˙m1
(3.12)
Daltons law specifies that the partial pressure of a component in a gas mixture may be cal-
culated with the molar fraction of the gas multiplied by the total pressure exerted by the gas
mixture. Accordingly, the partial pressure of each individual flue gas constituent in state point
1 may be calculated, as detailed below.
pCO2,1 = xCO2,1 · p1
pH2O,1 = xH2O,1 · p1
(3.13)
pN2,1 = xN2,1 · p1
pO2,1 = xO2,1 · p1
(3.14)
From the partial pressure of the water vapor, the dewpoint or saturation temperature for the
flue gas is determined with EES. This value may be used to determine if the gas is at the
dewpoint or is superheated. As it can be seen from eq. (3.15), the flue gas at state point 1 is
slightly superheated by approximately 0.2 °C.
Tdewpoint,1 = f(pH2O) = 63.39 °C
(3.15)
==PAGE:59==
3.5 Definition of Reference Carbon Capture System
41
Based on the partial gas pressures of each flue gas constituent and the temperature given, the
enthalpy and entropy of each flue gas constituent may be calculated with the thermodynamic
library in EES.
hCO2,1 = f(T1; pCO2,1)
hH2O,1 = f(T1; pH2O,1)
(3.16)
hN2,1 = f(T, 1; pN2,1)
hO2,1 = f(T1; pO2,1)
(3.17)
sCO2,1 = f(T1; pCO2,1)
sH2O,1 = f(T1; pH2O,1)
(3.18)
sN2,1 = f(T1; pN2,1)
sO2,1 = f(T1; pO2,1)
(3.19)
Assuming that the gas mixture is ideal, the overall enthalpy and entropy in state point 1 may be
determined. It should be noted, that since the reference value of the individual thermodynamic
libraries for the flue gas constituents may be different, the absolute value of the mixture enthalpy
and mixture entropy is not specifically relevant. Only in relation to the mixture enthalpy and
entropy in other state points does this value become useful.
hmix,1 = yCO2,1 · hCO2,1 + yH2,1 · hH2O,1 + yO2,1 · hO2,1 + yN2,1 · hN2,1
(3.20)
smix,1 = yCO2,1 · sCO2,1 + yH2,1 · sH2O,1 + yO2,1 · sO2,1 + yN2,1 · sN2,1
(3.21)
As heat is transferred in FGC3, the flue gas is initially desuperheated to the dewpoint after
which water vapor condenses out of the flue gas. Throughout the condensation process, the
gas remains saturated with water vapor with a partial pressure corresponding with the given
temperature of the flue gas. Thus the flue gases leave FGC3 in state point 2 as a water vapor
saturated gas mixture with a dewpoint of 56 °C. The same applies to the flue gases leaving
FGC4 in state point 3 with a dewpoint of 37 °C.
The same general procedure is used to determine the mixture enthalpy and entropy in state
point 2 and state point 3 and will be described in the following.
The pressure losses through both FGC3 and FGC4 are assumed to be negligible, and the partial
gas pressure of water vapor is equal to the saturation pressure for the given flue gas temperature
in state point n. The saturation pressure of the two state points n = 2, 3 is evaluated with
EES.
pn = p1
pH2O,n = psat,n = f(Tn)
(3.22)
Thus, with the partial gas pressure of water vapor determined, by applying Daltons law as
described in eq. (3.89), the molar fraction of water vapor may be determined.
xH2O,n = pH2O,n
pn
(3.23)
As the molar fraction and consequently the partial pressure of water vapor decreases through
the flue gas condensation unit, the molar fractions and partial pressures of the other gases
present are assumed to increase linearly to maintain the same total pressure. This is imple-
mented with a factor denoted as, Rc, which is implemented as shown in the equation below.
pCO2,n = xCO2,n · pn = pCO2,1 · Rc
(3.24)
pO2,n = xO2,n · pn = pO2,1 · Rc
(3.25)
pN2,n = xN2,n · pn = pN2,1 · Rc
(3.26)
==PAGE:60==
3.5 Definition of Reference Carbon Capture System
42
A final equation is required determine the partial gas pressures and molar fractions of each flue
gas constituent. This equation is implemented as shown below.
pn = pCO2,1 · Rc + pO2,1 · Rc + pN2,1 · Rc + pH2O,1 · Rc
(3.27)
The molar weight as well as the mass fractions of the individual flue gas components are
evaluated using the same general procedure applied in eq. (3.8) and eq. (3.10).
Since only water condenses out of the flue gas, due to all other flue gas constituents being far
from their respective saturation temperatures, the mass flow rates of N2, O2, and CO2 are
assumed constant through the different state points, such that the following applies.
˙mCO2,n = ˙mCO2,1
˙mO2,n = ˙mO2,1
(3.28)
˙mN2,n = ˙mN2,1
˙mH2O,n = yH2O,n · ˙mn
(3.29)
An additional equation is required to determine the total mass flow rate and the mass flow rate
of water vapor at state point n. This additional equation is implemented as shown below.
˙mn = ˙mCO2,n + ˙mO2,n + ˙mN2,n + ˙mH2O,n
(3.30)
The enthalpies and entropies of the individual flue gas constituents and the total mixture
enthalpy and entropy at state point n is determined with the same methodology described in
eq. (3.17) and eq. (3.21).
With the mixture enthalpies and mass flow rates in each state point through FGC3 and FGC4
determined, the heat transfer in each condensation unit may be determined with the following
expression.
˙QFGC3 = ˙m1 · hmix,1 −˙m2 · hmix,2
(3.31)
˙QFGC4 = ˙m2 · hmix,2 −˙m3 · hmix,3
(3.32)
Additionally, the mass flow of condensed water in each stage may be determined.
˙mFGC3 = ˙m1 −˙m2
(3.33)
˙mFGC4 = ˙m2 −˙m3
(3.34)
3.5.1.2
Modelling of Stripper Overhead Condenser and Carbon Dioxide Precooler
As it may be seen from table 3.3, the gas mixture at the stripper outlet contains trace amounts
of N2, O2 and MEA. These trace amounts will be neglected through the calculations for SOC
and CPC, which requires the molar fraction of water vapor to be increased slightly for balancing
purposes.
The condensation of water vapor from the CO2 and steam mixture from the outlet of the strip-
per is modelled with principally the same method detailed in section 3.5.1.1. The only major
differences is that the gas mixture only consists of two gases, which simplifies the calculations
slightly. An additional difference is the higher pressure level of the gas mixture of 1.8 bar.

To gain a deeper understanding of the gas condensation process, T-Q diagrams are developed.

plotted against the heat transfer imparted to the counter-current water coolant. This is done
by initially determining the imparted heat transfer as the gas mixture reaches the dewpoint
or saturation temperature. A looping procedure is then implemented in EES with a linearly
spaced temperature interval with the boundaries defined by the inlet and outlet temperatures
of the respective condensation unit. The T-Q-diagram may be seen in fig. 4.1.

3.5.3.1
Refrigerant Investigations
A large number of commercial refrigerant are available in the market today with different
thermodynamic and chemical properties that make them more or less suitable for specific ap-
plications. In this thesis seven different refrigerants of different types and with widely different
properties will be investigated for the heat pump cycle design. The refrigerants investigated
range from natural refrigerants including hydrocarbons to hydrofluoroolefins (HFOs). Relevant
thermodynamic data for each investigated refrigerant may be seen in table 3.11. The thermo-
dynamic properties of a given refrigerant is of great importance for refrigerant selection. In
particular the critical temperature is a defining factor for how high a forward temperature may
be delivered from the heat pump system.

Apart from thermodynamic properties of a given refrigerant, properties relating to the en-

As an
example while ammonia (R717) has very good performance in relation to the Ozone Depletion
Potential (ODP) and Global Warming potential (GWP) of the refrigerant it is also toxic and
mildly flammable which can preclude it from use in certain applications. Similarly hydrocar-
bon refrigerants such as propane (R290), isopentane (R601) and, isobutane (R600a) are all
highly flammable. Information related to the safety properties as well as the environmental

For the modelling of the liquefaction unit only ammonia (R717) and propane (R290) are con-

unit is designed to cool the flow of CO2 to -30 °C, utilizing ammonia and propane means that
it is possible to operate the liquefaction unit evaporator at near atmospheric pressure, which
can help minimize or eliminate air ingress through leakages in the low-pressure section of the
refrigeration cycle.
3.5.3.2
Modelling of Single-stage Liquefaction Unit
A simple way to provide the required liquefaction of the stream of compressed CO2 is with
a single stage vapor compression heat pump (VCHP). A concept diagram for a generic single
stage refrigeration unit may be seen in fig. 3.13. It should be noted that state point designations
in the following description of the applied thermodynamic modelling methodology will adhere
to the numbering shown in fig. 3.13.
State Point 1 - Before Compression With a defined degree of superheating out of the
evaporator, the temperature into the compressor may be calculated. It should be noted that
==PAGE:64==
3.5 Definition of Reference Carbon Capture System
46
Figure 3.13: Concept diagram of the single stage refrigeration unit.
this calculation assumes negligible heat losses in the suction line.
T1 = Te + ∆TSH
p1 = pe
(3.35)
Then for heat pump designs where the superheating is 0 K, the enthalpy and entropy of is
evaluated as a saturated vapor with the built-in thermodynamic library in EES [32].
h1 = f(x1, p1)
s1 = f(x1, p1)
(3.36)
For all heat pump cycles with superheating higher than 0 K, the outlet state of the evaporator
before compression is evaluated as superheated vapor from the temperature and pressures as
detailed below.
h1 = f(T1, p1)
s1 = f(T1, p1)
(3.37)
Additionally, since this is an important consideration for heat pump compressor selection, the
volumetric flow rate of the refrigerant is evaluated with the following expression:
˙V1 = ˙mREF · νREF,1
ν = f(h1, s1)
(3.38)
State Point 2 - Before Condensation At the outlet of the compressor before the con-
denser, the pressure is equal to the condensation pressure, pe, of the refrigerant. Assuming
isentropic compression, the entropy at the outlet of the compressor is equal to the entropy at
the compressor inlet.
p2 = pc
s2,s = s1
(3.39)
The enthalpy under isentropic compression may then be evaluated from the pressure and en-
tropy in state point 2.
h2,s = f(s2,s, p2)
(3.40)
By applying the assumed isentropic eﬀiciency and heat loss factor detailed in table 3.10, the
enthalpy at the compressor outlet under adiabatic compression, h2w, and the actual enthalpy
at the compressor outlet, h2, may be determined.
ηs =
˙Wideal
˙Wreal
= h2s −h1
h2w −h1
(3.41)
fq =
˙Qloss
˙Wcomp
= h2w −h2
h2w −h1
(3.42)
==PAGE:65==
3.5 Definition of Reference Carbon Capture System
47
With the actual enthalpy determined, the actual entropy and temperature after compression
may be determined with EES [32].
s2 = f(h2, p2)
T2 = f(h2, p2)
(3.43)
The power requirement for the compression process may then be determined with the following
expression:
˙Wcomp = ˙mREF · (h2w −h1)
(3.44)
State Point 3 - Before Expansion Valve With the degree of subcooling out of the condenser
defined, the temperature before the expansion valve may be calculated. It is assumed that there
are negligible pressure losses in the condenser, and the pressure before the expansion valve is
equal to the condensation pressure, pc, of the refrigerant.
T3 = Tc + ∆TSC
p3 = pc = p2
(3.45)
Then with both pressure and temperature defined, the enthalpy and entropy at the outlet of
the condenser may be determined with EES [32].
h3 = f(T3, p3)
s3 = f(T3, p3)
(3.46)
With the enthalpy evaluated, the heat effect produced in the condenser may be calculated from
the mass flow rate of the refrigerant and the enthalpy difference across the condenser.
˙Qc = ˙mREF · (h2 −h3)
(3.47)
State Point 4 - Before Evaporator Since the evaporator is modelled without pressure losses,
the pressure at the inlet of the evaporator after the expansion valve is equal to the evaporator
pressure. The expansion valve is modelled as isenthalpic, and the enthalpy difference across
the valve is thus zero.
p4 = p1
h4 = h3
(3.48)
From the pressure and enthalpy at the outlet of the expansion valve, the temperature and
entropy of the refrigerant may be evalutated with the built-in thermodynamic library in EES
[32].
T4 = f(h4, p4)
s4 = f(h4, p4)
(3.49)
Similarly to how the heating effect of the condenser was calculated, the heat effect of the
evaporator may be calculated from the refrigerant mass flow and the enthalpy difference cross
the evaporator.
˙Qe = ˙mREF · (h1 −h4)
(3.50)
Pinch Point Temperature Definition The thermodynamic model is implemented in EES
such that the minimum pinch point temperature in both the evaporator and the condenser
are 5 K. Two generic T-Q-diagrams for a counter-current evaporator and a counter-current
condenser may be seen in fig. 3.14. As may be seen in fig. 3.14a, for the condenser, the pinch
point may occur in three different locations in the diagram: (1) at the outlet of the refrigerant
flow and inlet of the sink flow at 0 MJ/s, (2) at the point where the refrigerant is saturated
==PAGE:66==
3.5 Definition of Reference Carbon Capture System
48
vapor at 15 MJ/s, and (3) at the inlet of the refrigerant flow, where the refrigerant is in the
superheated region at 20 MJ/s. Similarly, as is visualized in fig. 3.14b, for the evaporator, the
pinch point may occur in two separate locations: (1) at the inlet of the refrigerant flow and
outlet of the source flow at 0 MJ/s, and (2) at the outlet of the refrigerant flow, where the
refrigerant is shown with superheating larger than 0 K. A refrigerant flow with no superheating
is shown as a dashed line.
0

Figure 3.14: Generic T-Q-diagrams for condensers and evaporators.
Implementing the pinch temperature definition described above, initially requires a determina-
tion of the temperature of the sink flow at the point where the refrigerant is exactly saturated
vapor, shown in fig. 3.14a at 15 MJ/s. Initially, the temperature of the refrigerant, which is
equal to the saturation temperature at the given condenser pressure, as well as the enthalpy at
the saturated vapor point are determined with EES. Properties related to the saturated vapor
point in the condenser are denoted as SV.
TREF,SV = f(pc)
hREF,SV = f(pc; x = 1)
(3.51)
Then the enthalpy of the sink flow at the SV point may be determined from the following
energy balance.
˙mREF · (h4 −hREF,SV ) = ˙msink · (hsink,o −hsink,SV )
(3.52)
From the enthalpy, since the sink medium is coolant water in the internal air cooler circuit,
the temperature may be determined with the built-in thermodynamic library in EES.
Tsink,SV = f(psink; hsink,SV )
(3.53)
Then finally the following equations may be defined for the pinch point temperature in the
condenser.
∆Tpp = min(TREF,SV −Tsink,SV ; T3 −Tsink,i; T2 −Tsink,o)
(3.54)
A similar expression may additionally be defined for the evaporator pinch point temperature.
∆Tpp = min(Tsource,i −T1; Tsource,out −T4)
(3.55)
==PAGE:67==
3.5 Definition of Reference Carbon Capture System
49
Overall System Performance To evaluate the overall performance of a refrigeration unit or
heat pump cycle, the Coeﬀicient of Performance (COP) is often used. The COP is classically
defined as the ratio between useful cooling and/or heating effect and the required compression
power.
COP =
˙Quseful
Wcomp
(3.56)
This means that the definitions of COP are different for a refrigeration than for a heat pump
cycle.
For a refrigeration cycle where the source cooling effect is the useful product, the
evaporator cooling effect, ˙Qe, is used as the numerator in the COP calculation. Conversely,
for a heat pump cycle, the delivered heat effect to the heat sink in the condenser is the useful
product. The heating COP is not strictly relevant for refrigeration systems unless the condenser
heat is utilized. However, the definition is introduced in this section regardless.
COPcool =
˙Qe
˙Wcomp
COPheat =
˙Qc
˙Wcomp
(3.57)
Similarly, the temperature lift of a given refrigeration or heat pump cycle can be useful to
quantify the difference between the heat source and sink. The definition of temperature lift
from [35] is used.
Tlift = Tsink,o −Tsource,o
(3.58)
Optimization of Subcooling and Superheating The designed refrigeration cycle for the
given refrigerants is optimized through a parametric variation study where initially the amount
of subcooling, ∆TSC, is varied to determine the value of subcooling that delivers the highest
possible COP. With the optimal subcooling defined, the optimal degree of superheating, ∆TSH,
is determined with a similar methodology.
3.5.3.3
Modelling of Two-stage Liqufaction Unit with Open Intercooler
Since the temperature-lift delivered by the liquefaction unit is 65 °C from the source outlet
temperature of -30 °C (liquefied CO2) to the sink outlet temperature of 35 °C (coolant flow to
air cooler), there can be significant performance improvements from implementing multi-stage
compression. To quantify the performance improvement potential, a two-stage refrigeration
cycle with an Open Intercooler (OIC) is modelled. A concept diagram for a two-stage refrig-
eration unit with an open intercooler is shown in fig. 3.15. The principal differences are the
additions of a secondary compressor, an OIC to separate the two compressors, and a second
throttle valve. Additionally, a receiver is shown at the outlet of the condenser in the lower
right of the figure. The receiver is not included in the numerical modelling nor does it normally
influence a refrigeration or heat pump system in nominal point operation. Instead the receiver
serves as a buffer tank for refrigerant when the heat pump operates in off-design.
The modelling of the two-stage liquefaction unit with OIC generally follows the same method-
ology detailed in section 3.5.3.2 for the single-stage liquefaction unit. There are a number of
implemented changes though, and these are described in the following.
==PAGE:68==
3.5 Definition of Reference Carbon Capture System
50
Figure 3.15: Concept diagram of the two-stage refrigeration unit with OIC.
The two compressors will be designed with equal pressure ratios defined as a function of ratio
between condenser and evaporator pressures.
Rp =
(pc
pe
)1/2
(3.59)
This means that the intermediate pressure in the OIC including state points 2, 3, 6, and 7 may
be calculated as.
pint = p2 = p3 = p6 = p7 = p1 · Rp
(3.60)
Additionally, for the OIC which acts in essence as a separation tank with direct heat exchange,
the following is assumed:
1. The flow exiting the OIC at state point 3 towards the high-pressure compressor is satu-
rated vapor, such that x3 = 1.
2. The flow exiting the OIC at state point 7 towards the throttle valve and evaporator is
saturated liquid, such that x7 = 0.
Finally, an energy balance over the intercooler is implemented for the OIC.
˙mREF,L · h2 + mREF,H · h6 = mREF,L · h7 + mREF,H · h3
(3.61)
Where ˙mREF,L denotes the mass flow rate in the low pressure side of the liquefaction unit, and
˙mREF,H denotes mass flow rate in high pressure side. It should be noted that since the mass
flow rates differ in the low- and high-pressure sides of the liquefaction unit, the compressor
power for the individual compressors as well as heat and cooling effect in the condenser and
evaporator are calculated with different refrigerant mass flows as shown below.
˙Qe = ˙mREF,L · (h1 −h8)
˙Qc = ˙mREF,H · (h4 −h5)
(3.62)
˙Wcomp,1 = ˙mREF,L · (h2 −h1)
˙Wcomp,2 = ˙mREF,H · (h5 −h3)
(3.63)

3.6
Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery
Scenario 2
With the designed CO2 capture system and the additional gas condensation units and CO2
compression and liquefaction unit defined in section 3.5, the modelling of the described waste
heat recovery scenarios may begin. As was explained in section 3.2, in Scenario 2, the AHPs
are moved to FGC4 and CPC, since FGC2 is turned off to accommodate the high absorber inlet
temperature. Thus the general arrangement of the DH connections are as shown in fig. 3.16
Figure 3.16: Schematic overview of the DH connection in Scenario 2.
Additionally, the heat from the absorber intercoolers, AIC1 and AIC2, the amine precooler,
APC, the stripper overhead condenser, SOC, and the direct post-capture flue gas condensation,
FGC3, are cooled away to the ambient with air coolers with the arrangement shown in fig. 3.17.
As it may be seen from the figure, three separate air coolers are implemented. AC1 as shown on
the left delivers the compressor intercooling, while AC2 in the middle cools away the condenser
effect from the liquefaction unit. Finally, AC3 on the right cools away the heat energy from
SOC, APC, AIC1, AIC2 and, FGC3.
3.6.1
Design of a Carbon Capture Module in Thermoflex
While TFX has a built-in absorption-type carbon capture component that might have been ap-
plied for the integration modelling, it has multiple limitations that prevent it from accurately
modelling the advanced absorber configuration with two-stage absorber intercooling investi-
gated in this project.
==PAGE:70==
3.6 Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 2
52
Figure 3.17: Schematic overview of the air cooler arrangement in Scenario 2.
To properly simulate the Aspen Plus modelling results detailed in section 3.4, a flexible solution
consisting of a variety of different components was developed. This module is shown in fig. 3.18
and is described in the following. The absorber module is shown in the top part of the figure,
while the stripper module with the reboiler is shown on the bottom. It should be noted that
the designed module is not capable of modelling the actual chemical processes taking place in
the absorber and stripper and is thus wholly reliant on the modelling results from Aspen Plus
detailed in section 3.4. Instead the CO2 capture (CC) module ensures that the overall gas
streams stemming from the absorber and stripper as well as the heat flows from the internal
heat exchangers and the reboiler duty are consistent with the results of the Aspen Plus process
models shown in table 3.3 to table 3.5.
Flue gas coming from the flue gas condensation units, FGC1 and FGC2, enters the CC module
at the top left corner of the absorber module in fig. 3.18 where the pressure is slightly increased
and superheated to 60 °C in an ID fan. The CO2 removal from the flue gas flow is achieved
with a built-in TFX CO2 capture component shown as a grey barrel-shaped component in the
middle left of the absorber module. The CO2 capture eﬀiciency is defined as 91.26 % based
on the calculated CO2 removal rate shown in table 3.7. The TFX CO2 capture component
only works for fuel-type streams, as it was originally designed to model natural gas sweetening
where CO2 is removed from natural gas fuels. To be able to utilize the TFX CO2 capture
component, a gas-to-fuel fluid converter is implemented on the left side of the CO2 capture
component while a fuel-to-gas converter is implemented on the right. The fluid converters
only change the classification of the streams and do not not change the chemical compositions
of the flue gas. After the final fuel-to-gas converter following the CO2 capture component, a
heat adder increases the temperature of the flow ahead of an evaporative cooler that cools the
flue gas by injecting and evaporating water in the gas flow. To achieve the correct dewpoint
of the gas leaving the absorber and consequently the correct molar fraction, a control loop
is implemented that governs the temperature after the heat adder and the mass flow rate of
injected water in the evaporative cooler. Finally a separate heat adder slightly superheats the
flue gas to the correct absorber outlet temperature as detailed in table 3.3.
==PAGE:71==
3.6 Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 2
53
Figure 3.18: The developed Thermoflex carbon capture module.
Three separate HEXs representing the absorber intercooler, AIC1 and AIC2, and the amine
precooler, APC, are also shown in the absorber module in fig. 3.18.
Since TFX does not
have integrated fluid properties for an aqueous 30 % MEA solution, the hot side of the heat
exchangers, AIC1, AIC2, and, APC is modelled as water. However, since water does not have
the same specific heat capacity as the 30 % MEA solution, the Aspen Plus determined hot side
flow from table 3.4, does not yield the correct heat transfer in heat exchangers. Instead the
hot side mass flow rate is fitted to achieve the correct heat transfer with water as the fluid.
From the CO2 capture module in the absorber, the removed CO2 flow is directed towards the
stripper module in the bottom of fig. 3.18. A compressor is implemented between the absorber
and the stripper module in the CO2 path to raise the pressure to the 1.8 bar stripper pressure.
In the top left corner of the stripper module in fig. 3.18, a steam source originates, with a mass
flow rate corresponding to the absolute steam flow at the outlet of the stripper. Since TFX
does not allow the mixing of different fluid type, ie. gas/air, water/steam, refrigerants and fuel,
the steam flow is converted to a gas-type fluid using a water-to-gas converter. As with the fluid
converters in the absorber module, this does not alter the chemistry, temperature or pressure of
the stream, but solely changes the classification of the fluid flow. After the converter, the steam
flow is mixed with high-temperature CO2, following which a heat remover is implemented to
cool the CO2 and steam mixture to the desired stripper outlet temperature.
==PAGE:72==
3.6 Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 2
54
At the bottom the stripper module in fig. 3.18, the reboiler arrangement is shown. Reboiler
steam is sourced from the live steam bypass and extracation point E1 with priority given to
using extraction steam (see also fig. 3.2). Bypass and extraction steam enter the reboiler stream
at right of the stripper module where it is throttled to a pressure of 2.703 bar corresponding to a
saturation temperature of 130 °C. This pressure level is chosen to have an approach temperature
of approximately 10 K in the reboiler. The throttled steam then enters the reboiler which is
modelled as a heat remover with a heat rejection of 74.6 MJ/s, as specified in table 3.5. The
steam flow exits the heat remover as saturated liquid, known as reboiler condensate, after which
it is pumped to the dearator as is shown in fig. 3.5.
3.6.2
Integration of Gas Condensation Units in Thermoflex
The basic methodology for the integration of the post-capture gas condensation units in TFX
follows the procedure detailed for Scenario 0 in section 3.3.1.
For each gas-to-water heat
exchanger an internal water coolant loop is implemented, in which the mass flow rate governs
the terminal temperature of the water coolant at the outlet of the heat exchanger. FGC3 and
FGC4 may be seen modelled in this way in fig. 3.19. It should be noted that though SOC is
shown as a gas-to-water heat exchanger in fig. 3.10, both SOC and CPC have been implemented
as simple shell-and-tube type heat exchangers with condensate drainage as is shown in fig. 3.19.
The condensate drainages are modelled as moisture separators that remove liquid water from
the flow. The outlet temperatures of FGC3, FGC4, SOC, and, CPC are defined according to
the design values detailed in table 3.8.
Figure 3.19: Detailed overview showing the implementation of the post-capture gas conden-
sation unit in Thermoflex.
Though it is not directly shown in fig. 3.19, at the outlet of the gas-to-water heat exchanger in
FGC4 the flue gas is directed to the stack, while at the outlet of the moisture separator after
==PAGE:73==
3.6 Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 2
55
CPC, the cooled CO2 and water vapor are directed towards the compression train. The chilled
circuit that is cooled with the AHPs, and in-turn is cooling both FGC4 and CPC may be seen
on the lower right in fig. 3.19.
3.6.3
Integration of Compression Train in Thermoflex
With the integration of the gas condensation units completed for FGC3, FGC4, SOC and, CPC,
the compression train may be integrated in the TFX model for Scenario 2. With reference to the
results of the numerical modelling of the compression train in EES in appendix D, a three-stage
compression and intercooling arrangement is chosen. The compression train is implemented
as shown in fig. 3.20. The cooled CO2 and water vapor enters the the first compressor, C1,
in the top left of figure fig. 3.20. The compression ratio is the same for each of the three
compressors and may calculated with eq. (D.2) to deliver compressed CO2 and water vapor
at 15 bar at the outlet of the compression train. In each intercooler, the gasses are cooled to
35 °C and condensed water vapor is removed with a moisture separator. On the coolant side of
the intercoolers, water enters at 30 °C and leaves the intercooler at 35 °C. The water coolant
is then in-turn cooled with an air cooler. While the flow paths of the coolant water in fig. 3.20
looks relatively complex, it follows the exact same pattern as the stylized schematic shown in
fig. 3.17.
Figure 3.20: Detailed overview showing the implementation of the compression train in Ther-
moflex.
3.6.4
Integration of Liquefaction Unit in Thermoflex
From the compression train, the compressed CO2 and water vapor enters a dessicant absorption
unit that removes the remaining water vapor in the gas mixture, and sends pure CO2 to the
liquefaction unit, where it is cooled, condensed and slightly subcooled to -30 °C. Based on
the results of the numerical modelling of the liquefaction unit in EES, which may be seen in
section 4.1.2, a two-stage refrigeration unit with OIC using ammonia (R717) as a refrigerant
is chosen for the integrations in the TFX model.
The liquefaction unit is implemented as
shown in fig. 3.21, with the desiccant water absorption implemented as a gas-to-refrigerant
fluid type converter. Contrarily to the fluid type converters implemented earlier in this paper,
the gas-to-refrigerant fluid converter only accepts CO2 to pass through it, while the water vapor
is rejected as shown in fig. 3.21. The liquefaction unit is implemented with the evaporation
and condensation pressures determined through the EES modelling with superheating and
==PAGE:74==
3.7 Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 3
56
subcooling temperatures found through the optimization efforts conducted. It should be noted
that the pressure ratio of the high-pressure compressor has been increased very slightly, since
the TFX heat exchanger component does not accept two equal pinch points in the same heat
exchanger. Thus to prevent numerical problems, the condenser pressure is thus modelled as
slightly higher. While this obviously has a detrimental effect on the overall performance of the
heat pump, the very small condenser pressure increase does not decrease the COP significantly.
Figure 3.21: Detailed overview showing the implementation of the liquefaction unit in Ther-
moflex.
3.6.5
Integration of Cooling of Carbon Capture Heat Exchangers in Thermoflex
As mentioned in section 3.4, the individual heat exchangers in the designed carbon capture
system are modelled as being cooled with 45 °C DH return water. Thus for the large air cooler
that is cooling away all of the waste heat from the CC system, denoted as AC3 in fig. 3.17,
an inlet coolant temperature to the heat exchangers of 45 °C is defined. Since the air cooler
component has been shown for both the compression train and liquefaction unit integration
in the TFX model, it is deemed tedious to show the integration for the large air cooler, AC3,
since the principal integration exercise is to connect the different coolant streams to the air
cooler. A full overview of the TFX model for Scenario 2 may be seen in appendix A.3
3.7
Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery
Scenario 3
The ARC WtE plant and the integrated carbon capture system for Scenario 3 is identical to
Scenario 2 (c.f. section 3.2). The penalty on the district heating production caused by the
extraction of reboiler steam relative to the district heating production in Scenario 1 (reference
scenario), is supplied by external air-source heat pumps (ASHPs) in Scenario 3. The heating
==PAGE:75==
3.7 Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 3
57
effect to be delivered by the ASHPs is thus defined as:
˙Qtotal,Scen1 = ˙QCTR,Scen1 + ˙QHOFOR,Scen1
(3.64)
˙Qtotal,Scen2 + ˙Qtotal,Scen1 = ˙QCTR,Scen2 + ˙QHOFOR,Scen2 + ˙QASHP
(3.65)
The total heat productions for Scenario 1 and Scenario 2 and the heat delivered by the ASHPs
may be seen in table 4.12.
3.7.1

designed to deliver district heating to the CTR network. The main input parameters for the
ASHP modelling in EES may be seen in table 3.13. According to the Danish Meterological
Institute (DMI), the average air temperature in Denmark from year 2011 to 2020 was 9.1 °C
[36]. This serves as the design point heat source inlet temperature to the ASHP evaporator,
through which the ambient air is cooled by 5 K to 4.1 °C on the source side at the outlet of the
evaporator.

3.8
Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery
Scenario 4
While Scenario 4 utilizes the same general configuration of the AHPs as Scenario 2 and 3 (c.f.
section 3.2), the waste heat from the designed CC facility is coupled in parallel to the AHPs
on the CTR bypass flow in this scenario. The coupling of the heat exchangers is done in a way
that maximizes the temperature of the bypass flow where it returns before the third turbine
condenser, DH3. The design of the heat exchanger coupling is described in the following.
3.8.1
Coupling of Carbon Capture Heat Exchangers in EES
By examining the temperature levels for the different heat exchangers in the CC facility, shown
in table 3.4, as well as the different design conditions for the post-capture gas condensation
units, it my be seen that waste heat can be generally classified in four different levels; high-
temperature heat, medium high-temperature heat, medium low-temperature heat and, low-
temperature heat. The classification of each heat exchanger will be described below.
1. SOC is classified as a high-temperature heat source, with hot side temperature levels of
104.4 °C at the inlet and 69 °C at the outlet.
2. AIC1 and AIC2 are classified as medium high-temperature heat sources. The hot side
temperature levels for AIC1 are 71 °C at the inlet and 62.2 °C at the outlet, while the hot
side temperature levels for FGC3 are 71.4 °C at the inlet and 62 °C at the outlet.
3. APC and FGC3 are classified as medium low-temperature heat sources. The hot side
temperature levels for APC are 63.3 °C at the inlet and 50 °C at the outlet, while the hot
side temperature levels for FGC3 are 63.6 °C at the inlet and 56 °C at the outlet.
4. FGC4 and CPC are classified as low-temperature heat sources that require heat pump
cooling, as the outlet temperatures are too low to be cooled with district heating return
water. The hot side temperature levels for FGC3 are 56 °C at the inlet and 37 °C at the
outlet, while the hot side temperature levels for CPC are 69 °C at the inlet and 37 °C at
the outlet.
The principal reasoning for classifying CPC as a low-temperature heat source even though the
inlet temperature is actually relatively high is that the overall heat transfer in the CPC is
==PAGE:77==
3.8 Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 4
59
relatively small as well as the fact that the design point outlet temperature is much lower than
the CTR district heating return water.
As mentioned, the low-temperature heat sources are cooled by the AHPs through a chilled
circuit, denoted as Stream C4, as in Scenario 2 and 3. The other heat exchangers from the CC
facility are directly cooled with CTR DH return water at 45 °C. These heat exchangers are
coupled such that the DH return water is initially heated by the medium low-temperature heat
sources, then further heated by a medium high-temperature heat source before finally being
heated by a high-temperature heat source. For the medium low-temperature and medium high-
temperature heat sources there are two of each heat exchanger at roughly the same temperature
levels, while only one high-temperature heat source exists (SOC). Thus two parallel streams,
denoted as Stream A4 and Stream B4, are defined with only Stream A4 heated by the high-
temperature heat source (SOC). The heat exchangers are coupled as shown in fig. 3.22.
Figure 3.22: Schematic overview of the DH connection in Scenario 4.
As it may be seen in fig. 3.22, Stream A4 consists of the APC, AIC1 and the SOC coupled in
series, while Stream B4 consists of FGC3 and AIC2 also coupled in series. The primary reason
for coupling FGC3 and AIC2 together is that they are both significantly larger in magnitude
in terms of heat transfer than APC or AIC1. Thus to be able to design the DH flow through
the heat exchangers with a minimal exergy destruction, the larger heat exchangers are coupled
together.
==PAGE:78==
3.9 Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 5
60
This means that the overall transferred heat energy to the district heating return water for
Stream A4 is given as.
˙QDH,A4 = ˙QAPC + ˙QAIC1 + ˙QSOC
(3.66)
Similarly the overall heat transfer for Stream B4 may be written as.
˙QDH,B4 = ˙QFGC3 + ˙QAIC2
(3.67)
For Stream A4, since it is coupled with the high-temperature heat source from SOC, the design
point temperature of the DH flow at the outlet where the flow is mixed with the other streams
before DH3 in fig. 3.22 shall be 85 °C. This is the design inlet temperature from the absorption
heat pumps at the current WtE facility. It should be noted that a minimum pinch temperature
of 5 K is imposed in Stream A4 and thus the 85 °C outlet temperature requirement must not
violate this. Achieving the design outlet temperature is done quite simply by fitting the DH
flow through Stream A4.
˙QDH,A4 = ˙mDH,A4 · (hA4,o −hA4,i)DH
(3.68)
For Stream B4, since the hot side inlet temperature of AIC2 is only 71.4 °C it is naturally not
possible to achieve the design point outlet temperature of 85 °C. Thus the mass flow rate of
DH water through Stream B4 is governed to achieve a minimum pinch temperature of 5 K,
with the outlet temperature of Stream B4 being a resultant from this requirement.
˙QDH,B4 = ˙mDH,B4 · (hB4,o −hB4,i)DH
(3.69)
A relatively simple thermodynamic model in EES is built to be able to evaluate the designed
Stream A4 and Stream B4. It should be noted that for FGC3, the internal coolant loop is not
modelled, and thus the determined pinch relates to the temperature difference between the gas
side and the DH water flow. The results of the thermodynamic modelling in EES may be seen
in section 4.3.1
3.8.2
Thermoflex Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 4
For the TFX modelling of Scenario 4, the individual heat exchangers in the developed Carbon
Capture module described in section 3.6, are coupled in the configuration shown in fig. 3.22. The
outlet temperatures determined in section 4.3.1 are imposed to the different heat exchangers
in the developed CC module, and the mass flow is accordingly determined by the TFX model.
The entire developed TFX model for Scenario 4 may be seen in appendix A.4.
3.9
Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery
Scenario 5
Scenario 5 is modelled with a similar heat exchanger configuration as the one applied in Scenario
4 (c.f. section 3.2). However, rather than using AHPs for the heat upgrading of FGC4 and
CPC, Vapor Compression Heat Pumps (VCHPs) are used. Stream A5 in Scenario 5 will be
==PAGE:79==
3.9 Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 5
61
exactly the same as Stream A4 modelled in Scenario 4. Recalling that Stream B4 in Scenario 4
is not able to reach the design point temperature of 85 °C before DH3, the heat rejection from
the implemented VCHPs in Scenario 5 will be used to boost the temperature of Stream B5. A
schematic overview of the DH coupling in Scenario 5 is given in fig. 3.23.
Figure 3.23: Schematic overview of the DH connection in Scenario 5.
Since the performance for VCHPs is heavily dependent upon the temperature lift of the heat
pump, additional design configurations are made with regards to Stream C5, the chilled circuit
cooling FGC4 and CPC. Thus as a design condition, a 5 K approach and terminal temperature
difference is applied for the chilled circuit for both FGC4 and CPC. Since both FGC4 and CPC
have outlet temperatures of 37 °C, the inlet temperature of the chilled cicuit must be 32 °C,
while the outlet temperatures are 51 °C and 64 °C for FGC4 and CPC, respectively. This means
that the outlet temperature of the heat pump evaporator must be 32 °C. Thus for the given
temperatures and with a design pressure of 25 bar in the chilled circuit, the enthalpies at the
inlet and outlet of the chilled circuit through FGC4 and CPC may be determined with EES,
with the methodology shown below:
hsource,o = f(Tsource,o; pchilled)
(3.70)
hchilled,CPC,o = f(Tchilled,CPC,o; pchilled)
(3.71)
hchilled,FGC4,o = f(Tchilled,FGC4,o; pchilled)
(3.72)
With the results of the EES modelling of the heat transfer in FGC4 and CPC shown in
section 4.1.1, the mass flow rate through each of the two heat exchangers may be determined
==PAGE:80==
3.9 Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 5
62
with the equations below.
˙QCPC = ˙mchilled,CPC · (hchilled,CPC,o −hsource,o)
(3.73)
˙QFGC4 = ˙mchilled,FGC4 · (hchilled,FGC4,o −hsource,o)
(3.74)
A mass and energy balance for the mixing of the two parallel streams in the chilled circuit
may then be used to calculate the enthalpy at the inlet of the heat pump evaporator. This is
calculated with the following equation system.
˙mchilled,total = ˙mchilled,CPC + ˙mchilled,FGC4
(3.75)
˙mchilled,total · hsource,i = ˙mchilled,CPC · hchilled,CPC,o + ˙mchilled,FGC4 · hchilled,FGC4,o
(3.76)
With the mass flow rates calculated, the outlet mixing temperature of the of the chilled circuit
as it returns to the heat pump evaporator, may be determined with the built-in thermodynamic
libraries in EES.
Tsource,i = f(hsource,i; pchilled)
(3.77)
The results for the determined total heat transfer, mass flow rate and, temperature levels at
the inlet and outlet of the chilled circuit from the evaporator serves as design input parameters
for the numerical modelling of the different heat pump systems. These heat pump systems will
be investigated in the following.
3.9.1
Numerical Modelling of Vapor Compression Heat Pump System in EES
Multiple different heat pump configurations and refrigerants are investigated to determine the
best possible solution, with both singe-stage heat pumps, single-stage heat pumps with internal
heat exchangers and, two-stage heat pumps with OICs being modelled. The optimal heat pump
cycle and refrigerant combination is evaluated on parameters such as having a high COP and
a reasonable refrigerant mass and volume flow rate.
All the different refrigerants listed in
table 3.11 are investigated. Design boundary conditions and assumed parameters for the EES
modelling of the VCHPs are detailed in table 3.14.
The overall heat transfer delivered to the DH flow in Stream B5 is thus defined as the sum of
heat transfer contributions from the FGC3, AIC2 and the heat pump condenser.
˙QDH,B5 = ˙QFGC3 + ˙QAIC2 + ˙Qc
(3.78)
Since the inlet to FGC3 is CTR DH return water at 45 °C, and the heat pump is designed to
deliver a sink outlet temperature of 85 °C, the overall mass flow rate of DH water going through
Stream B5 is defined by the total heat transfer and the enthalpies imposed by the inlet and
outlet design temperatures on the CTR bypass flow, as expressed below.
˙QDH,B5 = ˙mDH,B5 · (hB5,o −hB5,i)DH
(3.79)
It should be noted then, that the overall heat transfer in Stream B5 is liable to change depending
on the COP of the designed heat pump cycle. This is a result of the evaporator effect being
a defined input, with the condenser heat rejection then being a product of the overall heating
COP of the designed heat pump cycle.

investigations and optimization efforts for the simple single-stage heat pump may be seen in
table 4.5.
3.9.1.2
Modelling of Single-Stage Heat Pumps with Internal Heat Exchanger in EES
Aside from modelling a simple single-stage heat pump, a single-stage heat pump with an
internal heat exchanger (IHEX) is also modelled in EES. A concept diagram for a generic
single-stage heat pump with IHEX may be seen in fig. 3.24. It should be noted that state point
designations in the following description of the applied thermodynamic modelling methodology
adhere to the numbering shown in fig. 3.24. The IHEX is commonly integrated in heat pump
and refrigeration systems to increase the heating or cooling capacity.
For the single-stage system with IHEX, the effectiveness of the IHEX was defined as:
ε =
˙QIHEX
˙QIHEX,max
(3.80)
Applying an energy balance around the IHEX yields the following expression, using the state
==PAGE:82==
3.9 Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 5
64
Figure 3.24: Concept diagram of the single stage heat pump with IHEX.
point numbering from fig. 3.24:
˙QIHEX = ˙mREF · (h3 −h4) = ˙mREF · (h1 −h6)
(3.81)
The maximum achievable heat transfer in the IHEX, ˙QIHEX,max, is defined as:
˙QIHEX,max = ˙mREF · (h3 −h4,min)
(3.82)
Where the minimum enthalpy that can be achieved through the heat exchange in the IHEX in
state point 4, h4,min, is evaluated as the enthalpy of the refrigerant with the pressure of state
point 4, p4, and the temperature of state point 6, T6.
The calculated COP of the refrigerant investigations and optimization efforts for the single-
stage heat pump with IHEX may be seen in table 4.6.

modelled refrigerants for the two-stage heat pump system with OIC are optimized for the

For the TFX modelling of Scenario 5, the individual heat exchangers in the developed Carbon

As the integration of the two-stage heat pump is very similar to the methodology applied for
the integration of the two-stage liquefaction unit, the reader is referred to section 3.6.4 for more
information. The entire developed TFX model for Scenario 5 may be seen in appendix A.5.
==PAGE:83==
3.10 Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 6
65
3.10
Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery
Scenario 6
To evaluate the performance of integrating high-temperature heat pump systems internally in
the CC facility, a Mechanical Vapor Recompression (MVR) system is investigated. A MVR
system may in principal be described as an open heat pump cycle, where a gaseous refrigerant
is directly compressed and condensed. The investigated MVR system in this paper is derived
from the system described in Jeong et. al. [28]. A process flow diagram for the Jeong-type
MVR system may be seen in fig. 3.25.
Figure 3.25: Concept diagram of the integration of the Jeong-type two-stage MVR system[28]
As it may be seen from fig. 3.25, the hot gas mixture from the stripper outlet is directly
compressed in the first compressor. After the compression, the hot high-pressure gas is then
cooled in a heat exchanger to a specific outlet temperature. This cooling process causes some
of the water vapor in the gas to condense and thus transfer energy to generate steam for the
reboiler on the cold side of the heat exchanger. After the heat exchanger, the cooled gasses
then travel through a separation tank, which removes the condensed water and directs the
saturated gas mixture to compression in the second compressor, thus repeating the process.
Since by far most of the energy in the stripper outlet flow is contained in the water vapor, the
principal purpose of the first and second compressor is to increase the partial gas pressure of
the water vapor to allow it to condense out of the gas mixture at a higher temperature level.
This higher temperature level enables the heat energy leaving the stripper to be “recycled” as
new reboiler steam. The motivation for studying the MVR solution is that substantial savings
in the steam requirement for the CO2 desorption process may be achieved. It should be noted
==PAGE:84==
3.10 Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 6
66
though that the total reboiler duty remains the same with the implementation of MVR and
thus only the actual external steam consumption from turbine bypass steam and extraction
steam is reduced.
For the MVR system studied in this paper, a set of boundary conditions is defined. These are
listed below:
1. The pressure at the outlet of the MVR-system is 15 bar, such that the MVR-system
may perform the required compression previously undertaken by the compression train.
Thus the utility of the MVR is two-fold; recovering steam for the desorption process and
replacing the compression train.
2. The steam generated by the MVR-process is saturated steam at a pressure of 2.703 bar
which corresponds to a saturation temperature of 130 °C. At the inlet of the heat ex-
changers, the water will be saturated liquid at the same 2.703 bar. Thus only evaporation
takes place on the cold side of the MVR heat exchangers.
3. The minimum pinch temperature between the high-pressure gas mixture and the gener-
ated steam for the reboiler is 5 K. This means that the hot side of the heat exchangers
are designed for an outlet temperature of 135 °C.
3.10.1
Numerical Modelling of Mechanical Vapor Recompression System in
EES
Based on the aforementioned boundary conditions and in keeping with the general compressor
modelling parameters applied through this paper, the design boundary conditions and assumed
parameters for the EES modelling of the MVR system are detailed in table 3.15.
Table 3.15: Design boundary conditions and assumed parameters for EES modelling of MVR.

°C
While Jeong et.
al.
[28] study exclusively a two-stage MVR system, this thesis broadens
the scope and investigates multiple different MVR configurations. To determine the optimal
==PAGE:85==
3.10 Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 6
67
MVR configuration for the studied Jeong-type MVR system, both single and multi-stage MVR-
systems are investigated. The methodology applied for the modelling of the MVR system is
detailed in the following.
It should be noted that the utilization of the high-temperature heat energy from the outlet of the
stripper for the MVR, naturally precludes the use of the same heat energy for district heating
production from the SOC. For this reason the DH coupling from Scenario 5 is altered slightly,
such that both streams A6 and B6 flow through the heat pump condenser to maximize the
temperature of the bypass flow before DH3. Additionally, to provide roughly similar conditions
at the inlet of the liquefaction unit, the CPC, which was placed after the SOC before the
compression train in Scenarios 2 to 5, is moved to after the MVR system. In Scenario 6, as
in the Scenarios 2 to 5, the CPC cools the water vapor and CO2 gas mixture to 37 °C. A
schematic overview of the changed DH coupling in Scenario 6 may be seen in fig. 3.26.
Figure 3.26: Schematic overview of the DH connection in Scenario 6.
3.10.1.1
Modelling of Single-stage Recompression Systems in EES
A concept diagram of the single-stage Jeong-type MVR system may be seen in fig. 3.27. It can
be observed, that only a single stage of compression and heat exchange is included. It should be
noted that state point designations in the following description of the applied thermodynamic
modelling methodology adhere to the numbering shown in fig. 3.27.
==PAGE:86==
3.10 Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 6
68
Figure 3.27: Concept diagram of the single-stage MVR system.
State Point 1 - Before Compressor At the outlet of the stripper before the compressor,
denoted as state point 1, both molar fractions, temperatures, pressures and mass flow rates
are given in table 3.15 and table 3.3. It should be noted that while the stripper outlet gas
mixture in table 3.3 does contain trace amounts of both O2, N2 and MEA, these will be
neglected through the calculations, and the molar fraction of CO2 shall be increased slightly
for balancing purposes. This means that the following will apply:
xH2O,1 ≈xH2O,REG,o
xCO2,1 ≈xCO2,REG,o
(3.83)
p1 = pREG,o
T1 = TREG,o
(3.84)
˙m1 = ˙mREG,o
(3.85)
Thus, the molar mass of the gas mixture may be calculated from the molar fractions and the
molar weights of the individual flue gas components detailed in table 3.6.
MWmix,1 = xH2O,1 · MWH2O + xCO2,1 · MWCO2
(3.86)
With the molar weight of the gas mixture in state point 1 established, the mass fractions of
the individual flue gas constituents may be calculated.
yCO2,1 = xO2,1 · MWCO2
MWmix,1
yH2O,1 = xH2O,1 · MWH2O
MWmix,1
(3.87)
Based on the mass fractions, the mass flow rate of the individual flue gas constituents may be
calculated. It should be noted that the total mass flow rate in state point 1 is equal to the
mass flow rate from the stripper as described in table 3.3, such that ˙m1 = ˙mREG,o.
˙mCO2,1 = yO2,1 · ˙m1
˙mH2O,1 = yH2O,1 · ˙m1
(3.88)
With Daltons law, the partial pressure of each individual flue gas constituent in state point 1
may be calculated, as detailed below.
pCO2,1 = xCO2,1 · p1
pH2O,1 = xH2O,1 · p1
(3.89)
==PAGE:87==
3.10 Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 6
69
Based on the partial gas pressures of each flue gas constituent and the temperature given, the
enthalpy and entropy of each flue gas constituent may be calculated with the thermodynamic
library in EES.
hCO2,1 = f(T1; pCO2,1)
hH2O,1 = f(T1; pH2O,1)
(3.90)
sCO2,1 = f(T1; pCO2,1)
sH2O,1 = f(T1; pH2O,1)
(3.91)
Assuming that the gas mixture is ideal, the overall enthalpy and entropy of the gas mixture
in state point 1 may be determined. It should be noted, that since the reference value of the
individual thermodynamic libraries for the flue gas constituents may be different, the absolute
value of the mixture enthalpy and mixture entropy is not specifically relevant. Only in relation
to the mixture enthalpy and entropy in other state points does this value become useful.
hmix,1 = yCO2,1 · hCO2,1 + yH2,1 · hH2O,1
(3.92)
smix,1 = yCO2,1 · sCO2,1 + yH2,1 · sH2O,1
(3.93)
State Point 2 - After Compressor For the single-stage MVR system the pressure ratio of
the compressor shall be defined as:
Rp,C1 = pMV R,o
p1
= 8.33
p2 = p1 · Rp,C1 = 15 bar
(3.94)
With centrifugal compressor designs it is possible to reach pressure ratios for a single compressor
stage of around 10 [37]. Thus for the given boundary conditions with a compressor inlet pressure
of 1.8 bar and the requirement for an outlet pressure of 15 bar as detailed in table 3.15, resulting
in a compression ratio of 8.33, the pressure lift may be facilitated with a single compressor stage.
Since no condensation or leakages are assumed to occur in the compressor, the mass flow rate
is unchanged.
˙m2 = ˙m1
(3.95)
Similarly, the mass flow rates of individual gas mixture constituents, mass fractions and, molar
fractions are the same in state point 1 and 2.
˙mH2O,2 = ˙mH2O,1
˙mCO2,2 = ˙mCO2,1
(3.96)
xH2O,2 = xH2O,1
xCO2,2 = xCO2,1
(3.97)
yH2O,2 = yH2O,1
yCO2,2 = yCO2,1
(3.98)
From the molar fractions and the total pressure in state point 2, the partial gas pressure of
water and CO2 may be found.
pH2O,2 = p2 · xH2O,2
pCO2,2 = p2 · xCO2,2
(3.99)
Under isentropic compression, the entropy change through the compressor is zero.
sH2O,2s = sH2O,1
sCO2,2s = sCO2,1
(3.100)
==PAGE:88==
3.10 Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 6
70
With the partial gas pressures and entropy under isentropic conditions determined, the en-
thalpies of individual gas components in state point 2 assuming isentropic compression may be
determined with EES.
hH2O,2s = f(pH2O,2; sH2O,2s)
hCO2,s,2 = f(pCO2,2; sCO2,2)
(3.101)
With the enthalpies of the gas mixture components determined, the total mixture enthalpy
assuming isentropic compression may be determined with the mass fractions in state point 2.
hmix,2s = yCO2,2 · hCO2,2s + yH2O,2 · hH2O,2s
(3.102)
The actual thermodynamic conditions after compression are determined with the same method-
ology detailed in eq. (3.42) in section 3.5.3.2. With the actual thermodynamic conditions after
compression determined, the compression work exerted on the gas may be calculated taking
into account the heat loss in the compressor.
˙WC1 = ˙m1 · (h2w −h1)
(3.103)
State Point 3 - After Heat Exchanger Inside the heat exchanger, the gas mixture is cooled
to the dewpoint, where it is saturated with water vapor that when cooled further will start to
condense out of the mixture.
By imposing the minimum pinch temperature described in table 3.15 and recalling that the
steam generation temperature is 130 °C, a design point outlet temperature on the gas side of
the heat exchanger is defined to be 135 °C. The modelling of the gas condensation in the heat
exchanger is conducted in the same way as in section 3.5.1.1. The principal differences being
that the gas mixture consists only of water vapor and CO2 and that the condensation occurs
at much higher pressures.
The total heat transfer in the heat exchanger from gas cooling and condensation of water vapor
may thus be calculated as:
˙QCondensation = ˙m2 · hmix,2 −˙m3 · hmix,2
(3.104)
However, it should be noted, that the above equation does not account for subcooling of the
water condensate in the heat exchanger. If the condensate is assumed to be cooled to 135 °C
before being drained at the bottom of the heat exchanger as seen in fig. 3.27, then the heat
from the subcooling may be determined by discretizing the condensation curve in n number of
steps. Then the enthalpy of saturated liquid (xi = 0), denoted as hSL,i for the given saturation
temperature, Ti is determined with EES:
hSL,i = f(Ti; xi)
hi,o = f(135 °C, pi)
(3.105)
Thus the heat transferred from subcooling is determined as follows:
˙QSC =
n
∑
i=1
˙mcondensate,i · (hSL,i −hi,o)
(3.106)
Thus the total heat transfer to the steam inside the heat exchanger tubes is defined as:
˙QMV R,total = ˙QCondensation + ˙QSC
(3.107)

Overall Systems Performance The COP of the system will be defined as:
COPMV R =
