3.10.1.2
Modelling of Multi-stage Recompression Systems in EES
For the numerical modelling of multi-stage MVR systems, the basic methodology is the same
as applied in section 3.10.1.1, only being implemented for each stage of the MVR system.
For a single-stage MVR system, the overall pressure ratio is defined by the requirement for
delivering 15 bar at the outlet of the MVR system. The principal purpose of the MVR com-
pressors is to increase the partial pressure of the water vapor to a temperature that allows the
condensation of water from the gas mixture at a temperature level that allows it to be used for
reboiler steam generation. Because of this, there can be a significant improvement potential
in using multi-stage systems where the overall compressor power requirement is lower due to
the cooling in the heat exchangers between the compressors. Additionally, multi-stage systems
enable the pressure ratios to be fitted to yield the highest possible COP. A concept diagram
of a two-stage MVR system may be seen in fig. 3.28.
Figure 3.28: Concept diagram of the two-stage MVR system.
For the multi-stage MVR systems shown in fig. 3.28 and fig. 3.29, the pressure ratio of the
compressors are fitted, such that the outlet pressure of the last compressor is 15 bar. For the
two-stage MVR system, this yields the following relation for the pressure ratios:
pMV R,o = p4 = p1 · Rp,C1 · Rp,C2 = 15 bar
(3.109)
The extra degree of freedom imposed by the extra pressure ratio allows for the distribution of
pressure ratios to be optimized to deliver the highest possible COP. For the two-stage system
this is done rather simply with a parametric variation of the pressure ratio over the first
compressor, Rp,C1. As is defined in eq. (3.109), due to the requirement for an outlet pressure
of 15 bar, varying Rp,C1 will yield a corresponding value of Rp,C2.
==PAGE:90==
3.10 Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 6
72
For the three-stage MVR system shown in fig. 3.29, the following relation for the pressure ratios
over the three compressors apply:
pMV R,o = p6 = p1 · Rp,C1 · Rp,C2 · Rp,C3 = 15 bar
(3.110)
As it may be noted, eq. (3.110) has two degrees of freedom.
Thus to optimize the three-
stage MVR system, the EES function Min/Max-table was used to build a parametric variation
where for every value of Rp,C1 the corresponding value of Rp,C2 that yields the highest possible
system COP is computed, while Rp,C3 is resultant from the requirement of a 15 bar MVR
outlet pressure. The result for the parametric analysis may be seen in fig. 4.10.
Figure 3.29: Concept diagram of the three-stage MVR system.
To compute the overall COP of the multi-stage MVR systems, the following definition is used:
COPMV R =
˙QMV R,total
˙Wcomp,total
(3.111)
Where for the multi-stage MVR systems the total heat transferred to the steam flow and the
total compressor powers are defined as expressed below:
˙Wcomp,total = ˙WC1 + ˙WC2 + ... + ˙WCn
(3.112)
˙QMV R,total = ˙QHEX1 + ˙QSC1 + ˙QHEX2 + ˙QSC2 + ... + ˙QHEXn + ˙QSCn
(3.113)
3.10.2
Numerical Modelling of CO2 Precooler in EES
For the CPC in Scenario 6, the applied methodology is the same as applied for the gas con-
densation occurring in the MVR heat exchangers described in section 3.10.1.1. The outlet
temperature for the heat exchangers in the MVR system is 135 °C.
Along with the given
requirement for a 37 °C temperature at the inlet of the liquefaction unit, and MVR heat ex-
changer outlet conditions determined, the overall heat transfer from the gas mixture in CPC
may be calculated.
3.10.3
Thermoflex Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 6
For the TFX modelling of Scenario 6, the individual heat exchangers in the developed Carbon
Capture module described in section 3.6, are coupled in the configuration shown in fig. 3.26. The
==PAGE:91==
3.11 Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 7
73
outlet temperatures determined in section 4.3.1 and section 4.4.1 are imposed to the different
heat exchangers in the developed CC module, and the mass flow is accordingly determined
by the TFX model.
Similarly to Scenario 5, a two-stage heat pump system with an OIC
using ammonia as refrigerant is integrated in Stream 6B in the TFX model. However, since
for Scenario 6, the configuration of heat exchangers is different from Scenario 5 as shown in
fig. 3.23, the source and sink conditions of the heat pump integration are also radically different
from Scenario 5. Thus to properly model the different heat pump conditions, the developed
EES model for the two-stage heat pump with OIC is utilized to determine the optimal operating
conditions for the heat pump integration in Scenario 6. This is done in an iterative process,
where the EES model is fitted to results in the TFX model until a converging solution with
results is achieved.
Aside from the integration of a two-stage heat pump system with OIC, the three-stage MVR
system designed in section 3.10.1.2 the results of which is shown in section 4.5.1, is integrated
in the TFX model. For the integration, the optimal pressure ratios for the three compressors
detailed in table 4.9 are implemented. A detailed overview showing the implementation of the
three-stage MVR system in TFX may be seen in fig. 3.30.
Figure 3.30: Detailed overview showing the implementation of the three-stage MVR system
in Thermoflex.
As may be seen from fig. 3.30 three stages each consisting of a gas compressor, fire-tube type
evaporator and a moisture separator with drainage is implemented to represent the designed
MVR system. For the three gas compressors, since TFX only allows for the polytropic eﬀiciency
to be used as input, the value of the polytropic eﬀiciency is varied to achieve the design
isentropic eﬀiciency of 85 % as detailed in table 3.15 using a control loop. The entire developed
TFX model for Scenario 6 may be seen in appendix A.6.
3.11
Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery
Scenario 7
As mentioned in section 3.2, Scenario 7 is designed for full heat recovery from the integrated
CC facility. Schematically, the heat exchanger configuration is very similar to the configuration
investigated for Scenario 5. The most important change is that the condenser heat from the
liquefaction unit, which in Scenario 5 is cooled to the ambient, is utilized as heat input for the
==PAGE:92==
3.11 Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 7
74
chilled circuit to the integrated VCHPs. This integration naturally implies a much larger heat
pump and may be considered as a type of heat pump cascade system with an intermediate
water circuit. A schematic overview of the DH coupling implemented in Scenario 7 is shown
in fig. 3.31.
Figure 3.31: Schematic overview of the DH connection in Scenario 7.
The sink inlet design point temperature for the liquefaction unit in Scenario 7 is 32 °C, as
the chilled circuit was designed with a forward temperature from the outlet of the heat pump
evaporator with this same temperature level to be able to cool both gas sides of both FGC4
and CPC to 37 °C with a 5 K temperature difference. The sink outlet design temperature for
the liquefaction unit in Scenario 7 is 47 °C.
It should be noted that due to the much larger heat pump installation in Scenario 7, the flows
from Stream A7 and B7 are mixed before the heat pump condenser. This is done in order to
avoid having a very high temperature at the outlet of the condenser before DH3.
Additionally for Scenario 7, the compressor intercooling is changed. In Scenario 5 the com-
pressor intercooling is cooled to the ambient, while in Scenario 7, the compressor intercooling
is delivered by return DH water for the HOFOR DH network that is then heated directly to
the forward temperature. The primary reason for choosing the HOFOR DH network over the
CTR network, is that the temperature levels of the HOFOR network allow for the DH forward
temperature to be delivered directly from the intercooling, which is not possible for the CTR
DH flow.
==PAGE:93==
3.12 Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 8
75
3.11.1
Thermoflex Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 7
The TFX modelling methodology for Scenario 7 is nearly identical to the method applied for
Scenario 5 detailed in section 3.9.2. The principal change, as mentioned previously, is that
the liquefaction unit condenser heat is integrated in the chilled circuit denoted as Stream
C7 in fig. 3.31. However, for Scenario 7, reaching the design sink outlet temperature of 47 °C
requires a higher pressure in the heat pump condenser section. To determine optimal operating
conditions for the liquefaction unit, the developed EES model for the two-stage liquefaction
unit with ammonia refrigerant is used to determine the condenser pressure for the given design
point temperatures in the condenser. This is done in an iterative process, where the EES model
is fitted to results in the TFX model until a converging solution with results is achieved. The
entire developed TFX model for Scenario 7 may be seen in appendix A.7.
3.12
Thermodynamic Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery
Scenario 8
As mentioned in section 3.2, Scenario 8 is designed for full heat recovery from the integrated
CC facility while also utilizing MVR for high-temperature energy recovery. Schematically, the
heat exchanger configuration is very similar to the configuration investigated for Scenario 6,
shown in fig. 3.26. The principal change is that the condenser heat from the liquefaction unit,
which in Scenario 6 is cooled to the ambient, is utilized as heat input for the chilled circuit to
the integrated VCHPs. A schematic overview of the DH coupling implemented in Scenario 8
is shown in fig. 3.32.
3.12.1
Thermoflex Modelling of Waste Heat Recovery Scenario 8
The integrated MVR system is unchanged from the system integrated in Scenario 6, and the
only principal difference from the TFX model for Scenario 6, is the inclusion of the liquefac-
tion unit condenser heat to the chilled water circuit. The methodology of implementing the
liquefaction condenser heat in Scenario 8 follows the approach detailed in section 3.11.1.
3.13
Techno-Economic Analysis
To determine the Levelized Cost of Carbon Capture (LCCC) for each investigated scenario, a
detailed techno-economic analysis was conducted. The LCCC is used to compare the different
investigated scenarios. The techno-economic analysis entails an estimation of the Capital Ex-
penses (CAPEX) and Operating and Maintenance Costs (OMC) for each investigated scenario,
as well as determining the relative income from energy sales gauged against the income from
Scenario 1. The methodology applied for the different elements required for determining the
LCCC are detailed in this section. It should be noted that Scenario 0 is not included in the
analysis conducted in this section as it is not relevant or representative for the future operating
conditions at ARC.
==PAGE:94==
3.13 Techno-Economic Analysis
76
Figure 3.32: Schematic overview of the DH connection in Scenario 8.
3.13.1
Estimation of Total Capital Investment
The Purchased Equipment Cost (PEC) is calculated according to the guidelines detailed in
[38]. The Free On Board (FOB), which for this project is applied as a base component base,
will be multiplied with relevant material, fm, and design-type factors, fd, to reflect the actual
component used for the application. Additionally, a scaling factor, α, is used to estimate the
difference between a component of the reference and actual application sizes.
PEC = fd · fm · FOB ·
( size
sizeref
)α
(3.114)
The size of the components are generally based on the determined values from the TFX mod-
elling results as shown in table 4.12 to table 4.15. However for the heat exchangers, the heat
exchanger area, which serves as the sizing parameter as detailed in table 3.16, must first be
determined. Recalling that the area of a heat exchanger may be determined with the following
equation:
˙Q = U · A · ∆TLM
(3.115)
Where U is the overall heat transfer coeﬀicient, A is the heat exchanger surface area and,
∆TLM is the logarithmic mean temperature difference, defined below:
∆TLM = ∆T1 −∆T2
ln(∆T1
∆T2 )
(3.116)

Where ∆T1 and ∆T2 is defined in the following way:
∆T1 = Th,i −Tc,i
∆T2 = Th,o −Tc,o
(3.117)
For heat exchangers where the size of the dimensioned equipment is larger than the high range
limit for the given component type, multiple parallel heat exchangers are assumed to be used.
Heat exchanger sizing is determined from the overall heat transfer coeﬀicients detailed in [39].
According to Woods [38], the Total Capital Investment (TCI) can be estimated from the PEC
by multiplying with a Total Cost Estimation (TCE) factor that covers the costs of installa-
tion, control systems and multiple other associated costs. TCE factors fTCE are generally in
the range between 3-10 with values between 4-5 being the usual. The TCE factor generally
decreases as more alloys are used in the process [38]. For this paper, a TCE factor of fTCE = 4
is applied for all cost estimations. The TCE factor is applied as detailed in eq. (3.118).
TCI = fTCE · PEC
(3.118)
The results of the TCI estimations may be seen in table 4.16. The overall sum of the estimated
TCI value for the different investigated scenarios, is then used as the capital investment made
in year zero of the calculation in eq. (3.127).
3.13.2
Estimation of Operational Expenses
The estimation of the annual Operating and Maintenance Costs (OMC) for the CC integration
at ARC is calculated as a fraction of the TCI cost of the given component. Generally speaking,
complex components that have moving parts are assumed to have a larger OMC fraction, while
simple components such as heat exchangers and absorber and stripper columns are afforded
a lower OMC fraction. The OMC fractions applied for the different scenarios may be seen in
table 3.17. It should be noted that these fractions are applied to the results of the overall TCI
estimations for the individual individual scenarios shown in table 4.16. It should be noted that
this project does not account for the price of the refrigerant charge or the running costs of
replenishing lost refrigerant. The results of the OMC estimations are shown in table 4.17.
While the concept of solvent degradation has not been thoroughly detailed in this paper, it
should be noted that operating a MEA-based CC facility will entail some MEA degradation
that will then have to be replaced to ensure consistent performance.
According to Union
Engineering, the MEA consumption factor, fMEA, is roughly 1.5 kilograms of MEA per ton of
captured CO2, and the unit price per kilogram of MEA, UPMEA, is approximately 10 DKK.
This is of course liable to change due to market fluctuations, but for this project the price of
MEA is assumed to be constant. The annual costs of MEA consumption is then calculated as:
OMCMEA = mCO2,yr · fMEA · UPMEA
(3.119)
The total OMC costs are then determined as the sum of both component-based OMC costs
and the annual costs for MEA consumption as detailed below:
OMC = OMCcomponents + OMCMEA
(3.120)
==PAGE:97==
3.13 Techno-Economic Analysis
79

3.13.3
Analysis of Future Energy Prices
In a report written for Energinet, the Danish Energy Agency estimated future electricity spot
prices from 2021 to 2040 with a RAMSES model [40]. Using the predicted prices for East
Denmark (DK2) from year 2025, when the full-scale carbon capture facility at ARC is planned
to begin operations, to year 2040, an average electricity price of 351 DKK/MWh is found. This
electricity spot price will be used for the techno-economic evaluation of the different modelled
scenarios. It should be noted that the electricity price assumes that internal electricity con-
sumption is sourced directly from the turbine generator and thus are not subject to distribution
tariffs.
Presently, ARC sells district heating energy to the CTR and HOFOR district heating networks
in the Copenhagen Area. According to a publicly accessible annual report from 2019, ARC
sells district heating at the substitution-price [41]. The substitution-price is defined as the price
at which district heat may be delivered from other sources such as Amagerværket, if ARC eg.
is not able to supply the required district heating demand. Apart from the substitution-price
which in 2020 amounts to 87 DKK/GJ, an upper limit exists for the district heating price,
known as the price ceiling, that in 2020 amounted to 94 DKK/GJ. The principal reasoning for
using the data from 2020 is that due to challenges in the comissioning of Amagerværkets new
BIO4 facility, both substitution-price and price-ceiling were both uncharacteristically high in
20219. For heat produced from WtE facilities a government tariff of 25.8 DKK/GJ applies in
2021 [42], which means that the net price for DH sales is 61.2 DKK/GJ. In a future scenario
where ARC delivers CO2 negative district heating through the integration of carbon capture,
the price of the heat supplied is assumed be the substitution price of 87 DKK/GJ, and the
government tariff is assumed to still be 25.8 DKK/GJ.
Thus to summarize, the prices detailed in table 3.18 are used for the economic evaluations.
9Information supplied by ARC
==PAGE:98==

80

3.13.4
Estimation of Relative Incomes
The total district heating production for the ARC WtE facility is the sum of the heat delivery
to both the HOFOR and the CTR networks as shown below:
˙Qtotal = ˙QHOFOR + ˙QCTR
(3.121)
Since this project considers only nominal operation, which the plant is assumed to operate in
8000 hours per year, as detailed in table 3.19, the overall annual heat production in kWh may
be determined:
Qyr = ˙Qtotal · ANOH
(3.122)
Then by dividing the overall annual heat production with 1000 to have consistent units and
multiplying with the net unit price for district heating, detailed in table 3.18, the overall income
from heat sales may be determined:
IncQ,yr =
˙Qtotal · UPheat,net
103
(3.123)
With a similar methodology and the assumed unit price for electricity sales, detailed in ta-
ble 3.18, the total electricity production and the overall income from electricity sales may be
determined:
Pyr = Pnet · ANOH
IncP,yr = Pyr · UPpower
103
(3.124)
The total yearly income from energy sales is then determined as the product of the income
from district heating production and electricity production:
Inctotal,yr = IncQ,yr + IncP,yr
(3.125)
The relative income for the modelled scenarios is then determined by subtracting the total
yearly income from Scenario 1 without CC integration from the total income of a given scenario,
as is shown in the equation below:
Inctotal,yr,rel = Inctotal,yr −(Inctotal,yr)Scen1
(3.126)
==PAGE:99==
3.13 Techno-Economic Analysis
81
It is this relative income that is used to determine the Net Present Value (NPV) and the Lev-
elized Cost of Carbon Capture (LCCC) of the individual investigated scenarios. It is important
to mention that the relative income for the investigated scenarios is constant in all years ex-
pressed in fixed prices. In actuality inflation will increase the unit price for DH and electricity
sales and thus also the magnitude of the relative income, but the price will follow the inflation.
The principal reasoning for using the relative income of the individual scenarios is that this
thesis considers only differences from the reference ARC WtE facility described in Scenario 1,
to determine the additional overall cost increase from the CAPEX and OMC of the CC facility
but also taking into account the penalty incurred by a lower overall income from energy sales.
The whole discrepancy between these three factors; CAPEX, OMC and, lower income must be
covered by the LCCC to make the overall NPV at the end of the project lifetime zero.
It should be noted that the gate fee applied for the incoming MSW has not been included in
the calculations. This is because the mass flow of burned MSW and thus also the income for
the incineration is the same for all scenarios.
3.13.5
Calculation of The Net Present Value and The Levelized Cost of Carbon
Capture
In the following, the methodology applied for the determination of the Net Present Value
(NPV) and the Levelized Cost of Carbon Capture is detailed. The general methodology is
based on [43]. For the determination of the NPV and LCCC, a project lifetime, n, of 20 years
is assumed. Additionally, the real escalation rate, also known as the rate of return or discount
rate, is assumed to be 4 %. The real escalation rate is assumed to be constant for the defined
project period. Finally, the number of Annual Nominal Operating Hours is assumed to be 8000
hours per year. A summary table of the assumed values for the determination of the NPV and
LCCC may be seen in table 3.19.
Table 3.19: Summary table for assumed values for determining NPV and LCCC.
Parameter
Symbol
Value
Unit
Project lifetime
n
20
yrs
Real escalation rate
rr
4
%
Annual Nominal Operating Hours
ANOH
8000
hrs/yr
The Net Present Value (NPV) of a given project may be calculated as the accumulated sum of
discounted cash flows with the capital investment, TCItotal, assumed to be paid in year zero.
The mathematical definition of NPV used throughout the techno-economic calculations may
be seen in eq. (3.127).
NPV = −TCItotal +
n
∑
i=1
(Inctotal,yr,rel −OMC)(1 + rr)i
(3.127)
As it may be seen from eq. (3.127), the NPV is calculated as the sum of accumulated discounted
cash flows at the end of the project lifetime, after n years have passed. As such, the initial
investment and OMC are defined as negative, while the relative income from energy sales is
==PAGE:100==
3.14 Sensitivity Analysis
82
defined positively. It should be noted though, that the relative income for most of the investi-
gated scenarios is negative, as may be seen in fig. 4.26. Furthermore, neither current prices or
future price projections for the fossil CO2 emission quotas are included in eq. (3.127). The rea-
soning for this is to determine the raw price of CO2 capture that applies for the given scenario.
The question of who will eventually pay for the implementation of CO2 capture is heavily de-
pendent upon how future government CO2 taxation and tariff schemes are implemented, and
beyond the scope of this project.
It is further assumed, that the LCCC and the CO2 capture from the CC facility are constant
for all years. The annual CO2 capture may thus be calculated as the mass flow rate of CO2
determined through the TFX, modelling multiplied by 3.6 to convert from kilograms per second
to tons per hour and then finally multiplying with the assumed ANOH.
mCO2,yr = ˙mCO2 · 3.6 · ANOH
(3.128)
Rewriting the defintion of NPV from eq. (3.127) with a constant LCCC and CO2 capture rate,
the following expression is determined:
NPV = −TCItotal +
n
∑
i=1
(Inctotal,yr,rel −OMC + (mCO2,yr · LCCC))(1 + rr)i = 0
(3.129)
Where the LCCC price multiplied with the annual CO2 capture is used to balance the NPV.
The same result may be determined in a somewhat simpler calculation by introducing a Annuity
Discount Factor. It must be noted that the LCCC is constant for all years and expressed in
fixed prices. Thus while in reality the inflation rate will increase the annual capture fee, the
LCCC price will follow the inflation.
The Annuity Discount Factor is used to determine the present value of a constant annuity for a
given number of years. Described simply, if a project receives a given annuity, x, for n number
of years, then the annuity discount factor is used to determine how many years of the given
annuity is equal to the present value of the annuity. The annuity discount factor is defined as
[44]:
AF(n, rr) = 1 −(1 + rr)−n
rr
(3.130)
With the constant annual tonnage of captured CO2 determined, the LCCC may be calculated
by dividing the determined NPV from eq. (3.127) with an annuity based on the discounted
sum of the annual tonnage of captured CO2, as shown below:
LCCC =
NPV
mCO2,yr · AF(n, rr)
(3.131)
The results for the determination of the LCCC may be seen in fig. 4.27 and table 4.18.
3.14
Sensitivity Analysis
To make an assessment of the importance of input factors for the techno-economic evaluation
detailed in table 3.18 and table 3.19 a sensitivity analysis is conducted. The sensitivity of the
==PAGE:101==
3.14 Sensitivity Analysis
83
different input variables on the calculated LCCC price is evaluated by investigating the relative
change in the output (LCCC) for a relative change in the input variable. For the sensitivity
analysis only one component is varied at a time with all other input variable kept constant.
The input parameters investigated along with the investigated range in the sensitivity analysis
are shown in table 3.20.
Table 3.20: Investigated intervals for sensitivity analysis as a percentage of the initial value.

For the CAPEX costs, the investigated interval ranges from 50 % to 150 % of the initial
values.
This is implemented to reflect the relatively high uncertainty associated with the
pricing correlations derived from [38]. Since the OMC is calculated as a percentage of the
CAPEX, a similar range is investigated.
It should be noted that the CAPEX and OPEX
prices are different for each scenario and may be found in table 4.16 and table 4.17. The real
escalation rate is varied from 0 % to 200 % to reflect market insecurities and determine how
these may impact the overall economy of implementing carbon capture at ARC. Additionally
the electricity spot price and district heating price is varied from 60 % to 140 % to reflect
the great uncertainty in the future Danish energy market. With increasing prevalence of wind
and solar power generation, the spot price for electricity is liable to fluctuate greatly, while
increasing implementation of domestic heat pump systems and future decommissioning of the
large central CHP facilities may influence the district heating price. Finally, it should be noted
that the upper limit for the investigated interval for the ANOH in table 3.20 corresponds to
8760 nominal operating hours annually.

Chapter 4
Results
This section details the results of the different modelling exercises conducted through this thesis
project. Initially in this section, the results of the detailed subsystems investigations carried
out in EES will be presented. Later on, the results of the TFX modelling will be shown and
the investigated scenarios are compared in terms of thermodynamic performance. After this,
the results of the techno-economic analysis are presented. Finally an overall evaluation of the
performance of the integrated CO2 capture system with regards to emissions abatement at the
ARC WtE facility is shown.
4.1
Results of Modelling of The Reference Carbon Capture Sys-
tem in EES
This section will detail the results of the work conducted with EES to determine the basic
reference carbon capture system for use in the modelled scenarios. This section includes the
results of the designed configuration of the modelled gas condensation units, the results of the
modelling of the CO2 compression train and, the designed CO2 liquefaction unit.
4.1.1
Results of Numerical Modelling of Gas Condensation Units in EES
A T-Q-diagram from the numerical modelling of the gas condensation units, FGC3, FGC4,
SOC and CPC may be seen in fig. 4.1. The methodology applied in the modelling may be seen
in section 3.5.1.1.
As it may be seen from fig. 4.1, the condensation curves may be seen to be relatively flat at
high temperatures and then becomes gradually more slanted as the gas is cooled and the water-
carrying capacity decreases. The general shape of the condensation curves are the motivation
for dividing flue gas condensation units with large overall temperature drops into more than
one unit. This is done to decrease the exergy loss between the gas and the coolant. Additionally
the overall heat transfer rate in each condensation unit is evident from fig. 4.1, with the specific
calculated numbers as detailed below:
˙QFGC3 = 18 043 [kJ/s]
˙QFGC4 = 23 269 [kJ/s]
(4.1)
˙QSOC = 27 181 [kJ/s]
˙QCPC = 3 909 [kJ/s]
(4.2)
A T-Q-diagram for all the different hot side heat flows in the CC facility may be seen in fig. 4.2.
Here the absolute magnitude and inlet and outlet temperature levels of the different heat flows

described in table 3.13 to the required future DH forward temperature on the CTR network
implies a temperature lift of 90.9 °C and results in a COP of 3.15. Additionally, based on the
defined inlet and outlet conditions on the air-side of the evaporator results in a very large flow
of ambient air.

1Very close to critical point.

As it may be seen from table 4.5 by comparing the base cycle with the optimized cycle, there is
considerable improvement potential in optimizing the subcooling and superheating in the heat
pump cycle. For the optimized cycle it may be seen that the superheating for most refrigerants
is equal to 19.97 °C. This is a result of the definition of a 5 K minimum pinch point, where
the superheating is increased until a 5 K pinch temperature difference exists both at the inlet
and outlet of the evaporator. The primary way in which the degree of superheating influences
the COP is that the mass flow rate of the refrigerant in the single-stage heat pump system
decreases with increasing superheating since more heat can be transferred for a given flow rate.
Contrary to the other refrigerants investigated, it may be seen in table 4.5 that the single-
stage heat pump system using ammonia as a refrigerant reaches the highest possible COP
with a superheating temperature of 0 K corresponding to the refrigerant outlet state from the
evaporator being saturated vapor. This is a result of the increasingly slanted isentropic lines
in the log(p)-h-diagram for ammonia which may be seen for the two-stage liquefaction unit in
fig. 4.3. Here it may be seen that the isentropic lines get more and more slanted as temperature
increases. The result is that the compressor power for ammonia heat pump systems increases
as the degree of superheating increases.

Principally the same tendencies as table 4.5 are shown in table 4.6 for COP improvement by

refrigerant, the COP may however be observed to decrease with the integration of the IHEX.
This is explained by the same tendencies causing the COP of the single-stage ammonia heat
pump system described in table 4.5 to decrease with higher superheating. Thus as the IHEX
transfers heat from the outlet flow from the condenser to the outlet flow from the evaporator,
the temperature before the compressor increases which requires more compressor power due to
the reasons described previously.

superheating as well as an optimized cycle where the condenser subcooling and evaporator
superheating have been studied to provide the maximum heat pump COP are shown.

As it may be seen from table 4.7, there is a considerable improvement potential in changing
from a single-stage heat pump to a two-stage heat pump system with OIC. It may however
be seen that many of the investigated refrigerants, isopentane, isobutane, R1234ze(Z) and,
R1234ze(E) either have compression occuring into or through the two-phase area. The issues
==PAGE:111==
4.5 Results of Thermodynamic Modelling of Scenario 6 in EES
93
with the compression process are a product of the very skewed log(p)-h-diagrams of the prob-
lematic refrigerants and the design condition that the outlet side of the OIC before the second
compressor is saturated vapor. The principal problem with compressing in the two-phase area
is that refrigerant condensate can form inside the compressor, which for some compressor types
can lead to erosion of the compressor components. Thus all refrigerant cycles with compression
taking partly place inside the two-phase area will be discarded from the selection of the most
suited heat pump cycle for the integration in the TFX modelling.
The modelled heat pump cycles that yield the highest possible COP are listed in table 4.8,
where also the refrigerant volume and mass flow rates are detailed at the inlet of the first
compressor.
Table 4.8: Refrigerant volume and mass flow rates for the best modelled heat pump cycles and
refrigerants.
Name
No.
Config.

˙mREF,C1, [kg/s]

R1234ze(Z)

18.45
154
The refrigerant mass and volume flow rates are important parameters for heat pump systems
design, as they both influence the scaling of the heat pump components.
As an example,
the refrigerant volume flow rate for a reciprocating compressor is a governing parameter in
determining the sizing for the given compressor, with higher volumetric flow rates resulting in
larger, more expensive component sizes [45]. Thus for this project, since the volumetric flow
rate of the two-stage heat pump system with OIC using ammonia as a refrigerant is by far the
lowest, only this heat pump system will be included in the TFX modelling.
The T-Q-diagram for the designed heat pump integration in stream 5B may be seen in fig. 4.7.
At it may be seen trough a comparison with fig. 4.6, the heat pump integration studied in
Scenario 5 is capable of delivering a much higher temperature where the CTR bypass flow is
returned before DH3, corresponding to a much lower heat requirement for heat upgrading in
DH3. As is also evident from fig. 4.7 is that a minimum pinch point temperature of 5 K occurs
at both the refrigerant outlet side of the heat pump condenser as well as in the point where
the refrigerant is saturated vapor. Additionally, it may be seen from fig. 4.7 that a relatively
low temperature difference of 5.18 K exists at the hot side outlet of AIC2. Because of the
already relatively low temperature difference at this point, improving the COP beyond the
4.69 achieved for the two-stage cycle while maintaining the 85 °C DH outlet temperature from
the heat pump condenser is diﬀicult if not impossible without violating the design point 5 K
minimum temperature difference.

modelling for Scenario 6 may be seen in table 4.12 to table 4.15 and appendix A.6.

the COPs detailed in table 4.9, there is a significant improvement potential in implementing
multi-stage MVR systems over single-stage systems.

It may however also be seen in table 4.9, that the pressure ratio in the first compressor for
the multistage systems remains above 3. This is a result of the requirement of boosting the
partial pressure of water vapor to a level where it begins to condense out of the gas mixture
at a temperature level higher than 135 °C.
For the three-stage MVR system, the pressure
ratio may be seen to be much lower than for the first compression stage. This is because the
partial pressure at the outlet of the first heat exchanger is equal to the saturation pressure at
a temperature of 135 °C, and thus the gas mixture only requires a slight pressure increase in
the second compressor to allow for condensation in the next heat exchanger.
==PAGE:113==
4.5 Results of Thermodynamic Modelling of Scenario 6 in EES
95
The T-Q-diagram for the single-stage MVR system may be seen in fig. 4.8. The outlet state
of the compressor, shown in the top left, is heavily superheated and a considerable amount of
desuperheating (on the order of 9 MJ/s) is required before the water vapor starts to condense
out of the gas mixture. This high degree of superheating is the primary reason why both the
compressor power and total transferred heat in the MVR system is higher for the single-stage
system than for the investigated multi-stage MVR systems.
Figure 4.8: T-Q-diagram of single-stage MVR system.
The T-Q-diagram for the three-stage MVR system may be seen in fig. 4.9. Upon comparison
with the single-stage T-Q-diagram shown in fig. 4.8, it may be seen that the degree of super-
heating at the outlet of the first compressor in the three-stage MVR system is significantly
lower than for the single-stage system. Additionally, the T-Q diagram shows how through each
stage, the gas is compressed to a point allowing the water vapor to condense out at a temper-
ature considerably higher than the defined heat exchanger outlet temperatures of 135 °C. It
may also be seen how the slope of the condensation curves get more and more steep through
the different stages of the MVR system. This occurs since the slope of the condensation curve
is directly a result of the molar and mass fractions of water vapor in the gas mixture, both of
which decrease through the MVR system.
A plot showing the results of the conducted optimization efforts for the pressure ratios of the
MVR compressors in the three-stage MVR system may be seen in fig. 4.10. Here it may be
seen that there is an optimum for a compression ratio around 3.35 for the first compression
stage, as detailed in table 4.9. It should be noted that for each value of Rp,C1 a corresponding
value of Rp,C2 that yields the highest possible COP is found through the Min/Max table tool
in EES. It may be seen from fig. 4.10 that the increase in Rp,C1 from two to three yields a
significant increase in the COP of the MVR system. This is because values of Rp,C1 below
three do not increase the partial gas pressure of water vapor suﬀiciently to condense out above
135 °C.
==PAGE:114==
4.5 Results of Thermodynamic Modelling of Scenario 6 in EES
96
Figure 4.9: T-Q-diagram of three-stage MVR system.
Figure 4.10: Results of multi-variable optimization of MVR compressor pressure ratios.
Since the three-stage MVR system has the highest overall COP, this is the only MVR configu-
ration implemented in the TFX modelling of Scenario 6, and all references to the implemented
MVR system will be for the three-stage MVR system unless otherwise explicitly stated.
In fig. 4.11, both partial gas pressures for water vapor, CO2 and the total pressure exerted by
the gas may be seen through the different stages of the MVR system. Here it is seen how the
==PAGE:115==
4.5 Results of Thermodynamic Modelling of Scenario 6 in EES
97
partial gas pressure of water vapor is maintained at a level that allow for condensation at a
temperature level higher than 135 °C. Additionally it may be seen that the partial pressure
of CO2 increases through all stages of the MVR system. This is a result of the water vapor
condensing out of the gas mixture, which increases the molar fraction and thus also the partial
gas pressure of the CO2 in the mixture.
Figure 4.11: T-Q-diagram of stream B5
To quantify the potential improvement potential if the approach temperature between the
generated MVR steam for the reboiler and the reboiler solvent flow can be decreased and the
compressor heat losses minimized, an investigation of MVR performance for different steam
generation temperatures was conducted. The results of this investigation where the calculated
COP is shown for a given set of compressor heat loss factors and steam generation tempera-
tures. It should be noted here, that as the temperatures shown in fig. 4.12 refer to the steam
generation temperature, the gas side outlet temperature is 5 K higher than the listed investi-
gated temperatures due to the imposed pinch point temperature in the MVR heat exchangers.
As it may be seen from fig. 4.12, there is a significant improvement potential in decreasing the
reboiler approach temperature when using the three stage MVR system. Similarly, by reducing
the compressor heat loss factor, which may be achieved e.g. by insulating the compressor, a
significantly higher COP may be realized. It should be noted here, that since the MVR system
operates at very high temperatures with gas mixtures very near or at the dewpoint temper-
ature, minimizing heat losses in the suction line of all compression stages will be extremely
important to minimize water droplets forming on the compressor blades.
It should be noted though, that since a minimum approach temperature of 5-10 K in the reboiler
was detailed by Union Engineering, this thesis will investigate exclusively the integration of a
three-stage MVR system with a steam generation temperature of 130 °C.

4.6
Results of Thermoflex Modelling of the Investigated
Scenarios
This section will include a relatively brief description of the major results for each investigated
scenario. An overview of the these results is presented in table 4.12 to table 4.15 which will
subsequently used for a detailed comparison between the thermodynamic performance of the
scenarios.
4.6.1
Results of Thermoflex Modelling of Scenario 0
The results of the TFX modelling of Scenario 0 for the ARC WtE plant without CC and mod-
elled with the current DH temperature levels, detailed in table 3.1, may be seen in table 4.12,
table 4.13 and table 4.14. As it may be seen from table 4.14, the AHPs deliver an outlet
temperature of 85.33 °C after the drive steam subcooling. This fits the information regarding
the CTR bypass flow supplied by ARC very well. As was mentioned in section 3.1, the AHPs
==PAGE:117==
4.6 Results of Thermoflex Modelling of the Investigated Scenarios
99
are rated for a nominal steam consumption of 50 MJ/s and a total nominal heat delivery of
82 MJ/s corresponding to a COP of 1.64, which is very close to the TFX-calculated value
of 1.65 detailed in table 4.14. As may be seen in table 4.14, the determined heat delivery is
considerably less than the nominal heating effect, which also corresponds well with the actual
operation of the AHPs. Additionally the total calculated fuel utilization of Scenario 0 is shown
in table 4.12. The value of 108.1 % measured on the LHV is very close to the value provided
by ARC of 108 %. The remaining results shown in table 4.12, table 4.13 and table 4.14 are
very diﬀicult to corroborate with real operational data, since ARC is constantly regulating the
energy outputs of the facility to meet market demand.
4.6.2
Results of Thermoflex Modelling of Scenario 1
The results of the TFX modelling of Scenario 1 for the ARC WtE plant without CC and
modelled with the future DH temperature levels, detailed in table 3.1, may be seen in table 4.12,
table 4.13 and table 4.14. As is previously mentioned, Scenario 1 serves as the reference scenario
against which all other scenarios with CO2 capture are gauged. From table 4.12 it may be
seen that the fuel utilization in Scenario 1 is roughly comparable to the value determined in
Scenario 0. However, there is a considerable decrease in the heat transfer and thus the bleed
steam consumption in DH3 in Scenario 1 compared with Scenario 0. The principal reasoning
for this is that the lower DH forward temperature in Scenario 1 for the CTR DH flow requires
less heat upgrading in in the third turbine condenser DH3.
4.6.3
Results of Thermoflex Modelling of Scenario 2
The results of the TFX modelling of Scenario 2 for the ARC WtE plant with CC integration may
be seen in table 4.12, table 4.13 and table 4.14. Additionally, the results for the liquefaction
unit are shown in table 4.15. As may be seen in table 4.12 the total energy production is
significantly lower than in the reference case without CC (Scenario 1), as a result of the steam
consumption for the CO2 desorption process. With nearly all heat from the CC facility being
cooled away, the total drop in energy production from Scenario 1 to Scenario 2 is approximately
34 % which is consistent with the findings of other literature for CC integration without heat
recovery [25, 26].
For Scenario 2, the reboiler steam sourcing is governed by the TFX model with 61.7 MJ/s
coming from turbine bypass steam and 12.9 MJ/s coming from the turbine extraction point
E1. It may also be seen in table 4.12 that for the HOFOR DH network, the total heat delivery
is decreased from 81.4 MJ/s in Scenario 1 to 59 MJ/s in Scenario 2. This lower heat production
for the HOFOR DH network is a result of a redistribution of the bleed and exhaust steam to
the turbine condensers to allow the CTR DH flow to reach the forward temperature.
4.6.4
Results of Thermoflex Modelling of Scenario 3
Since for Scenario 3 the TFX modelling results are identical to the results of Scenario 2, the
results shown in table 4.12 equate to the modelling results for Scenario 2 with the addition of the
results for the designed ASHPs that provide the discrepancy between the total heat productions
in Scenario 1 and 2. The results of the designed ASHPs may be seen in table 4.4. As it may
be seen from table 4.12, the net power production in Scenario 3 is slightly negative, since the
power consumption of the ASHPs is larger than the net power production in Scenario 2.
==PAGE:118==
4.6 Results of Thermoflex Modelling of the Investigated Scenarios
100
4.6.5
Results of Thermoflex Modelling of Scenario 4
For Scenario 4, the results of the TFX modelling may be seen in table 4.12, table 4.13 and
table 4.14. Additionally, the results for the liquefaction unit are shown in table 4.15. Scenario 4
details a limited heat integration with waste heat from the CC unit using AHPs for waste heat
upgrading. As it may be seen from table 4.12, the total heat production is actually relatively
high, but there is a major caveat to this. The forward temperature from DH4 for the CTR DH
flow only reaches 81.1 °C, which is very far from the desired forward temperature of 95 °C, and
as a result the DH production for the CTR network is considered to be non-useful heating with
no meaningful value. The cause of the low outlet temperature is the extremely large mass flow
rate of relatively low-temperature water entering DH3 from the CTR bypass flow as shown in
table 4.13. The large mass flow rate is a result of the CTR bypass DH coupling of Scenario 4
which may be seen in fig. 3.22.
In an effort to raise the forward temperature of the CTR to 95 °C, an analysis was conducted
in TFX. Here turbine bypass steam was taken and delivered directly to DH3 to facilitate
additional waste heat upgrading. The results of this analysis may be seen in fig. 4.13. As it
may be seen from the figure, for the investigated interval, the forward temperature of the CTR
DH flow never reaches the required temperature regardless of the very large mass flow rate of
bypass steam to DH3. It should be noted that the dashed lines beyond a steam bypass flow of
10 kg/s indicates convergence problems in the TFX model due to an extremely low mass flow
rate through the back-end of the turbine at these relatively high turbine bypass flows. It may
thus be concluded, that it is not possible to deliver the required forward temperature for the
CTR DH flow in Scenario 4 regardless of the consumption of turbine bypass steam without a
radical redesign. The total required heat transfer to upgrade the bypass flow to the required
forward temperature of 95 °C is approximately 78.4 MJ/s. As the turbine bypass steam to DH3
increases, the AHP drive steam decreases and the flue gas temperature at the outlet of FGC4
starts to increase. It may then be concluded, that the DH coupling studied in Scenario 4 is not
capable of delivering CO2 capture at a net zero energy consumption without massive penalties
to the heat delivery of the ARC WtE facility. Thus for Scenario 4, the results detailed in the
rest of this project will be for no turbine bypass steam to DH3.

4.6.6
Results of Thermoflex Modelling of Scenario 5
For Scenario 5, the results of the TFX modelling may be seen in table 4.12, table 4.13 and
table 4.14. Additionally, the results for the liquefaction unit are shown in table 4.15. Scenario
5 details a limited heat integration with waste heat from the CC unit using VCHPs for waste
heat upgrading to maximize the temperature of the CTR bypass flow before DH3. As it may
be seen, the gross power production from the turbine for Scenario 5 is significantly higher than
for Scenario 4, which is primarily a result of removing the extraction of drive steam for the
AHPs such that a larger mass flow rate of steam expands through the back-end of the turbine.
Since Scenario 5 has a relatively high inlet temperature from the CTR bypass flow before DH3
(cf.
table 4.13) an analysis was conducted to determine whether the reboiler steam might
feasibly be sourced with the majority of the energy input stemming from extraction steam
rather than from turbine bypass steam. The results of the analysis may be seen in fig. 4.14.
As it may be seen from fig. 4.14, when decreasing the turbine bypass steam by moving towards
the left side of the plot, the CTR forward temperature decreases below the desired forward
temperature. This occurs because the enthalpy of the drawn turbine bypass is significantly
higher than the enthalpy of the extraction steam from extraction point E1. Thus a larger mass
flow rate of extraction steam is needed to provide the same energy input as the smaller mass
flow rate of live steam. Thus for Scenario 5, a distribution of reboiler steam with 61.7 MJ/s
coming from turbine bypass steam and 12.9 MJ/s coming from the turbine extraction point
E1 is maintained from Scenario 2 to 4.

table 4.14. The gross power production for Scenario 6 may be seen to be much higher than
Scenario 2 to 5. This is a result of the inclusion of the three-stage MVR system that provides a

As it may be seen in fig. 4.15, despite using only extraction steam for the desorption process at

It should be noted that a forward temperature of 89.7 °C is considerably above the classically
defined limit of 80 °C for conventional heat pumps systems and well within the realm of high-
temperature heat pumps that are typically classified by forward temperatures between 80-
100 °C [46]. Additionally, for ammonia (R717) the forward temperature may be approaching
the technically practical limit of the refrigerant and other types of refrigerants may be more
applicable.

4.6.10
Thermodynamic Comparison of investigated Scenarios
The gross and net power production of each individual investigated scenario may be seen plotted
in a bar chart in fig. 4.16. As described previously it may be seen that there is a considerable
drop in the gross power production in Scenario 2 to 5, and to a much lower extent for Scenario
6 to 8. The primary explanation for this trend is that Scenario 2 to 5 consume live steam from
the turbine bypass for the CO2 desorption process in the reboiler, as may be seen in fig. 4.20,
which heavily reduces the flow through the first stages of the turbine, where the majority of
the shaft power is generated as is shown in fig. 4.23.
The individual contributions to the parasitic power consumption for the individual scenarios
are presented in fig. 4.17. Generally, it may be seen that the implementation of CC brings a
considerable increase in the parasitic loads for the ARC WtE facility. It may also be seen that
the major reason for the negative net power production shown in fig. 4.16 for Scenario 3 is due
to the very large power consumption of the implemented external ASHPs. Additionally it may
be seen that the major factor contributing to a lower net power production in Scenario 2 and
3 relative to Scenario 4 - both scenarios using AHPs - is the power consumption used for the
large air coolers implemented in Scenario 2 and 3. Finally the increase in parasitic load from
Scenario 5 to 7 and from Scenario 6 to 8 may clearly be seen to be caused by the much larger
heat pump installation.
The DH production from the different scenarios may be seen in fig. 4.18. It can be observed that
the total heat production from Scenario 5 to 8 all have significantly higher heat productions
than the reference Scenario 1. The additional heat production is a result of sacrificing power
generation to recover waste heat from the CC facility by means of vapor compression heat
pump systems. The power consumption for waste heat recovery becomes useful heating which
==PAGE:126==
4.6 Results of Thermoflex Modelling of the Investigated Scenarios
108
Gross

scenarios.

explains the larger heat productions. Additionally, the integration of the MVR systems in
Scenario 6 and 8 has a significant impact on the total heat production. This is due to the
heat energy from SOC being utilized for reboiler steam generation rather than DH production,
as in Scenario 5 and 7. Additionally, for Scenario 7, the heat recovered from the compressor
intercooling, shown in fig. 4.18, is found to be relatively small. Finally, for Scenario 4 the total
heat production may be seen to be the HOFOR DH delivery only, as the CTR DH flow does
not reach the required forward temperature and is thus counted as non-useful heat.

Figure 4.18: Total heat production in the investigated scenarios.
Based on the net power shown in fig. 4.16 and the total DH production shown in fig. 4.18 the
fuel utilization and the electrical eﬀiciency of each investigated scenario are shown in fig. 4.19.
As it may be seen, the base implementation of CC without heat recovery in Scenario 2 has a
major penalty on the fuel utilization of the plant. Additionally the fuel utilization of Scenario
4 is very low due to the majority of the heat production in the CTR flow not being counted due
to not reaching the required forward temperature. Finally it may be seen that Scenario 7 and
8 have a higher fuel utilization than the reference case in Scenario 1. This may be explained by
condensation energy of the CO2 liquefaction being utilized. Thus considering a control volume
around the ARC WtE facility, for Scenario 1 a flue gas composed of CO2 and other gases is
cooled to 37 °C before being emitted to the ambient. For Scenario 2 to 6, the same flue gas
only with 91.3 % of the CO2 in the gas removed, as detailed in section 3.4, and the nearly CO2
free gas is cooled to 37 °C and emitted to the ambient. For Scenario 2 to 6, the captured CO2
is then cooled and liquefied, but the liquefaction unit condenser heat is cooled to the ambient.
For Scenario 7 and 8 it is thus the fact that far lower flue gas losses exist due to the recovery
of the condensation heat from the CO2 stream that ultimately yields a higher fuel utilization.
For the scenarios with CC integration, the distribution of the sources delivering the reboiler
steam may be seen in fig. 4.20.
As was mentioned previously, Scenario 2 through 5 have
the exact same distribution of turbine bypass and turbine extraction steam with 61.7 MJ/s
coming from turbine bypass steam and 12.9 MJ/s coming from the turbine extraction point E1.
Scenario 6 and 8 utilize 27.4 MJ/s of MVR steam while the remaining 47.2 MJ/s is extraction
steam from E1. Finally, for Scenario 7 the total 74.6 MJ/s is delivered with turbine extraction
steam from E1.
The effect of the different steam flows taken from the turbine for the investigated scenarios
may be seen in fig. 4.21 where the mass flow rates of steam through the different sections of
the turbine are illustrated. It should be noted, that the mass flow rates for Scenario 2 to 4 are
nearly identical, and thus only the steam mass flow rate for Scenario 2 is shown in fig. 4.21.
By comparing the mass flow rates in Scenario 1 and Scenario 2, the effect of drawing live

and, 8 that do not use turbine bypass steam for the CO2 desorption, the steam mass flow in

extraction point E1, the steam mass flow rates may be seen to diverge as a result of how the

The energy flow rates through the turbine for the investigated scenarios may be seen in fig. 4.22.
The energy flow is gauged against the enthalpy of the dearator which is constant for all sce-
narios. The decreases in the energy flow from inlet to outlet of the different turbine stages
equate to the generated shaft power for the given turbine stage, which is shown specifically in
fig. 4.23. The energy flow for Scenario 2 also provides an indication of why DH3 in Scenario 4
is incapable of upgrading the CTR DH flow to the desired forward temperature. As it may be
seen for the energy flow at the turbine bleed to DH3, the total energy flow gauged against the
dearator is approximately 45 MJ/s. Calculating this energy flow instead for Scenario 4 at the
DH3 turbine bleed gauged against the outlet enthalpy of the turbine condensate from DH3,
returns a total potential energy flow of 48.8 MJ/s. This is much lower than the 78.4 MJ/s
required to upgrade the CTR DH flow to the required forward temperature, and thus even
utilizing the entire steam flow present to heat upgrading in DH3 is still insuﬀicient to reach
the forward temperature.
The cumulative shaft power for the different stages of the turbine for the investigated scenarios
is shown in fig. 4.23.
As expected, the primary portion of the cumulative generated shaft
power is produced in the first stages of the turbine, with as much as 47 % of the total shaft
power generated in the very first turbine stage. Additionally it may be seen upon comparison
with fig. 4.21, that scenarios utilizing bypass steam for CO2 desorption have significantly lower
cumulative shaft power and thus also a lower gross power due to the lower mass flow rate
through the first high-pressure turbine stages.

4.7
Results of Techno-economic Evaluation
This section details the results of the techno-economic evaluation conducted for the investigated
scenarios, the methodology for which is described in section 3.13. Initially the results of the
TCI and OMC estimations are presented, after which the calculated incomes from energy sales
for the different scenarios will be detailed. Finally the results for the calculated LCCC for the
investigated scenarios will be presented.
4.7.1
Results of Total Capital Investment Estimation
The results of the TCI estimations based on the pricing correlations shown in table 3.16 are
shown in table 4.16.
As it may be seen, scenarios with a higher degree of heat recovery
have a much higher total TCIs than scenarios with lower heat recovery rates. Thus Scenario
2 and 4 which utilize the existing AHPs installed at ARC today, have the lowest TCItotal
of the investigated scenarios, with Scenario 4 being slightly lower price as a result of lower
expenditures for air coolers. Scenario 3 may be seen to have the highest TCItotal of all the
investigated scenarios because of the very large installation of external ASHPs. Additionally
it may be seen that the scenarios utilizing the three-stage MVR system, Scenario 6 and 8, are
generally more expensive when compared to Scenario 5 and 7 without the MVR system. As it
may be seen, Scenario 6 is on the order of 40 MDKK more expensive than Scenario 5 which
in large part is due to the significantly more expensive compressor installation required for the
MVR system, though this is slightly offset by avoiding the costs related to the SOC as well
as a lower cost for the CPC. The same trend may be observed upon comparing Scenario 7
and 8 with the price discrepancy being slightly offset by the need for a larger compression
==PAGE:132==
4.7 Results of Techno-economic Evaluation
114
installation in VCHP system due to the higher condenser outlet temperature in Scenario 7
seen in table 4.14.

4.7.2
Results of OMC Estimation
The results of the OMC estimations may be seen in table 4.17. Since the yearly OMC costs are
calculated as fractions of the TCI costs for the different components, the same general trend
showing higher costs for higher degrees of heat recovery also applies to the OMC estimations.
Additionally it may be seen that for most scenarios, the largest contributor to the total OMC
is the costs of MEA consumption which totals 8.26 MDKK/yr.
4.7.3
Results for Calculation of Net Present Value and The Levelized Cost of
Carbon Capture
The determined absolute incomes for the investigated scenarios may be seen in fig. 4.25 with the
contributions from both electricity sales and DH production shown in a stacked arrangement.
As it may be seen, the total absolute income for all scenarios with CC is lower than the
reference case. This is in large part due to the income from electricity sales also being lower
for all scenarios with CC than for the reference in Scenario 1, with the electricity sales in
Scenario 3 even being slightly negative because of the negative net power production detailed
in table 4.12. For Scenario 4 the absolute income from heat sales may be seen to be very low
due to the insuﬀicient CTR forward temperature which means that the CTR DH production
is not included in the heat sales and thus assigned zero value.

The relative incomes from heat and electricity sales may be seen in fig. 4.26 with the net
total relative annual income gauged against the absolute income for Scenario 1 as shown in
fig. 4.25. the net total relative annual income, Inctotal,yr,rel, is shown as a wide black line. For
all investigated scenarios with CC, the net total relative income is negative. For Scenario 2
through 4, the relative income from heat sales is either negative or zero (Scenario 3) due to
lower DH heating productions than the reference case in Scenario 1. Interestingly, for Scenarios
5 through 8 which all have larger DH productions than Scenario 1, the relative income loss
from electricity sales is offset by the higher income from DH sales.

From the NPV which is calculated from eq. (3.127), the LCCC is determined with eq. (3.131).
The calculated values for the LCCC price for all investigated scenarios with CC integration
may be seen in fig. 4.27. Since all scenarios are balanced with the same annual CO2 capture and
annuity factor, the general trends from the net relative incomes from fig. 4.26 also translate to
the calculated LCCC. It may be seen from fig. 4.27, that scenarios with higher relative incomes
stemming from having higher degrees of heat recovery from the CC facility also have lower
LCCCs. Recalling that Scenario 2 has no CC heat recovery, Scenario 3 has some external heat
recovery, Scenario 4 through 6 have limited heat recovery as most of the heat from the CC unit
is recovered, while some is cooled away and, finally Scenarios 7 and 8 have full heat recovery
from the CC unit. Ignoring Scenario 4, a progression may be seen, showing that as the degree
of heat recovery from the CC unit increases, the LCCC accordingly decreases, thus supporting
the project hypothesis described in chapter 1.
The LCCC for the four best investigated scenarios are shown in fig. 4.28. Here the progression
of going from a limited heat recovery scenario to a full heat recovery scenario may clearly be
seen. Scenario 7 has the lowest overall LCCC. The primary reason for the LCCC being lower
in Scenario 7 than 8, is that the net power production in Scenario 7 is slightly higher than for
Scenario 8.

By comparing the scenarios without the contribution from DH production, it may be seen that
Scenario 6 without MVR then has the lowest LCCC. This might indicate that for power-only
facilities with CC, the three-stage MVR solution could be the preferred option.

MVR-system yields a lower LCCC for this type of facility.
The individual elements of the contribution to the LCCC from the loss of electricity sales may
be seen in fig. 4.30. Here it may be seen that for most scenarios that consume live steam from
the turbine bypass (Scenario 2 to 5) the major factor contributing to the loss of electricity sales
that effect the net contribution to the LCCC, is the loss of shaft power in the turbine which
results in a lower gross power production. It may be seen, that the gross power loss in Scenario
5 is significantly smaller than for Scenario 2 to 3, due to avoiding the extraction of AHP drive
steam in Scenario 5 causing a larger steam mass flow rate to expand through the turbine. For
Scenarios 6 and 8, the LCCC constituent from gross power loss is relatively small due to much
of the reboiler steam being generated by the MVR system. For Scenario 7, the gross power
loss may be seen to constitute a much larger portion of the LCCC than in Scenario 6 and 7
due to the larger steam extraction for the CO2 desorption. It may however also be seen that
the sum of the gross power loss and the net power loss due to the MVR power consumption
in Scenario 8 is much larger than the gross power loss in Scenario 7. The opposite is true for
Scenario 5 and 6, where the sum of the gross power loss and the net power loss due to the MVR
power consumption in Scenario 6 is much smaller than the gross power loss in Scenario 5. It
may thus be concluded, that for scenarios utilizing a significant fraction of live steam for CO2
desorption, the designed MVR system can effect a significant reduction of the LCCC, while it
is not competitive against scenarios utilizing only extraction steam. It should be noted that
auxiliary power consumption has not been included in fig. 4.30 due to being nearly constant
for all investigated scenarios including the reference in Scenario 1.

Figure 4.31: Spider plot showing the relative sensitivities for Scenario 7
==PAGE:138==
4.9 Emissions Abatement with CCS Integration at ARC
120
As it may be seen from fig. 4.31, the LCCC for Scenario 7 is highly sensitive to the Annual
Nominal Operating Hours, with the LCCC nearly doubling as the ANOH decreases from 8000
hrs/yr to 4000 hrs/yr. This occurs because both the incomes and the annual CO2 capture
decreases with the resultant LCCC becoming exponentially higher as the number of ANOH
decreases. This explains the impetus for favoring implementation of CC in base load facilities
over peak or variable load facilities such as biomass CHP plants and gas turbine units in
Denmark. Facilities delivering base load will have a much higher number of ANOH and thus
may be able to deliver a significantly lower LCCC.
It should be mentioned that the relative sensitivities for the DH price and electricity price
shown in fig. 4.31 are calculated from incomes relatively to the reference Scenario 1. This
explains why the LCCC increases with increasing electricity prices, since the income is gauged
relatively to the income in Scenario 1. For the DH price, the LCCC may be observed to decrease
as the price increases since the income from heat sales is measured relatively to heat sales in
Scenario 1.
4.9
Emissions Abatement with CCS Integration at ARC
To determine the integrated CC facility’s performance in relation to CO2 emissions abatement,
the net CO2 emission is determined. The fraction of biogenic CO2 in the flue gas at ARC
is measured on a monthly basis and for the year 2020, the biogenic fraction was between
65-70 % of the CO2 emissions1.
As may be seen in table 4.15, the captured CO2 flow in
nominal operation is 19.08 kg/s with a CO2 removal rate of 91.3 % as described in section 3.4.
Thus the total CO2 emissions from both biogenic and fossil sources may be determined, and
these are shown in table 4.19. Then both the CO2 emissions with CCS and the avoided CO2
emissions may be calculated. The net CO2 emissions with CCS may then be calculated with
the methodology described in [47] where the fossil CO2 emissions with CCS subtracted from
the biogenic CO2 emissions avoided with CCS. It should be noted, that the values for the net
CO2 emissions determined in this section require that the captured CO2 is safely sequestered
after capture and not simply intermittently stored and later re-emitted as would be the case
for use of the captured CO2 as feedstock for the production of green fuels. The results of the
net CO2 emissions may be seen in table 4.19.
