As it may be seen in table 4.19 the net CO2 emissions are -353 760 tCO2/yr. These are negative
emissions that when safely sequestered in underground geological formations may be applied
to offset emissions from other sectors or as a carbon removal technology to combat global
1Information provided by ARC
==PAGE:139==
4.9 Emissions Abatement with CCS Integration at ARC
121
warming by effectively capturing CO2 from the atmosphere. To put the net CO2 emissions
in perspective, the average European car drives approximately 12 000 km/yr [48], and the
average CO2 emission for new passenger cars is 122.3 gCO2/km [49]. This puts the annual
CO2 emissions for an average new passenger car at 1.46 tCO2/yr.
Thus the negative net
emissions may be used to offset the emissions of 241 000 new fossil fuel powered passenger
cars. According to [50] in the beginning of 2021 2.72 million passenger cars were registered in
Denmark. This equates to the negative emissions from the CC facility at ARC being able to
offset the fossil CO2 emissions from approximately 8.8 % of the Danish passenger car fleet.
It should be noted though that the emissions reduction from ARC with CC also significantly
aids in achieving the goals of the Danish climate legislation of a 70 % emissions reduction
in 2030 and reaching net zero in 2050. The total absolute fossil emissions decrease from the
ARC WtE facility with CC may be calculated as the total avoided CO2 emissions with CCS
subtracted by the remaining fossil CO2 emissions with CCS. This yields a total fossil CO2
emissions decrease of 532 279 tCO2/yr, of which 370 771 tCO2/yr are negative emissions. Of
the total target absolute CO2 emissions reduction from 2019 to 2030 of 16.01 MtCO2/yr, as
shown in table 2.1, the ARC facility may thus contribute approximately 3.3 % of the total
emissions reduction required in this period.
==PAGE:140==
Chapter 5
Discussion
As it may be seen upon comparison between table 4.15 and table 3.7, the mass flow rates of
removed CO2 are slightly different with 19.12 kg/s determined by Union Engineering based
on data delivered by ARC and 19.08 kg/s based on the numbers determined with TFX. Both
numbers are calculated with a CO2 removal rate of 91.3 % with the discrepancy being caused
by slight variations in the flue gas molar fractions between the data supplied by ARC and the
values determined with the TFX model. It should be noted though, that the discrepancy in
the captured CO2 mass flow rate amounts to only 0.2 %, so it is unlikely to massively influence
the overall results, even though the reboiler duty for the TFX-modelling has been implemented
with the values determined by Union Engineering.
As may be seen in table 4.13, the mass flow rate of the CTR bypass flow is maintained from
Scenario 0 with the current DH temperatures to Scenario 1 with the future temperature levels.
Due to the 5 K lower DH return temperature on the CTR bypass flow in Scenario 1, the outlet
temperature of the bypass flow before DH3 decreases to around 80 °C for Scenario 1 from 85 °C
in Scenario 0. To ensure total consistency between the modelled scenarios, the mass flow rate
of the CTR bypass flow should have been decreased such that the outlet temperature before
DH3 was equal to the 85 °C in Scenario 1. Decreasing the flow through the CTR bypass would
have decreased the overall amount of bleed steam to DH3 which allow more steam to pass
through the fifth turbine stage, T5, and thus produce slightly more shaft power. However it
should be noted that the TFX model, to utilize the condensation energy at the back-end of
the turbine, would have increased the primary flow for the CTR DH network to absorb the
steam energy freed up by increasing the temperature of the bypass flow. Thus the saved steam
energy from DH3 would be distributed to DH1 and DH2 with some final heat upgrading still
being required in DH3 for the CTR primary DH flow, slightly negating the potential benefits
of increasing the bypass temperature.
As was shown in table 4.10, there is a discrepancy between the calculated heat transfer from
the gas condensation units calculated in EES and TFX. This is likely due to the relatively
simplistic approach that considers the gas mixture to be ideal such that the total enthalpy may
be determined as the mass fraction weighted sum of the gas constituents. It is theorized that
the cooling of the gas mixture at or very near the dewpoint may not be accurately modelled
with the specified approach in EES. Instead TFX may apply more advanced gas table property
data, that is able to more accurately model the gas condensation process. It should be noted
that other measures such as using the built-in thermodynamic functions in EES for moist air
are not applicable since this function assumes atmospheric air and thus is not able to properly
model the specified flue gasses or the stripper overhead gas mixture which is at high-pressure.
For future projects detailing a significant amount of design work with gas condensation units
in EES, a more advanced methodology should be implemented. It is important though to
==PAGE:141==
123
mention that the discrepancy between the heat transfer in the two modelling approaches does
not prevent the implementation of the designed overall DH couplings. Thus even though the
numerical modelling in EES yields different results than TFX, the design work conducted in
EES proved to be extremely valuable for the later integrations.
The results of this report suggest that CC retrofits in facilities with AHPs will be challenged in
getting a low LCCC. This is because the temperature levels in the CC heat exchangers studied
in this paper preclude the meaningful usage of AHPs due to considerations relating to the
different temperature levels in the AHP absorber and condenser. The different temperature
levels in the AHP absorber and condenser have not been investigated in-depth for this project,
but a detailed analysis should ultimately be carried out to determine, if the AHPs can be
used for waste heat upgrading in a similar configuration as in stream B5. Without external
steam sources, the AHPs and the reboiler will be competing for the same steam which will
require very large amounts of steam extraction from the turbine which negatively impacts the
performance of the power plant. Turbine ventilation and subsequent tripping may be induced
if the mass flow rate of steam becomes too low. With many plants likely being limited in the
amounts of extraction steam that can feasibly be removed from the middle of the turbine, the
use of turbine bypass steam may be required, which as shown in fig. 4.30 as the gross power
loss between Scenario 5 and 7 increases the overall LCCC price.
As detailed in table 4.16, the total estimated capital expenses for the designed CC solutions
are between 850 MDKK to 1 250 MDKK, with the scenarios with the best performance with
regards to the determined LCCC generally between 1 050 to 1 200 MDKK. This is significantly
above the estimations made by ARC in relation to the conducted EUDP project described in
[21]. ARC has estimated the costs of building a full-scale CC facility to be between 450 to 900
MDKK. While it is unclear exactly what the ARC CAPEX estimates include, the discrepancy
may in large part be caused by the addition of the relatively expensive VCHP systems integrated
in this project, that account for 200 to 400 MDKK depending on the scenario. According to
[51], the total costs of building the current ARC facility was 3 900 MDKK. This means that
the capital expenses of the CC facility for the investigated scenarios are between 22 % and 32
% of the total price of the ARC facility. Additionally, the OMC costs for the CC facility have
been estimated for this project between 26 to 39 MDKK/yr, with the ARC estimate being
an annual operation expense of 85 MDKK/yr.
Again, it is not described what ARC have
included in this figure or whether the loss of income from energy sales is included. This project
has utilized a somewhat simple methodology, with estimations for the OMC costs for a given
component being determined as a fraction of the capital expenses, and the fractional expense is
a function of the overall complexity of the given component. It should also be noted, that while
MEA CO2 capture is relatively well known, the implementation of CO2 capture at this scale
combined with the flue gas properties of the ARC WtE facility is a relatively new application
for the technology. This means that the costs of actually building a CC facility at this scale is
not really well understood and thus finding suitable capital expense pricing correlations proved
diﬀicult. Similarly, the presence of particulate matter and certain gasses in the flue gas from
ARC may potentially have a detrimental effect on the MEA solvent that may require a larger
annual amine consumption which already constitutes a major portion of the annual OMC as
shown in table 4.17.
This project assumes 8000 Annual Nominal Operating Hours (ANOH), which implies that the
heat and electricity off-take is constant throughout the year. Other projects such as [52] and
[53] have similarly assumed 8000 annual operating hours. For a country such as Denmark
==PAGE:142==
124
where the district heating demand varies greatly from winter to summer conditions, assuming
a constant heat off-take represents a somewhat simplistic approach. From fig. 4.29 it may
be deduced, that for Scenarios 5 through 8 that have the lowest LCCC, the loss of heating
sales to offset the LCCC will incur higher capture costs per ton of CO2. Thus to be able to
evaluate the LCCC price with higher precision, the yearly fluctuations in DH deliveries should
be accounted for through an analysis of the annual heat load distribution curve for ARC. It
should be noted though, that a future scenario where ARC is given preferred rights to DH
sales over other facilities due to producing CO2 negative heating might feasibly represent a
possibility for achieving the 8000 ANOH.
WtE facilities generally have more operating hours per year than eg.
coal and gas power
plants due to their secondary utility of disposing of residual wastes. Thus in a greater societal
perspective, integrating CC on WtE facilities rather than these alternative facilities will likely
have far lower socioeconomic costs in achieving the required emissions reduction in 2030 and
2050 that are detailed in the Danish climate legislation. For the large centralized bioenergy
facilities such as Avedøre, Studstrup and Amagerværket however, it may be possible to achieve
economy of scale benefits that increase the competitiveness of these facilities compared to WtE
facilities.
Zeman et. al. determined the costs of carbon capture from a WtE facility combusting MSW
to be 64 USD/tCO2 [54], which corresponds to 390 DKK/tCO2 using the same conversion
factor detailed in table 3.16. The IEA list the price for CO2 capture from dilute sources such
as power generation at between 40-120 USD/tCO2 corresponding to approximately 244-732
DKK/tCO2 [55]. Chandel et. al. estimated the costs of CC from a WtE facility to be between
58-93 USD/tCO2 dependent upon the calculation approach [56] which corresponds to 354-
567 DKK/tCO2. Thus it might be stated that the determined LCCC prices for the two best
investigated scenarios - Scenario 7 and 8 - Are well within range of LCCCs determined by other
projects. However it should be noted that the LCCC price depends heavily on the calculation
assumptions and applied methodologies as shown in section 4.8, which means that the absolute
magnitude of the LCCC may not be directly indicative of the overall economy of implementing
a CC facility at ARC. This is because, calculated values from other projects may account also
account for e.g. CO2 emission quotas, something which has not been included for this project.
Instead this project has focused on determining the raw price of CC per ton of CO2 captured
to determine the net costs that have to be covered to make the project feasible.
It should be noted that WtE facilities have a multiple different options for covering the LCCC.
For example the heat price of the district heating delivered to the CTR and HOFOR networks
could be increased with the argument that CO2 negative DH is a more valuable product. The
effect of increasing the heat price for Scenario 7 may be seen in fig. 4.31. Additionally an
increased gate fee per tonnage of MSW treated, may be used to deliver part of the LCCC
costs. Finally, the LCCC may be offset by saved CO2 quotas or the implementation of a CO2
taxation scheme.
By calculating the average projected future CO2 quotas from 2025 to 2040 as detailed in
[40], a CO2 quota of 256 DKK/tCO2 is determined. It should be noted that ARC presently
only pays CO2 quotas for fossil CO2 emissions, which means that only the avoided fossil CO2
emissions of 178 519 tCO2/yr detailed in table 4.19 will be exempt from paying the CO2 quota.
Implementing this into the NPV calculation detailed in eq. (3.127), yields the considerably lower
LCCCs detailed in table 5.1.
==PAGE:143==
125
Table 5.1: Calculated LCCCs for the investigated scenarios including a future fossil CO2
emissions quota of 256 DKK/tCO2.
Scen. No

Thus upon comparison between table 4.18 and table 5.1 it may be seen that the implementation
of the saved fossil CO2 quota, the LCCC drops by approximately 197 DKK/tCO2 for all
scenarios. It should be noted that the results detailed in table 5.1 account only for the quota
exempt avoided fossil CO2 emissions and thus do not cover the potential for selling the net
negative emissions on a CO2 market akin to the Emission Trading System (ETS).
Ignoring the present day implementation of fossil emissions CO2 quotas, for a future scenario
where the motivation to implement CO2 abatement technologies such as CCS is driven by
a CO2 taxation scheme, it may be interesting to quantify the magnitude of such a tax that
makes the CCS facility at ARC a viable investment. For this CO2 tax, the determined LCCC
detailed in table 4.18 as well as the costs of geological sequestration including transportation to
the injection site must be covered by the taxation scheme. As such, the tax is calculated as the
sum of LCCC and the costs of transportation and sequestration. According to [43], Ineos have
estimated the price for ship transportation and well injection for the Greensand CO2 storage
project in the North Sea to be between 80-100 EUR/tCO2, which for a conversion factor of 7.44
DKK/EUR amounts to 595 to 744 DKK/tCO2. Using the average value, this means that the
overall CO2 emissions tax needs to be at least 1 041 DKK/tCO2 for Scenario 7 with the lowest
LCCC to make it economically viable to implement CCS at ARC. This number correlates well
with the number determined by the Danish Economic Councils who suggest a taxation level of
1 200 DKK/tCO2 for a scenario with large-scale deployment of BECCS [15].
A collective partnership named Carbon Capture Cluster Copenhagen, abbreviated as C4, was
formed in early 2021 with the overall goal to achieve economies of scale for CC and transporta-
tion, utilization and/or sequestration. The idea is that achieving economies of scale will reduce
the overall costs of achieving emissions reductions through CCUS. The partnership includes
ARC and eight other companies; ARGO, BIOFOS, Copenhagen Malmö Port, CTR, HOFOR,
Vestforbrænding, VEKS og Ørsted. The C4 partnership has a vision of achieving an emis-
sions reduction of 3 MtCO2 annually which according to [57] corresponds to 15 % of the target
emissions decrease for 2030 specified in the Danish climate legislation. Due to the relative prox-
imity of the participating partners in C4, the facilities are to be connected via a CO2 pipeline
transporting the captured CO2 to Prøvestenen Harbor in Copenhagen for easy liquefaction
and shipping to sequestration points at sea. While this report has dealt exclusively with CO2
compression and liquefaction for transportation at 15 bar and -30 °C, other alternatives exist
such as supercritical pipeline transportation directly to the sequestration point. Both onshore
and offshore CO2 pipelines have been found to significantly reduce the transportation costs,
especially for large CO2 amounts [58]. Thus there may potentially be potentials for further
decreasing the CO2 transportation costs from C4 by building common supercritical pipeline
infrastructure directly to CO2 sequestration sites.
It should be noted that there are some who question the usage of large-scale CCS to offset emis-
sions from other sectors, as it promotes and allows, in their belief, a business-as-usual mindset.
It may encourage large-scale emitters of CO2 to postpone or even cancel the development and
implementation of sustainable technologies, since their fossil emissions may be reduced with
CCS or offset by BECCS that delivers net negative emissions. As an example, a steel manufac-
==PAGE:144==
5.1 Future work
126
turing plant that currently combusts coke and thus emits significant amount of fossil CO2 to
the atmosphere may be encouraged to pursue CCS integration to minimize their CO2 emissions
while continuing the combustion of fossil coke. Thus rather than deploying long-term better
solutions such as using green hydrogen or electric arc furnace steel making technologies that
do not consume fossil resources or emit CO2, wide-spread cost-competitive CCS deployment
may make certain sectors reliant on fossil resources for longer than is potentially necessary. A
similar problem is encountered for WtE facilities where future circular economy schemes that
make heavy use of waste recycling may be prevented from implementation due to a continuing
requirement to deliver negative emissions through BECCS to offset other fossil-reliant sectors.
Thus while CCS is a promising and relevant solution that allows for a rapid decarbonization of
society to reach the targets of the Danish climate legislation, a large-scale deployment of CCS
in the short-term must not hamper the deployment of future technologies in the long-term.
5.1
Future work
Since most WtE facilities and in fact most power plants in the world do not have the option
to integrate the CC waste heat into a DH network to reduce the costs, it would be extremely
interesting and valuable to investigate the introduction of heat recovery technologies for CC
integration in power-only facilities. Since electricity is the only product from power-only fa-
cilities, it constitutes a much higher percentage of the total annual income than for a facility
with income from DH sales. Thus depending upon the exact nature of the facility and whether
or not it is a retrofit or a green-field CC integration project, there are a multitude of heat
recovery schemes that may be extremely interesting for implementation. Especially since, for
power-only facilities the alternative to recovering waste heat internally is simply to cool it
away. As an example, both the studied MVR system may prove to be very interesting in a
power-only setting, as well as high-temperature heat pumps to recover absorber waste heat to
the reboiler.
As specified earlier there is a benefit of utilizing supercritical CO2 pipeline transportation
solutions for large-scale CC. Thus it would be very interesting to investigate and compare
supercritial CO2 delivery from the ARC WtE plant with the compression and liquefaction
scheme investigated through this report.
A number of potential heat recovery technologies for internal heat recovery in the CC facility
would have been interesting to investigate. These include Lean Vapor Compression (LVC)
and desorber interheating. Desorber interheating may potentially be an interesting way to
integrate high-temperature heat pumps in CC facilities, where heat is supplied in the middle
of the stripper column at a lower temperature level than in the reboiler. This is possible due to
the temperature in the stripper column varying significantly with 119 °C at the bottom of the
stripper and 104.4 °C at the stripper outlet as detailed in table 3.3. Thus implementing a heat
pump system that recovers waste heat from the CC facility to the stripper column interheating
may be extremely interesting for electrification of CC systems, where due to the lower tempera-
tures in the middle of the stripper column, the heat energy may be recovered with a reasonable
COP. Electrification schemes for CC systems may in general be interesting to investigate since
many CO2 point sources such as cement production, oil refineries and steel production may
not have the same opportunities for steam sourcing as power generation facilities do. Thus
rather than building separate boilers that further contribute to the CO2 emissions to produce
==PAGE:145==
5.1 Future work
127
steam for the CO2 desorption process, the heat may potentially be effectively supplied with
heat pump systems.
Due to the trends shown in fig. 4.12 where the COP of the investigated MVR system increases
as the steam generation temperature decreases, it would be very interesting to investigate the
potential for delivering the generated steam directly to the stripper column and thus circum-
venting the reboiler entirely. Supplying the heat this way would remove the required approach
temperature in the reboiler meaning that steam could be delivered at a much lower temper-
ature. Since the overall viability of the MVR system is heavily dependent upon the realized
COP of the system, any potential increases may be interesting to pursue.
Additionally, it would be very interesting to investigate the use of ejector systems for waste
heat upgrading either from the lean amine flow or utilizing the heat in the stripper overhead
condenser with high pressure steam. As previously mentioned, not all facilities will have the
same opportunities for turbine extraction steam as ARC, and thus may be constrained to use
live steam. Thus rather than throttling the live steam to a temperature and pressure level
suitable for the reboiler, fully utilizing the potential of the high-pressure steam to upgrade
waste heat sources may present an interesting option.
Finally, It would be extremely interesting to gain an additional set of data derived from an
Aspen Plus model with a lower absorber inlet flue gas temperature, to investigate whether the
penalty imposed by the extra heat pump work required is higher or lower than the decrease in
reboiler duty stemming from the lower temperatures in the absorber.
==PAGE:146==
Chapter 6
Conclusion
This thesis investigated the integration of an amine-based CO2 capture system at the ARC WtE
facility in Copenhagen, Denmark. From the results of a chemical process model, seven scenarios
with increasing degrees of heat recovery from the CO2 capture facility were investigated and
compared. These scenarios apply different heat recovery strategies including external waste
heat recovery to district heating return water and internal heat recovery with mechanical
vapor recompression (MVR) systems.
Depending on the turbine design and the potential for sourcing low-quality extraction steam,
implementation of an MVR system may yield a lower LCCC. If the MVR system results in
savings in high-quality steam e.g. live steam, then implementation of the three-stage MVR
system described in this paper yields a higher gross turbine power and thus a lower LCCC.
However, if the steam savings are low-quality turbine extraction steam, then the MVR system
power consumption becomes higher than the gross power increase, yielding a lower net power
and thus a higher LCCC than using extraction steam only. Thus it may be concluded that
the viability of integrating the studied MVR system for retrofit CC systems will depend on
the possibilities afforded by the turbine design of the given facility. For green-field facilities
with CC integration, the findings of this report indicate that a turbine designed for a large
extraction point for sourcing steam for the CO2 desorption is preferable to MVR integration.
The project hypothesis detailed in chapter 1 is supported by the findings of this thesis. The
calculated LCCCs for scenarios with full heat recovery are proved to be much lower than
scenarios with only limited or no heat recovery implementation. The estimated total Total
Capital Investment (TCI) and Operations and Maintenance Costs (OMC) were found to be
of lower importance than the expected yearly relative income for the magnitude of LCCC.
Scenario 7 has by far the lowest LCCC at 371 DKK/tCO2. Scenario 7 is a full heat integration
scenario where all waste heat is recovered to district heating return water, either through direct
heat exchange or with heat pump systems. It has been shown that the LCCC in Scenario 7 with
full heat recovery is on the order of 63 % lower than Scenario 2 with no waste heat recovery
from the CO2 capture facility. The full heat recovery scenarios 7 and 8 operate the CO2 capture
facility at a net-positive energy consumption meaning that the overall sum of energy deliveries
(heat and power production) is larger than the reference plant without carbon capture.
Finally, an estimation of the potential emissions abatement performance through CCS inte-
gration at ARC has been performed. Based on the results of this it may be concluded that
implementing the designed CO2 capture system at ARC may potentially contribute with 3.3 %
of the CO2 emissions reduction required from 2019 to 2030 to reach the target set by the Danish
climate legislation.

Appendix B
CO2 Utilization Technologies
Carbon Capture and Utilization (CCU) differs from CCS in that rather than sequestering the
CO2 in geological formations or depleted hydrocarbon fields, CCU involves the utilization of
captured CO2 as eg. input for Power-to-X (PtX) technologies to produce climate neutral fuels,
as a feedstock for chemical processes or as a working medium for thermodynamic cycles [59].
In the report from March 2020, the DCCC concludes that even though the captured CO2 is
typically re-emitted with CCU for fuel production, the climate impact of CCU is the same as
with CCS where the capruted CO2 is sequestered safely underground directly. The reasoning
being, that the utilization of captured carbon for fuel production reduces the usage of fossil
fuels for the given application, and thus a fossil emission is saved. The report finds however,
that the emissions reduction is less costly with CCS than with CCU. [15]
The following will include a brief overview of potential utilization pathways for captured CO2.
B.1
Power-to-X
Captured CO2 can be utilized as feedstock in the production of synthetic liquid fuels such as
methanol or dimethyl-ether (DME). Green hydrogen, produces through electrolysis of water
may as an example be mixed with the captured CO2 and compressed before being inserted in
catalytic reactors to produce methane, which may be further refined into other hydrocarbon
products such as methanol and DME [59]. The global estimated market potential for CO2
utilization in fuels varies widely from 1 GtCO2 to 4.2 GtCO2 annually by 2050, where the
high-end estimate represents a future scenario where synthetic fuels have a sizable market
share due to cost reductions and policy drivers [60].
B.2
Utilization in Thermodynamic Cycles
Another application of captured CO2 is its use as a working fluid in thermodynamic cycles.
As an example, CO2 has been used for years as a refrigerant in heat pump and refrigeration
systems. Additonally, several research institutes are investigating the use of supercritial CO2
Brayton cycles for power generation. [59]
==PAGE:186==
B.2 Utilization in Thermodynamic Cycles
168
B.2.0.1
Use in Chemical Products
Finally, captured CO2 may be eﬀiciently transformed into a variety of chemical products. Cur-
rently, by far the largest chemical utilization of CO2 is the production of urea, that utilizes
140 MtCO2 annually to produce 200 Mt of urea. Urea is produced from ammonia and CO2
with the ammonia typically produced through the energy intensive Haber-Bosch process. Cap-
tured CO2 may also be utilized in the production of polymers, for which the global utilization
potential in 2050 has been estimated between 10 to 50 MtCO2 annually. [60]
==PAGE:187==
Appendix C
Alternative CO2 Capture
Technologies
C.1
Pre-Combustion Capture
In pre-combustion carbon capture technology, the fuel (typically coal or natural gas) is pre-
treated before combustion. For coal-derived fuels, the pretreatment consists of a gasification
process conducted in a low oxygen environment in a gasifier that produces a syngas consisting
primarily of carbon monoxide (CO) and hydrogen (H2) as described in eq. (C.1). The syn-
gas then undergoes a water gas shift reaction with steam to form additional H2 while CO is
converted to CO2 as detailed in eq. (C.2). [22]
Coal (s)
gasification
=⇒
CO (g) + H2 (g)
(C.1)
CO (g) + H2O (g)
water−gas shift
=⇒
H2 (g) + CO2 (g)
(C.2)
For natural gas fuels containing mostly methane (CH4), instead of gasification the fuel under-
goes a steam reforming process to separate CH4 into H2 and CO, as described in eq. (C.3).
Following the reforming, the resulting syngas may undergo a water gas shift reaction, as detailed
in eq. (C.2) to increase the H2 concentration of the gas. [22]
CH4 (g) + H2O (g) reform
=⇒CO (g) + 3 H2 (g)
(C.3)
Following the gasification or reforming and water gas shift reaction, a gaseous mixture with
a relatively high concentration of CO2 above 20 % is ready for the separation process. Pre-
combustion utilizes the same separation techniques as post-combustion capture, the possibilities
for which will be described in section 2.3.1.2. [22]
C.2
Oxy-fuel Combustion
In oxy-fuel combustion, rather than combusting the fuel with air, pure oxygen is used. This
reduces significantly the amount of nitrogen (N2) present in the flue gases from the combustion,
thus easing the separation process of CO2. Additionally, using pure oxygen for the combustion
substantially reduces the amount of thermal NOx present in the flue gases. The major con-
stituents in the flue gasses from oxy-fuel combustion are CO2, water vapor, particulate matter
==PAGE:188==
C.2 Oxy-fuel Combustion
170
and SO2. Particulate matter and SO2 may be removed with conventional flue gas treatment
systems, such as electrostatic precipitators and desulphurization systems. The water vapor
may additionally in large part be removed by means of traditional flue gas condensation tech-
niques. The resulting flue gas with the aforementioned flue gas cleaning methods has a very
high concentration of CO2 between 80-98 % depending on the fuel used, and may thus be
directly compressed and prepared for storage or transportation. While oxy-fuel is theoretically
possible, it requires large amounts of oxygen, which may be sourced from energy intensive air
separation units, leading to a significant energy penalty for the facility. [22]
For future energy scenarios with prevalent PtX implementation, oxy-fuel combustion may
present an interesting solution to make use of waste oxygen produced by electrolyzer plants,
that in turn could potentially utilize the captured CO2 in the production of green fuels.
==PAGE:189==
Appendix D
Modelling of Single and Multi-stage
Compression Systems
With centrifugal compressor designs it is possible to reach pressure ratios for a single compressor
stage of around 10 [37]. Thus for the given boundary conditions with a compressor inlet pressure
of 1.8 bar and the requirement for an outlet pressure of 15 bar, a compression ratio of 8.33,
the pressure lift may be facilitated with a single compressor stage. Recalling that the work of
compression is defined as detailed in eq. (D.1).
Wcomp =
∫poutlet
pinlet
p dν
ν = 1
ρ
(D.1)
With density generally being inversely proportional to the temperature of a gas, a considerable
reduction in the compression work may be realized with the implementation of multiple com-
pression stages with intercooling. For a compression train with a given number of compression
stages of equal compression ratio, the pressure ratio for predefined inlet and outlet pressures
may be calculated with eq. (D.2).
Rp =
(poutlet
pinlet
)1/n
(D.2)
Where, n, is the number of stages in the compression train.
The state point designations follow the numbers detailed in fig. 3.12. With the compressor
inlet conditions in state point 1 given in table 3.9 the enthalpy and entropy of the inlet flow
may be determined with the built-in thermodynamic library in EES [32].
h1 = f(T1, p1)
s1 = f(T1, p1)
(D.3)
In state point 2 at the compressor outlet may be determined with the given compression ratio
of the compressor stage.
p2 = Rp · P1
(D.4)
With the pressure at the compressor outlet determined, the entropy and enthalpy under isen-
tropic compression may be determined.
s2s = s1
h2s = f(s2s, p2)
(D.5)
By applying the assumed isentropic eﬀiciency and heat loss factor detailed in table 3.9, the
enthalpy at the compressor outlet under adiabatic compression, h2w, and the actual enthalpy

Thus, with the pressure and enthalpy in state point 2 given, the temperature and entropy at

T2 = f(h2, p2)
s2 = f(h2, p2)
(D.8)
The compression work exerted on the gas may then be calculated accounting for the heat loss
in the compressor.
˙Wcomp = ˙mCO2 · (h2w −h1)
(D.9)
After the post-compression cooler (for single-stage systems) or compressor intercooler (for
multi-stage systems), the CO2 is cooled with an external water/glycol coolant loop to dissapate
the compression heat energy. In the design condition, the ambient temperature is assumed to
be 25 °C, and a 5 °C approach temperature is assumed, both for the coolant to ambient air
cooler and the coolant to CO2 intercooler. Thus, the compressed CO2 is cooled to 35 °C after
each compresser stage at the design condition.
P3 = P2
T3 = Tcoolant + ∆Tpp
(D.10)
With the pressure and temperature at the outlet of post-compression cooler or intercooler
given, the enthalpy and entropy may be evaluated with the built-in thermodynamic library in
EES [32].
h3 = f(T3, P3)
s3 = f(T3, P3)
(D.11)
For multi-stage systems, the outlet stage of the intercooler determines the inlet stage for subse-
quent compression stages, and the procedure detailed in eq. (D.3) to eq. (D.11) may be repeated
for the given amount of compression stages.
A number of compression stages ranging from a single-stage compression system to multi-
stage compression trains with up to five stages are investigated to evaluate the effect on the
total compressor power consumption and the cooling requirement needed to facilitate the post-
compression cooling and/or intercooling. The total power consumption and cooling requirement
will be calculated with the following expression.
˙Wtotal = ˙Wcomp,1 + ... + ˙Wcomp,n
(D.12)
˙Qtotal = ˙QHEX,1 + ... + ˙QHEX,n
(D.13)
D.1
Results of Numerical Modelling Compressor Train in EES
Results of the compression train investigations may be seen in fig. D.1. In fig. D.1a and fig. D.1b
it may be seen that while the total compressor power and intercooling requirement may be seen
to decrease with an increasing number of stages. However with both curves flattening out as the
number of stages goes towards five, each additional compression stages thus offers diminishing
improvements, a three stage compression train with intercooling is used for the reference carbon
capture scenario.
